# Not Worth the Interruption

# Chapter 1

Breakfast still arrived on silver platters. That was part of what made the year feel deranged.

The porridge steamed, the toast racks made their little domestic journeys up and down the Hufflepuff table, and somewhere above the smell of bacon fat and damp wool there floated the ordinary beeswax polish of the Hall itself, as if the castle meant to keep being a school by force of habit. Amycus Carrow stood in the middle aisle with a mug in one fist and a second-year by the back of the neck, and the house-elves still sent out kidneys and tea.

Owen sat three-quarters of the way down the table, where people could borrow the marmalade from him without needing to look at him for long. It was a useful position. He had been in it often enough to know which plates would come back with egg yolk drying on them and which people would pretend they were not still hungry. He shifted the butter dish toward a fourth-year before she had to ask. He moved his own cup out of the line of an elbow. He had become, without ever quite deciding to, competent at surviving rooms that wanted something larger out of everyone in them.

His bag sat between his shoes. The old copy of *A History of Magic* inside it dragged the leather down against his ankle like a brick wrapped in cloth.

Martha Sorrell dropped onto the bench opposite him hard enough to rattle spoons. Her dark plait had half come apart already, and she had one cuff rolled because the other still carried a brown stain from whatever she had been lifting before breakfast. She always ate the burnt side of the toast first, as if disappointment ought to be dealt with early and without ceremony. She tore a blackened edge off now, chewed it, and pointed the rest at him.

"You're coming tonight."

Owen kept his eyes on the marmalade jar. "To what."

"Don't do that."

"I'm asking."

"Neville needs people after supper. Not heroes. Just people with legs and a sense of direction." She reached across, took the knife out of his hand because he was not using it quickly enough, and buttered her toast in three brutal strokes. "We're moving two first-years out of the west stair dorms before the Carrows decide they've had enough sleep for one week."

Owen looked up then, not at her but past her, toward the high table. Snape sat in the middle under the black banners, one white hand laid next to his goblet, not eating. He had the particular stillness of a man who seemed to regard movement as an avoidable error in other people. Alecto was saying something to him. Amycus was still in the aisle with the boy.

"I've got detention," Owen said.

"Detention ends."

"Sometimes it doesn't, actually."

Martha snorted once through her nose. "Fine. Then after. We need runners."

"Martha."

"What."

"If they're watching the west stair dorms, they'll be watching the corridors off them. More bodies don't always improve a corridor."

She stared at him for a beat, then looked down at his bag. "You've brought Binns to breakfast."

He should not have. He had meant to return the book before anyone noticed he still had it. Yesterday he had taken it from the shelf in Binns's classroom because the newer editions were all missing the older index tables, and because Binns himself had drifted through the end of the lesson half a yard above the floor and had not noticed a live boy borrowing anything. It was a classroom copy from 1878, stout-backed and shedding at the hinges, with the title barely readable under a skin of old finger grease. Too old to be useful for the O.W.L. syllabus, really. Too old to be worth the trouble of carrying. He had brought it down intending to leave it on the return trolley outside History of Magic.

"I'm taking it back," he said.

"You said that yesterday."

"I was delayed."

"By what."

He considered, briefly, telling the truth, which was that he had sat on his bed and read a chapter on twelfth-century wand tariffs because it was easier than going where Neville had told half the school to go. "Homework."

Martha laughed without any amusement in it. "That isn't even a lie with proper shoes on."

Amycus jerked the boy in the aisle around to face the room. He was a small Ravenclaw, maybe twelve, with one lace loose and his fringe wet where tea or sweat had got into it. Amycus's fingers nearly disappeared around the back of the child's neck.

"Look at him," Amycus said. His voice carried easily. He had the sort of coarse cheer that made every sentence sound like it might end in a slap. "Go on. It's breakfast, not a funeral. Look."

Nobody looked quickly enough for him, so he shoved the boy's face down into the nearest bowl of porridge. The bowl jumped, tipped, and rolled in a white arc across the table runner. There was a wet choking sound. A few people flinched. Owen did not. Flinching where Amycus could see was just participation from the other side.

Amycus hauled the boy upright again. Porridge clung to his eyebrow and one ear. "Mr Penrose thinks muttering counts as courage. Mr Penrose thinks if he says filthy little things under his breath in my lesson, perhaps I won't hear them."

The boy's mouth worked. Oatmeal and spit hung in a thread from his lower lip. He was trying not to cough.

"Tell them," Amycus said.

"I didn't say anything," the boy whispered.

Amycus smiled. He had a chipped front tooth he worried with his tongue when he was pleased. "Wrong answer."

He slapped the side of the boy's head, not with enough force to floor him but enough to ring the sound through the Hall. The porridge on the boy's ear flecked onto the table and somebody's sleeve. Amycus turned him by the shoulder and marched him toward the staff dais with one hand still sunk into his robes as if he were carrying a sack that leaked.

There was a long moment after, a long soft scrape of cutlery starting again all over the room because the alternatives were worse.

Martha put her toast down. "This is what I mean."

Owen kept his voice low. "I know what you mean."

"Do you."

"Yes."

"Because from where I'm sitting it looks a lot like you mean to be very sensible till the year is over and then discover there wasn't one."

He should have said something better. Something human-sized, at least. What came out instead was, "If I get caught in a corridor with two first-years and Neville, that doesn't improve anything."

"It improves it for the first-years."

"Until it doesn't."

She closed her eyes briefly. When she opened them again the anger had gone flatter, which was worse. "You always do this. You answer the bit of the sentence that lets you get away."

Owen looked down at the table. Someone had left a ring of tea around the base of the milk jug. His own thumb moved toward it with the reflexive urge to wipe it clean.

"Tonight," Martha said, but there was less demand in it now and more disgust, as if she already knew what his answer was going to be and was testing whether he knew as well. "If you change your mind, seventh-floor tapestry. After supper. If you don't, then at least stop carrying that corpse of a textbook about like it can excuse you."

Amycus came back down the aisle still drinking from his mug. He paused near the Hufflepuff table long enough for the smell of stale drink under his breath to reach them. His little eyes moved over the plates, the faces, the hands. Owen slid one hand off the table and hooked it through the strap of his bag.

"Phipps," Amycus said.

Every part of Owen that could tighten did so quietly.

"Yes, Professor."

"Library again tonight. Pince says you stack straight and don't whine. Don't make a liar of the woman. She has one of those faces already."

A few people laughed because not laughing would have been visible. Amycus gave the table one satisfied glance, then moved on.

Martha watched him go. "There. Your exciting life with books."

"I didn't ask for detention."

"No. You ask for the sort of life that makes detention feel like a personality." She stood, gathered her satchel, and then, because she was still Martha and not only what the year kept making her into, pushed the butter dish back within his reach before she left. "Eat something."

He said, "I'm eating."

She pointed at his untouched eggs. "That isn't the same thing."

Then she was gone into the standing scrape of benches and robes.

Owen waited until the table had started to break apart around him. He pulled the old History of Magic volume half out of his bag and looked at the cracked board, the faded classroom stamp, the brittle threads showing at the spine. Returning it would have been the tidy thing. Returning it would have been what a boy did when he meant to keep the rest of his life containable.

He slid it instead into the deep inner pocket of his robe, where the weight of it sat against his ribs like concealed contraband, and finished his tea before it went cold.

* * *

Library detention would have been less hateful if the library had been allowed to remain itself.

It had not. The north stacks still smelled of the blast from four nights ago: hot stone, singed paste, the sour dust that came out of old bindings when they had been struck hard enough to remember they were made of hide and glue. One entire bay leaned two inches out of true. A cracked atlas case had been roped off with red cord that trembled whenever anyone walked past. The high windows on that side of the room showed a black September evening pressing itself flat against the glass.

Mrs Pince patrolled the damaged aisles like an insult given legs. Her bun had been pinned so tight that nothing about it admitted weather, and every few minutes she bit down on a clove pastille and made the sound of a drawer shutting. The blast had cost her three front ranks of shelving, seven catalogue slips, a ladder, and, in her own estimate, civilization.

"Not there, Mr Phipps," she snapped as he reached for a folio with a split board. "That is Balkan herbals. You are holding punitive edicts. Unless the Ministry has merged botany with stupidity in the last hour."

He shifted the armful to the shelf she wanted.

"Sorry."

"You are. Put them flush."

He put them flush.

Amycus had done the damage himself, according to the whisper that had gone round Hufflepuff, though nobody agreed on why. The version Owen liked best held that he had attempted to blast a hidden packet of seditious leaflets and instead pulverized a cabinet of seventeenth-century border charters because he had mistaken calfskin for treason. The version most likely to be true was simpler and stupider: he had been angry, and the books had been there.

Pince had set Owen to the repair edge of the mess because he was, as Amycus had advertised, good at stacking straight and not whining. He worked with his sleeves rolled to the forearms, sorting scorched bindings from warped ones, brushing plaster grit out of hinges, carrying anything truly ruined to a trolley that looked too small for the amount of damage it was being asked to dignify. The labor would have been almost peaceful if it had not taken place inside occupation and under a woman who regarded breathing as a form of theft when done too noisily near manuscripts.

The old Binns copy pressed against his side each time he bent.

He waited until Pince had gone three aisles over to hiss at a sixth-year from Slytherin about index cards. Then he set down his stack, crouched in the shadow of the leaning atlas case, and slipped the book out under cover of the shelf.

It was worse in this light. The brown cloth had gone silky from handling generations ago and then rough again where damp had once got in. Professor Binns's classroom stamp sat on the inside board above a date that made Owen blink and look twice: 1878, as he had remembered, though the print line below carried the sort of fussy publisher's address modern school texts had long since abandoned. Several pages had been mended with old translucent strips that had yellowed to the colour of weak broth. It smelled faintly of dust, lamp smoke, and the cold cupboards of dead classrooms.

He had been using it for the index tables, nothing more. The older editions still admitted that magic had once possessed local histories and administrative embarrassments, which the modern school version preferred to flatten into proper nouns and dates. Binns lectured from any edition as if all of them were equally dead, but Owen had found, not for the first time, that the margins of the older books contained the things teachers no longer wanted to look after.

He turned to the section he had left folded with a bit of string: late medieval consolidations, educational charters, the slow swallowing of dangerous private collections into institutional libraries. Boring on purpose. Safe. Perfect detention reading.

One of the footnote marks near the bottom of the page had bled slightly into the paper as if the type had once been set too hard. He followed it down.

At first he thought he was misreading the line because of the light and the dust in his eyes. He bent closer.

*One trunk of covenant-restricted leaves remained at Hogwarts library, North Stacks C-13, sealed by founders' agreement.*

He read it again.

Then a third time, slower, with his thumb braced in the gutter.

North Stacks C-13.

The present library did not have anything called North Stacks C-13. It had History Aisle Four, Restricted Alcove Two, Charms Overspill, Oversize Reference, and a maddening modern system of floating brass labels that drifted half an inch in front of the shelves like well-brought-up ghosts. Pince referred to areas by subject or by who was forbidden from them. Nobody in school spoke in lettered north-stack shelf marks unless they were quoting something older than their grandparents.

Which, Owen thought, he currently was.

"Mr Phipps."

He shut the book at once and nearly trapped his own finger in it.

Pince stood at the end of the aisle with two cracked periodicals in her hand. "If you have found a way to restore collapsed shelving by staring at a school text, you may publish the method after you finish the actual work."

"Sorry."

"You keep saying that as if repetition improves the article."

He rose, slid the Binns copy inside his robe again, and took the periodicals from her.

Pince's mouth pinched. "And stay out of the Restricted side. I am not spending my evening explaining to Professor Carrow why one of my detentions has lost track of his own hierarchy."

"I wasn't going there."

"No?" She glanced once at the pocket where the outline of the book still showed through the cloth. "Then it is fortunate for us both that I did not ask why you are reading before curfew instead of after it."

She moved on. Owen stood with the cracked journals in his hands until the blood returned properly to his fingers.

The footnote kept reopening in his head. Not because it promised treasure. He was fifteen, frightened often, and not stupid enough to assume that anything hidden in Hogwarts under the present administration would improve his life on contact. It was the concreteness of it that caught him. Not some vague founder legend. Not a mystical whisper about lost knowledge. A shelf mark. A direction. The dead flat tone of institutional storage.

North Stacks C-13.

The blast damage was in the north stacks.

He put the journals away wrong on the first attempt and had to pull them out again before Pince saw.

After that the detention changed shape around him. The work did not get harder, exactly, but it ceased to fill the available space inside his head. He noticed the angles of the damaged shelving, the patches where old stone showed through behind ripped backing boards, the way one bay had been shoved sideways far enough to expose a strip of wall that had not seen light in years. Once, carrying a stack of warped indexes, he caught sight of a cut mark behind the leaning case, something like a carved numeral under the drift of plaster dust. Pince barked his name before he could angle in close enough to be sure.

At half past eight the clock near the entrance gave its discreet silver cough. Pince dismissed the Slytherin sixth-year with a threat about adhesive paste. She kept Owen another quarter-hour because his trolley, she said, had been arranged by a person with a moral allergy to order.

By the time she finally snapped, "Go, then, before your House invents a need for you," the corridors beyond the library had already begun to empty into curfew.

Owen said thank you, because one could say thank you to Mrs Pince without danger and to very few other authorities in the school, shouldered his bag, and walked out at an even pace.

At the end of the main aisle he stopped.

The sensible thing was obvious. Curfew, cellar, bed. If Martha had meant what she said, Neville's people would already be shifting first-years or risking themselves or both. Owen could still choose to go. He knew the tapestry she had named. He knew, with painful exactness, how disappointed she would look when he did not appear.

He stood under the library arch with the old book heavy against his ribs and listened to the quiet of the north side of the room.

Then he turned back.

* * *

The damaged section lay beyond the rope and two warning placards, one of them new enough that the ink still smelled fresh. Owen ducked under both. His shoes made almost no sound on the carpet runner there; Pince preferred the soft hush because it let her hear page-turning from offensive distances. Tonight it let him hear other things too: the small continual settling of a wounded room, a splinter shifting somewhere high above, the faint chink of chain from the Restricted cases further in.

He kept his wand low and lit only enough of it to move.

Up close, the blast had been clumsier than it had looked from the safe aisles. A shelf support had cracked clean through and then held anyway, which gave the whole bay a crooked stubbornness Owen recognized from some of the boys in Hufflepuff when they had decided not to cry in public. Plaster had blown off the wall in pale scabs. The backboard behind one history run had torn loose at the nails and curled outward.

He set down his bag and leaned in.

There it was: not floating brass, not modern ink, but a number cut straight into the stone pier behind the shelves.

C-11.

The carving had been done deep, meant to outlast labels and librarians alike. Dust sat in the grooves. Owen rubbed it with the side of his thumb and came away white.

He moved sideways. The next pier showed C-12, half hidden behind a drooping row of dislodged shelf pegs. Beyond that the leaning atlas case blocked the wall entirely.

North Stacks C-13.

His mouth had gone dry. He glanced back toward the library doors. Nothing. Only darkness and the far green glimmer of banked lamps at the desk.

He squeezed into the wedge between the atlas case and the wall. The space smelled of cracked leather, stone chill, and the old mouse-dust scent of untouched places. The case had shifted on the blast and jammed itself skewed against the pier. He had to turn one shoulder, then the other, and suck in enough stomach to slide another six inches. Dust dragged at his robe. Something with gilt edges dug into his hip.

At the far side, where the case had pulled away from the next section of wall, the plaster had gone completely. Bare stone showed under a drift of powdered mortar and splintered board.

He lifted the wand a little.

C-13.

Not merely a mark. A seam ran down beside it, hair-thin but deliberate, the sort of line no modern library wall should have had. The shelf in front of it had been built later, fixed across older work. Even in the poor light he could see the difference in material: the external facing smoother, school-century carpentry nailed over older blocks cut closer and heavier. Modern stacks sitting on top of a previous arrangement. A hidden run, or the bricked mouth of one.

The footnote had not been decorative. It had been an address.

Somewhere out in the corridor a bell rang once for the late edge of curfew. Owen flinched then, unable not to. He killed the wandlight and stood in the dark gap listening, breath held high in his throat. No footsteps. No Pince. No Amycus lurching in to make a moral lesson out of him.

When he lit the wand again he crouched lower and touched the seam beside the carving. The stone was colder there than the exposed pier. A thread of air moved through it and over his knuckles, stale and mineral and faintly metallic, as if the wall behind it enclosed something that had not been asked to share itself in a very long time.

He should have gone back at once. He knew that. Not because it was forbidden, though it obviously was, and not because Martha was right, though she probably was about several things at once. He should have gone because this was exactly the sort of opening a person like him was meant to leave alone. Older boys with nerves or talent or a martyr's appetite found hidden places in wartime schools. They brought back contraband, gossip, injuries, a story worth telling somebody else in a whisper. Owen liked indexes. Owen liked predictable shelves. Owen had spent the whole term meaning, as hard as he could, not to become noticeable to anyone who mattered.

But the line in the book had existed. The carved marks had existed. And now the wall itself had answered with architecture.

He pulled the Binns copy from inside his robe, braced it on his knee, and checked the footnote one more time in the wandlight. The paper shook slightly, whether from the draught or his own hand he could not tell.

North Stacks C-13, sealed by founders' agreement.

Sealed, then. Not gone.

Some part of him, a part he usually kept fed on timetable and caution, made one last competent effort. He could tell Pince in the morning that the damaged wall exposed obsolete shelf marks. He could mention it to a prefect. He could even, absurdly, mention it to Martha and let the secret become communal at once, which would almost certainly save him from doing anything stupid alone.

Instead he memorized the spacing of the stones, the height of the seam, the way the atlas case had jammed and how much room there was behind it for a boy his size and not much larger. He put the book away. He picked up his bag.

On the walk back to Hufflepuff he did not think about Neville's tapestry or the first-years or even Amycus, though Amycus's laugh carried once from a distant stair and made the hair rise briefly on Owen's arms. He thought about service routes, old shelf plans, whether the library had ever kept retired maps, whether house-elves knew the underside of the stacks, whether a seal by founders' agreement would be made to stop charms or only hands.

By the time the barrel entrance admitted him and the smell of warm earth and supper scraps closed around him, he was no longer simply getting through the year.

He was trying to work out how to get back to North Stacks C-13 without being seen.


# Chapter 2

North Stacks C-13 did not exist in any arrangement the library still admitted to using.

Owen proved that to himself three different ways before supper and twice after it, because there was always the chance that he had made one small stupid transcription error and built the whole thing out of that. He checked the current shelf index by hand until Madam Pince told him to stop breathing on it. He whispered the location charm fourth-years were taught for lost essays and watched the spell shiver uselessly over every respectable part of the north wall except the blasted section, where it went dull and dropped like wet thread. He measured the exposed stone numerals again, fingers in the cracks left by Amycus's last fit of disciplinary enthusiasm, and found the same thing he had found the night before: the modern shelving stood over an older system whose numbers had been cut straight into the stone, and C-13 belonged to that older system, not this one.

By nine o'clock the library had that late, watchful quiet it got under occupation. It wasn't peaceful. It was empty in the wrong way. A room could keep hundreds of books and still feel as if it had been searched for valuables. The hanging lamps were turned low. Dust sat in the broken joints of the north stacks where blasting had shifted the cases half an inch off true. Owen was supposed to be checking spines for damage on the nearest trolley. He had checked six. The rest sat untouched while he crouched in the gap between two leaning cases with the old Binns copy open on his knee and a second book balanced against the shelf.

The second book had been the useful one.

It was a prefect guide from 1881, bound in cracked school-green cloth and written in a tone that assumed rule-breaking was a regrettable but permanent branch of natural history. Owen had found it shelved among obsolete discipline records because nobody at Hogwarts now needed instructions for how to patrol after curfew without upsetting the house-elves, and nobody in any century had wanted to read it for pleasure. The useful part was not the advice about confiscating dungbombs or the outraged paragraph on courting couples behind armour. It was the foldout plan in the back, a cross-section of the castle's service levels and blind passages, annotated for prefect rounds in a tidy dead hand.

The modern library ended at the north wall.

The 1881 plan carried on past it.

Behind the wall, above a drop marked kitchen runs, an older line of storage spaces had been drawn in pale green ink: retired shelf-chases, too narrow for ordinary traffic, lettered by chase and numbered by span. C-13 sat there, behind what had once been the Restricted Section and what was now a bank of respectable history shelves with a blast crack through them.

Owen looked from the plan to the exposed numeral carved low in the stone. C-11, half hidden under a shifted case. Two spans along, if the old measurements still held. Another crack. Another offset. Then C-13, presumably on the other side of a wall no catalogue charm could see through because it was no longer part of the library as the library understood itself.

His hand hovered over the foldout's margin note.

"Access by service aperture only. Not for broad-bodied patrol use. Report disturbances to kitchen steward."

He read it again, because the line was the difference between a hidden shelf and a hidden shelf he might actually reach.

The route was not for broad-bodied patrol use.

The phrase would have been insulting if it had not been so helpful.

He was still looking at it when shoes clicked two aisles over. Owen shut the guide at once, not loudly, slid it under the trolley cloth, and reached for the nearest damaged book. Madam Pince came into view between the shelves with a candle balanced so steadily in her left hand that it looked nailed there. Her mouth had the pinched professional bitterness of a woman forced to babysit a war inside a room that had once obeyed rules.

"If you are shelving by smell, Mr. Phipps," she said, "you will be here all night."

"Sorry," he said. "This one had torn boards."

"Then you may put it on the torn-boards trolley instead of staring at it as if remorse will mend it."

"Yes, Madam Pince."

She moved on. The keys at her waist made a dry little rainfall against her skirt. Owen waited until the sound had passed into the west end of the room before he let out the breath he had been flattening in his ribs.

Curfew was in twelve minutes.

The guide gave him a route in theory. Theory was not enough. Half the castle's routes had been blocked since the year began, and the other half were usable only if you liked explaining yourself to people with authority. He needed to know where the service aperture actually opened now and whether it still opened at all.

There was only one person he could ask who might know the answer and might not immediately hand him over for being out after hours.

Owen finished the six books he was supposed to have shelved, stacked the rest exactly as he had found them, and signed out with Pince a minute before the bell. Then he took the long turn down the Hufflepuff-side stair, cut through a laundry passage that smelled of damp wool, and slipped into the kitchen corridor just as the castle settled into its curfew noises: doors thudding, distant shouts, the drag of benches somewhere overhead. The stone changed temperature down there. Library chill gave way to banked heat and old steam caught in the walls.

The kitchens were still working. They were always still working. Trays were being scrubbed. Cauldrons were cooling. Someone had dropped a spoon and not yet found it, which mattered immensely if you judged by the muttering.

Tallow stood on an upturned crate by the scullery sink, polishing a dent out of a copper pan with furious concentration. He was small even for a house-elf, all singed ears and knotted wrists, with soot worked so deep into the creases of his knuckles it looked permanent. He had a tea towel thrown over one shoulder and three bent soup spoons lined on the sill in front of him like patients waiting to be told how bad it was. Every few strokes he stopped polishing long enough to check whether the pan's loose handle had worsened.

He saw Owen without appearing surprised.

"Young sir is late for not being late," he said to the pan. "Tallow is busy with a thing that used to be round."

"Sorry. I just needed to ask one thing."

"One thing grows elbows. Then it wants another thing and another thing and the gravy skins over while everyone talks."

Owen came closer anyway, lowering his voice because even here the castle felt as if it had ears now. "Do you know where the old kitchen runs meet the north stacks?"

Tallow kept polishing.

"Lots of places meet lots of places. That's castle work."

"The retired shelf-chases," Owen said. "The ones above the kitchen side. There was an old patrol map and I think C-13 comes off the library north wall, only the aperture isn't marked properly and the catalogue doesn't see it and I can't tell if it's sealed or just forgotten."

That got Tallow to stop.

He looked up with the expression of someone who had hoped a schoolboy was asking for bread and discovered instead that the schoolboy had opinions about masonry. One of the bent spoons slid from the sill and clinked into the sink. Tallow did not take his eyes off Owen.

"Young sir has been reading in shelves that don't want company."

"I've been reading a guide."

"Mm."

Owen waited. Tallow set the pan down, picked up the fallen spoon, bent its neck back by hand with a soft hateful click, and only then spoke again.

"C-chase runs behind the old north books, yes. It drops from the library wall to the hot side through an ash throat nobody uses because it was built for smaller times. The opening is behind the flour bins, second panel after the cracked blue tureen. Don't lean on the right jamb. It gives."

"It still opens?"

"It still pretends to be stone. That isn't the same as being shut."

Owen felt his pulse move, not faster exactly but harder, as if something inside him had put its shoulder to a door.

"Could an adult fit?"

Tallow gave him a look which, on a human face, would have been almost rude.

"A grown wizard?" he said. "In robes? With the sort of dignity they're always carrying? No. A very thin one might get his arm in far enough to injure himself and blame the house. A prefect boy your size, if he takes off his jumper and has the sense to exhale before the second bend, can manage. That's what the passage is for now. Not that it's for you. It's for nobody."

"Right," Owen said. "I only meant generally."

"Young sir means specifically and badly."

Owen started to deny it, stopped, and tried a narrower truth. "If something was hidden there, and nobody could get at it physically, wouldn't a Summoning Charm bring it out?"

Tallow's mouth tightened. He began setting the bent spoons in a straighter line.

"If the box is what the old north stewards called a hush box, no. Lead in the walls, lead in the seams, old library spite all round it. It sits off the list. It doesn't answer pulls. The catalogues slide round it like grease round cold porridge. That was the point."

"A hush box," Owen repeated.

"A nasty little thing for books people meant to have and not have. Too hidden for lending, too important for burning. There were more once. Then there were fewer. Then everybody forgot why fewer did not mean none."

The cracked blue tureen sat on a high shelf by the flour bins, its hairline fracture stitched with old silver. Owen had passed it a hundred times without thinking about the wall behind it.

"You know what is in C-13?"

Tallow snorted softly and took up the copper pan again.

"If Tallow knew what all the old hidey-holes kept, Tallow would spend his life disappointing clever children. Tallow knows routes. Tallow knows which stones feel wrong. Tallow knows the north throat gives him a pain behind the eyes and a taste like pennies. That's enough knowledge for work."

He scrubbed hard at the dent. The pan squealed under the cloth.

"Don't take a lamp," he added. "Air goes mean in there. Use wandlight if you must, but keep it low or the shine will leak at the books. And if the wall starts humming, come out. I mean come out, young sir. Not in five minutes. Not after a look."

"Does it do that often?"

"Often enough for manners."

There was a pause in which the kitchens sounded suddenly immense: water thrown into a sink, someone chopping stale crusts for tomorrow's pudding, the distant rush of enchanted taps shutting themselves off. Tallow inspected the pan handle again, grimaced, and muttered something to it that Owen did not catch.

"Why tell me at all?" Owen asked.

Tallow did not look up.

"Because if you go blundering after curfew without the left-stone, you will put your foot through the ash throat and then I will be the one scraping you out before breakfast. Because boys who think in maps are worse than boys who think in fists. Because the house keeps enough mess. Pick one."

It was not permission. It was enough.

Owen nodded. "Thank you."

"Young sir can thank Tallow by putting the tureen back exactly where it sits if he starts touching things. That crack bites flour."

Owen almost smiled. He did not trust smiling just now; it felt like something that could make noise.

"I will."

Tallow had already gone back to the pan. "Mm. And wash the blood off the stone if there is blood. The grout stains."

That made Owen stop in the doorway.

"Why would there be blood?"

Tallow turned the pan to the light, judging the dent with offended attention.

"Because old work was not made for being found by boys after bedtime," he said. "Off with you."

Owen went.

He did not go straight back to the cellar. He waited in the passage beyond the kitchens until the last of the service noise had lowered and the curfew hush settled properly over the lower corridors. Then he counted two patrol cycles by the castle clock and came back in stocking feet, shoes slung over one shoulder, jumper knotted round his waist. He moved the cracked blue tureen first because Tallow had been irritatingly clear about that. Behind it the stone panel looked no different from the rest of the wall until he pressed the left edge and felt a hidden give.

The right jamb shifted under his testing hand exactly as Tallow had said. Rotten, but in stone rather than wood. Owen put his weight elsewhere and pulled.

The panel opened inward on a slit of old heat and dust.

The aperture was shoulder-high and immediately narrower than he had hoped. Not impossibly narrow. Just honest. He put his shoes aside, flattened himself, and slid in feet first until his hips jammed. For one blank second he had the stupid, complete conviction that this was it, that he would be found halfway into a wall after curfew with his trousers full of mortar dust and no explanation in English or any other language. Then he remembered to let the air out of himself. His ribs narrowed. The stone let him through another few inches. He twisted, found the left side with his hand, and dropped onto a sloping run of brick that shed cold ash against his palms.

The panel settled behind him with a soft closing cough.

Darkness took the passage whole.

Owen got his wand out carefully. "Lumos."

The light came up at the tip in a pinched blue bead, meaner than usual, but steady. The passage pitched down at once, no more than three feet across and less in places, old brick on one side and the dressed stone back of the library wall on the other. It smelled of soot, dead heat, and the dry metallic tang of places shut up for years. Ahead, the crawlspace bent sharply left. The floor was a mixture of ash, cinders, and grit fine enough to get into his sleeves and between his teeth.

He went on hands and knees after the first yard because there was no dignity to be had from pretending otherwise. The walls closed and opened unpredictably. Sometimes his shoulder slid through with room to spare; sometimes a jut of stone found the soft part under his arm and made him suck air through his nose until the pain passed. Above him, at irregular intervals, he could hear the library in fragments: a muffled footstep through planks, the creak of a settling case, once the tiny hard ring of Pince's keys. The whole structure seemed to be listening sideways.

C-11 had been visible from the library. He counted spans as he went, palming the old cut numerals where they emerged from soot and brick: C-12 on a recessed block, then a cracked support where part of the chase had slumped, then another low shelf of stone that forced him flat enough to feel his shirt drag on every grain.

At the second bend the passage tightened brutally.

He stopped, cheek against cold brick, and tried to send calm into parts of himself that had no interest in being calm. There was no room to turn back from here, not properly. He would have to inch backward blind if he failed to get through, shoulders burning, heels catching. He pictured that too clearly. Tallow's warning about manners and humming and blood came back in a stupid useless rush.

He took another breath, let it out, and reached forward.

His hand found empty space.

He dragged himself through the constriction and came into a pocket just large enough to crouch in. He sat back on his heels, blinking sweat out of his eyes. The wandlight lifted a narrow chamber built between the back of the library shelving and the older exterior wall: shelves long removed, iron brackets left in place, a drift of shredded cloth and mouse droppings in one corner. The stone here had a different feel from the crawlspace, less domestic, more deliberately secret. Someone had meant this gap to remain a usable volume long after the library forgot it.

C-13 was cut into the wall at knee height.

Nothing obvious sat beneath it.

For a few dreadful seconds Owen thought he had done all this for an empty niche and a dead numbering system. Then he saw the line in the stone above the numeral: a rectangular seam so fine he only caught it because the blue wandlight flattened differently on metal than on rock.

It was lead.

It was not painted or plated. It was a whole inset box driven into the wall and disguised with stone dust until even at arm's length it nearly vanished.

His heart gave a nasty, eager kick.

He tried the smallest thing first, because trying the biggest thing first was how people ended up with eyebrows missing. "Accio box."

Nothing happened.

Not even the courtesy twitch of resistance.

He swallowed and altered the angle. "Accio contents of C-13."

Still nothing happened.

The silence after the spell had a thick, almost personal quality, as if the box had listened and chosen not to answer.

Owen edged closer and laid two fingers against the seam. The metal was cold enough to sting. His locating charm in the library had not failed because he had cast it badly. The guide had not been misfiled by chance and the citation had not survived because the catalogue had overlooked it. The thing had been hidden from every ordinary library habit on purpose. There was lead in the walls, lead in the seams, no Summoning range, and no catalogue hold.

That was why it had stayed lost.

Any grown wizard with authority would have tried the same clean spells he had just tried, found nothing, and concluded there was nothing there. Adults did not generally take their jumpers off and crawl through ash for a footnote. Owen was not sure whether that made him cleverer or merely less well brought up.

The seam ran round three sides. On the fourth, low down, there was a pressure catch disguised as a bit of damaged tooling. He got his nails into it and failed. He tried the tip of his wand and felt the catch give half a line before the wood jerked in his hand like something alive. The bead of light at the end spat sideways into the wall. Blue sparks cracked over the stone and went out with a smell like singed wool.

Owen stared at the wand.

Nothing about that had been a normal recoil. It had felt less like a miscast than like the space itself had slapped his wrist.

"Right," he whispered. "Fine."

He set the wand between his teeth for light, braced his left hand against the wall, and used his right thumb to work the catch again. This time it depressed. A second catch, higher. Then a third, hidden under the lip. The box door shuddered but did not swing free. He had to put his palm flat to the face of it and shove.

The brand took him so fast he did not at first understand it as pain.

Heat struck through the centre of his right hand, flat and white and total, as if the lead had become a stove plate. He made a noise into the wand, half choke and half swear, yanked away, and knocked his head off the wall hard enough to fill his eyes with sparks. The wand dropped. Blue light spun across the cramped chamber, flashed once against the ceiling, and landed in the ash.

For one impossible instant the mark on his palm shone brighter than the wandlight.

Then the pain arrived properly.

It went up his arm in a grinding wave. Owen folded over it, forehead to his knees because there was nowhere else to go, sucking air through his teeth and trying not to vomit in the crawlspace. The skin across his palm had gone bone-pale where the heat had hit, not blistered yet, just raised in a shape he could not make sense of through the tears in his eyes: a hooked line, two branching cuts, something almost like a diagram burned into flesh.

"Fuck," he said, softly and sincerely.

The box door had sprung open.

Inside lay a stack of thin folios wrapped in oilskin so old it had gone stiff as bark. The covering had split where the latch released, and under it the manuscript showed itself in edges and corners: pale leaves, narrow and long, with green-black stitching through one margin and a title strip darkened by age.

The strip read Grinlow Leaves.

He knew the title before he had fully read it. The letters had that brute founder-era confidence old school objects sometimes kept, as if they had never expected a later century to exist long enough to mishandle them.

His palm throbbed with each beat of blood. He picked the wand up with his left hand, relit it, and reached back into the box more carefully, using the cuff of his shirt between his right hand and the folios. Even through the cloth, the packet made the small hairs on his arm rise. It was neither warmth nor cold. It was a pressure, like standing too near a dropped note that hadn't stopped ringing.

The first leaves were partly blank.

They were not empty. They had been worked over. Ink had once been there and had gone down into the page the way old pond water goes dark under weed. When he tilted the folios, faint geometry moved under the surface: ruled circles, cross-hatched margins, strokes too deliberate to be decoration. Then a page halfway through resolved suddenly into legible writing, not because the light changed but because his burned palm, hanging useless and hot at his side, seemed to pull the page into answer.

Owen sat very still.

The script was older than any school text he had handled and yet disturbingly practical in tone, like notes for competent people who had no patience for being impressed. He could not read every word. Some of the language slanted into archaic forms, and some of the notation was not any runic hand he knew. But the argument was plain enough to punch through the gaps.

This was not wandwork.

It was not incantation.

It was not a spell to be cast at an object.

It was a condition to be amended before any spell, any threshold, or any named category could behave.

The page set out examples in parallel columns: open, closed, held, permitted, witnessed. It was not teaching the reader how to throw force at the world. It was teaching the reader how to alter the rule under which force counted. Every spell Owen had ever learned assumed the world came pre-agreed, its permissions already fixed. These leaves were about working beneath the ordinary surface of that agreement, at whatever layer made Hogwarts magic possible in the first place.

He turned another page with the back of his left knuckle.

The next page went blank again, then offered a diagram of nested circles and a later English note squeezed into the margin: amendments proceed from ground conditions, not visible effects.

The next legible leaf was stranger still.

It carried no instructions he could understand, only a table and a sphere drawn with four marked points around its middle, each point linked to a line that went outward beyond the page edge. Around the sphere ran the familiar grammar of warding, but at a scale no school ward ought to need. The heading used a term he had to sound out twice before it meant anything at all: Meridian Commonwealth. Beneath it, in a thinner hand, a later note cross-referenced departure houses, continuation masks, terrestrial concealment.

The word terrestrial sat there in plain ink.

He looked at the drawn sphere again.

It had to mean Earth, then, or the magical world represented as Earth whether the diagram meant the whole planet or only a part of it. Four marked sites ringed the sphere, and lines led away from each of them. The wards were being held in place not around a room or a vault or even a school, but around something planetary and left active after some organised departure. It did not suggest extinction or ruins abandoned in panic. It suggested a leaving organized enough to keep the wards running afterward.

His scalp prickled under the dust.

Another page, half legible, showed a lattice over coastlines he recognised only because Hogwarts taught geography badly enough that Britain always looked lonelier than it should. Another page stayed stubbornly blank no matter how he angled it. Then another answered in a burst, text rising dark from the fibres while he watched, and he nearly dropped the folio at the sight of it.

The manuscript was not passively revealing itself to any reader with time.

It was answering a condition in his hand.

The burn seared again, as if to underline the point.

Above him, somewhere beyond a wall and several centuries of changing floorplans, a case shifted with a soft wooden groan. Owen froze. The library, the patrols, and the curfew all came back at once, insultingly local after what lay in his lap and still absolutely capable of ruining him.

He wrapped the folios back in the split oilskin as carefully as he could one-handed and looked into the lead niche again. There was nothing else inside except a bed of grey felt and, under that, the imprint where the packet had sat untouched for so long the fabric had learned its edges. Returning it would be the sensible thing. Returning it would also mean trusting a wall to keep behaving now that the box had burned him open.

He was not putting it back.

He slid the Grinlow Leaves under his shirt, flat against his stomach. The packet was colder than the air and somehow heavier than its size allowed. He shut the lead door with the heel of his left hand. The catches did not re-seat cleanly. One remained sprung, leaving the tiniest black line at the seam.

It would stay forced open, then.

He would have to live with that.

Getting back through the crawlspace with one functional hand was worse than getting in. He had to lead with his left arm and drag the rest of himself after it while trying not to crush the manuscript against the brick or scrape the burn on the wall. Twice his right palm brushed stone and the pain turned his vision grainy. Once the wand flared without warning, a sharp blue spit that lit the passage all the way to the far bend and made him lie still, breath stopped, until he was sure nobody in the library had seen.

By the time he pushed the kitchen panel open again his hair was wet at the roots and his shirt stuck to him with soot. The corridor beyond was empty. Warmth from the banked ovens pressed against his face. A pot somewhere in the dark gave a sleepy settling tick.

He set the cracked blue tureen back exactly where it had stood.

Then he leaned one shoulder against the wall and finally looked properly at his hand in the wandlight.

The burn had darkened from white to angry red. In the middle of it the branded shape stood out sharper now, a small angular figure with one branching stem and a hooked cross-line, neither letter nor rune he knew. The skin around it had already started to swell.

He touched his wand to the edge of the mark, meaning only to test whether a cooling charm might be worth the risk.

The wand jerked.

A thread of blue fire spat sideways, singed a line across the flagstone, and vanished under the flour bin with a smell like hot dust.

Owen snatched the wand back and stared at the scorched mark on the floor. Ordinary magic had never done that in his hand before. A misfire after pain, perhaps. Except it had not felt like pain. It had felt like interference. As though the burn had shifted some invisible tolerance and the wand no longer sat in his grip at the angle the world expected.

From the scullery came a soft clink. Tallow, somewhere beyond the doorway, still working.

"Did you bleed?" the elf called without raising his voice.

Owen looked at the flagstone, the singed flour dust, the sweat on his own wrist.

"No."

There was a pause.

"Mm," said Tallow, in a tone that meant he did not believe him but could inventory the lie later.

Owen rinsed the worst of the ash from his left hand at the kitchen tap, not daring to put the right under water yet, and slipped back into his shoes. The manuscript pressed flat and impossible under his shirt. He could feel each folio edge when he breathed. Outside the kitchen corridor the castle kept up its occupied-school business: patrol routes, shut doors, boys whispering in dormitories, girls pretending not to listen for steps in the passage, one whole frightened year trying to get to morning by routine.

He climbed toward the cellar with a founder-era manuscript tucked against his skin and the shape of a burn changing on his palm.

At the turn below the common room he stopped under a lamp and pulled the top folio half free again, just enough to make sure he had not imagined the sphere, the table, the buried instructional tone. The page that had been blank in the chase was still mostly blank now, but not entirely. Along the inner margin, as if surfacing through fog, a line of script had come dark enough to read.

It was not about a charm.

It was about permission.

He slid the leaf back in at once, hand shaking not with fear exactly but with the effort of fitting the thing he knew into the thing he had just found. Magic at Hogwarts had always arrived as craft and vocabulary and rule-bound effect. It depended on speaking correctly, moving correctly, and meaning it. These pages suggested that all of that sat on a deeper arrangement somebody could alter if they knew where to press.

And if the pages with the sphere meant what he thought they meant, then somebody had once used that deeper arrangement not to open doors or mend cups or curse enemies, but to hide the whole world.

The common-room barrels were a few yards ahead, warm and absurdly ordinary.

Owen tucked the manuscript back under his shirt, closed his burned hand around nothing, and went in without any idea how he was supposed to sleep with the universe suddenly reduced to a condition someone had written down.


# Chapter 3

The Room of Requirement had stopped pretending to be mysterious. By late September it had settled on the look of a school storeroom after a small war: camp beds shoved too close together, trunks underfoot, laundry strung between bookcases, steam from a kettle going nowhere because the windows it had invented were only a kindness and not a real route for air. It smelled of damp wool, onion soup, old socks, and the sharp medicinal green of dittany. Somebody had bled on one of the cushions and flipped it over. The blood had come through anyway.

Owen stood just inside the door with the Grinlow Leaves wrapped under his jumper and had the immediate sense that he had brought the wrong object into a room that needed five dozen other things first.

Neville Longbottom was at the centre of it, not elevated, not making a speech, just standing with a scrap of parchment in one hand and saying names in the flat voice of somebody who had already had to repeat himself too many times that day.

"If you're sleeping here tonight, you don't leave after lights-out unless somebody sends you," he said. "I don't care if you hear your own dormitory door. I don't care if you think it's your own dormitory door. Ginny, can you take the first-years on the left side? Dean, I still need two lookouts for the fourth-floor turn. No, not both of you, because if both of you get grabbed we're worse off than before."

A younger Hufflepuff on the nearest bed had her school tie tied around her wrist above a curse welt that shone wet and red. Seamus was trying to pry a splinter of broken frame out of his thumb with his teeth. At the back wall a tall Ravenclaw girl Owen knew only by sight was sorting salvaged library books into two stacks, dry and ruined, and muttering a separate apology to each ruined one before she set it down. Her wire-rimmed spectacles kept sliding to the end of her nose. She pushed them back with the back of her wrist because both hands were full of warped paper.

Martha saw him first.

"There you are," she said, low and exasperated, as if he had wandered off during washing-up rather than vanished into his own head for the better part of two days. Her dark plait was loosening down one shoulder and there was a smear of something grey on her cheek that might have been dust or soot. "If you've come to stand in the doorway, shift. You're making everyone squeeze round you."

He moved because three people immediately needed the space he had been occupying. The room accepted them and not him. His right palm pulsed under the handkerchief he had wound round it. The burn had quieted enough during the walk that he had nearly convinced himself it was ordinary. Here, in the crush and heat, it had started needling again, as if the manuscript disliked crowds.

"Sorry," he said.

Martha was tying a bandage round a third-year's forearm one-handed while she talked. "Take that kettle over if you're useful. If you're not useful, sit somewhere narrower."

He took the kettle. It was hot through the handle and slopped weak tea over his knuckles. Nobody thanked him, which was fair. The tea was not for him. It was for a first-year Gryffindor whose mouth still shook after every swallow. Owen carried it across and nearly trod on a pair of shoes drying by the fire, stuffed with newspaper. One of the soles had been patched with cardboard cut from a Potions essay.

By the time he got back to the edge of the room Neville was crouched over a rough floor plan of the fourth floor scratched in charcoal on the top of an old wardrobe laid flat.

"They've started checking the suits of armour again," Neville said. "Not all of them. The two by the tapestry of Barnabas are still blind if you pass close. Amycus knows about the broom cupboard now, so stop using the broom cupboard. If anyone is still hiding notes in the flour bins, stop doing that as well. Tallow nearly had my head off."

There was a tired ripple of laughter at that, real enough to count as relief.

Martha came up beside Owen with a basin that smelled of pennywort and burnt cloth. "What happened to your hand?"

He folded the burned one in against the manuscript under his jumper before she'd properly seen it. "Nothing. I caught it on a hinge."

She looked at him for a moment that was too long for the lie and too short for a fight. "Right."

He should have told her then. Or if not her, Neville, because Neville at least would know what to do with new danger if it had a shape and a corridor attached to it. That was why he had come. The manuscript had spent the afternoon on his bed like a thing that had chosen him badly. When he touched the resolved pages the diagrams had settled into legibility for a few seconds at a time, enough for him to understand that he did not understand them, and underneath that, worse, enough to understand that the claim was not a metaphor. Magic was not the machinery itself. It was only what happened at the surface. There was something lower down, old and structural and written into the world like load-bearing grammar, and the manuscript treated Hogwarts as one local problem inside a much larger one.

He had tried, alone, to think what a sensible person did with that.

The sensible answer had appeared to be: tell other people immediately.

The flaw in that answer was the other people.

"Owen," Martha said, because Neville had paused to hear from a fifth-year Slytherin about patrol changes and Owen was still standing there like a badly placed chair. "Did you need something?"

Half the room did not look at him. The half that did looked in the way people looked when any small interruption threatened to become a larger one.

"I found something in the library," he said.

Neville looked up, waiting. He had a bruise yellowing along one cheekbone and the particular worn expression of somebody who had stopped expecting good news but still had to leave a space for it in case it arrived by accident.

"Is it a route?" Neville asked.

"Not exactly."

"Is it about patrols?"

"No."

Martha shut her eyes briefly. "Just say it, then."

Owen pulled the manuscript free enough to show its wrapping, then more because stopping halfway looked furtive and going on looked mad, and there was not, apparently, a useful degree between those two conditions. The vellum leaves slid against one another with a dry, insect-thin noise. Several of the pages facing outward were blank. One held a smear of diagram in rust-coloured ink that looked less like writing than a map of cracked riverbeds.

The younger students nearest him leaned away first and then, seeing it was only paper, leaned back.

"This was in a hidden shelf behind the north stacks," Owen said. "In a lead coffer. The footnote was in an old classroom copy of *A History of Magic*. Pre-1883, I think. I checked the shelf numerals after the blasting and they still match the old arrangement, or enough of it does, and the note said `North Stacks C-13, sealed by founders' agreement,' which shouldn't have still been there at all, and it wasn't just a book hidden there, it's something older than that, and I think it means the castle has-"

"Slow down," Neville said.

Owen stopped, swallowed, and discovered he had already lost the order of what mattered.

"It means," he said, smaller, "there's a kind of magic under the usual sort."

There was a silence, but it was not the silence of interest. It was the silence of a room deciding whether the interruption had been stupidity or panic.

Seamus stared at the nearly blank page. "Under the usual sort."

"Not bigger," Owen said quickly. "Lower down. Structural. Conditions, not spells. I know how that sounds. I know it sounds-"

"Like you've not slept," said someone near the back.

That got a few grim smiles, and because they were grim they hurt more.

Neville straightened. "All right. Start again. What does it do?"

"I don't know yet."

"What can we use it for tonight?"

"Not tonight exactly. I mean, perhaps eventually, if it's what it says it is, because there are diagrams about the castle and maybe not just the castle. There are references to warding on a scale that doesn't make sense if they're only talking about one building. More like the country, or-" He heard himself and tried to cut the sentence smaller before it finished humiliating him. "Or larger than the country."

Martha stared at him. "Owen."

"I'm not making it up."

"Nobody said you were," Neville said, and he was trying, Owen could see that, trying not to let the room turn cruel just because he did not have time for this. "I'm asking what, exactly, you want from us."

It was a simple question. Owen should have had an answer ready before he'd opened the manuscript in public.

"Help," he said, and hated the word the moment it left his mouth because it sounded childish and because it was true. "I need another look at it. Or someone who knows old references. Or just-" He shifted the leaves and a resolved scrap of text ghosted up the margin and then slid away before he could force it to stay. "This cites things that don't fit the editions we have. It says the old magical civilisation didn't die out, it left, and that what we've got now is-" He looked round the room at cots, bruises, steam, a first-year trying not to cry into weak tea. "Not the whole of it."

Nobody laughed. That would almost have been kinder.

Dean said, "Brilliant. So while Amycus is skinning us alive we've also got a secret civilisation to worry about."

"It's not that," Owen said. "It's not another war, I don't think. It's more that there are protections, or were, and they're-"

"Failing?" Seamus said. "Excellent. Lovely. Put it on the list."

That did make people laugh, short and exhausted. Neville did not.

"Enough," he said, and the room obeyed him because it trusted him more than it liked the joke. He looked back at Owen. "Do you have a hidden room the Carrows don't know? A route we can use? A way to stop them dragging people out of bed? If the answer to any of that is yes, say yes and we'll sort the rest later."

Owen's palm burned harder. Sweat slicked the handkerchief. The manuscript seemed suddenly indecent, like he had brought in an old saint's fingerbone and wanted applause because it glowed.

"I don't know if it's that direct," he said. "I haven't had time."

"We've all had time," Martha said, too sharp now to be only tired. She set her basin down on the floor with a wet slap. "We've had exactly the same amount."

He felt heat climb his face. "That isn't what I meant."

"Then what did you mean?"

He could have lied. He could have said the room needed warning because the castle sat on something older and stranger than anyone knew. He could have said the manuscript might matter later. Both statements were true, and both would have sounded like a person trying to make his terror nobler than other people's terror.

Instead he said the truest and worst version.

"I think this is bigger than the Carrows."

The room changed around him. Not dramatically. No intake of breath, no shouted outrage. It was smaller than that, which was why it was impossible to answer. Shoulders shifted. Somebody at the fire gave a disbelieving little snort through the nose. A first-year actually looked afraid, not of the manuscript, but of him, of the possibility that one more older student had finally cracked and started speaking in useless grand statements while the rest of them still needed supper and lookout rotations.

Martha's voice dropped. "Don't say that in here."

"I don't mean they're harmless, I mean scale. I mean if the castle itself is part of-"

Neville cut across him for the first time. "No."

It was not loud. It did not need to be.

"Listen to me," Neville said. "If you've found something real, then good. Hide it properly and keep yourself alive long enough to work out what it is. But I can't pull people off moving children and covering corridors because you've found a mostly blank book that says the world is bigger than we thought. The world is already bigger than we thought. That's why half this room is sleeping in here."

Nobody moved. Neville went on, plain and tired and absolutely right.

"If you want a bed, have one. If you want someone to check whether you're hexed, we'll do that. If you want me to tell everyone here that their actual problem tonight is less important than your theory, I won't. I don't have that luxury, and neither do they."

Owen opened his mouth. Nothing came out that was fit to say.

Martha rubbed at the grey smear on her cheek with the heel of her hand and only spread it wider. "Have you even told me where you found it?"

"North stacks. Behind them. Through the old service run by the kitchens."

"Can we use the run?"

"Not for this. I mean, yes, physically, probably, but that's not-"

"Then say the useful bit first next time."

The words landed harder because she had not raised her voice. She sounded fed up, embarrassed for him, embarrassed for herself for having expected better.

At the back, the Ravenclaw girl with the ruined books had stopped sorting. She was looking at Owen now with her head slightly to one side, not in pity, not in agreement either. More like she had heard a shelf creak behind a wall and was trying to decide whether the building plan allowed it.

Dean let out a breath through his teeth. "Can we get back to not dying till breakfast?"

"Yes," Neville said at once, without taking his eyes off Owen. "We can."

That was the dismissal. It was kinder than Owen deserved and somehow worse for that reason. If Neville had told him to shut up, Owen could have disliked him for half a minute and gone away cleaner. Instead Neville simply turned his body a fraction, enough to include everybody else and exclude Owen, and asked Ginny whether the first-years on the left side had eaten yet.

The room resumed around him. Not smoothly. A few glances still snagged on the manuscript and then slid off in a hurry, the way people looked away from vomiting. Martha went to the third-year's bandage. Seamus found his splinter again. Someone by the fire said they had one heel of bread left and another voice said cut it thinner, then. The ordinary stupidity of surviving reassembled itself and left Owen standing outside it while still technically in the middle.

He wrapped the leaves up with fingers that had gone clumsy.

His burned palm had dampened the handkerchief through. When the cloth shifted, the skin beneath flashed up angry and ridged, not a simple blister now but the dark outline of a branching mark he had no name for. He covered it again at once. If anyone had seen, nobody said.

He did not know where to look. Anywhere in the room was wrong. At the beds made him feel theatrical. At Neville made him feel younger than eleven. At Martha made him feel false. He ended up looking at the floorboards, where somebody had spilled soup earlier and half wiped it, leaving a pale onion crescent in the grain.

For one shabby second he considered saying sorry to the whole room.

That would have made it about him again, which was apparently a skill he had discovered a fresh aptitude for.

He waited until nobody was directly watching and edged backwards toward the door. The Room, perverse as ever, had put a narrow table in his way since he'd come in. On it sat three labelled jars of salve, a stack of folded socks, and a chipped china dog with its head broken off. He had not seen the dog before. He had the absurd impression that the room itself had added it simply to improve the quality of his humiliation.

"Phipps."

He stopped with his hand on the latch.

It was not Neville. It was the Ravenclaw girl from the back wall. Up close she was older than he'd thought from a distance and more tired around the eyes. One cuff of her jumper was ink-black where she had clearly been using it as a handkerchief for weeks. She had tucked two bent quills behind one ear as if she'd forgotten they were there. In one hand she still carried a ruined book, held carefully away from her body so the warped pages could dry.

"Sorry," she said, though she did not sound sorry. "What printing was the *History of Magic*?"

Owen stared at her.

The room noise carried on behind them. Neville was assigning another corridor pair. Somebody swore softly because a bandage had stuck.

"What?" Owen said.

"The edition." She pushed her spectacles up with one knuckle. "The footnote you quoted. `North Stacks C-13, sealed by founders' agreement.` Which printing had that? Because the classroom text drops the founder shelf marks before the 1883 clean issue, and after the 1894 recatalogue nobody writes `C-13` for anything in the north stacks. So either you've had a breakdown in a very specific bibliography, or somebody cut that note out of every copy that ordinary people can still find."

For a moment Owen only looked at her. He had spent two days with a discovery so large it made the school year feel like a bad local arrangement of furniture. It had not occurred to him until then that the first person who might believe him would do it because the citation style was wrong.

"The title page was half gone," he said. His voice came back rough. "But it was old. Binns's classroom shelf. Not library stock."

The girl's gaze sharpened, not with excitement exactly, but with the grim concentration of someone seeing a filing error big enough to imply deliberate damage.

"Right," she said. "I thought so."

"Prue," Martha called from across the room, distracted and irritated. "If you're done courting mildew, I need the dry bandages."

The girl did not look away from Owen. "In a minute."

Then, quieter, to him: "Don't wave that about in here again."

It was not disbelief. It was worse and better than that.

It was advice.


# Chapter 4

He spent most of Tuesday proving that the pages would not do it for his left hand.

They behaved themselves for the left hand. They stayed as they had been since the shelf-chase: mottled vellum, old watermarks, diagrams that began cleanly and then gave up halfway across the leaf as if the scribe had been dragged from the table by the collar. When he turned them with his unburned fingers, they were only damaged and maddening and older than anyone had a right to hide in a school.

With the right hand they stopped pretending.

Owen had chosen the little Hufflepuff study alcove off the cellar corridor because the pipes knocked there and the sound covered page-turning. It smelled of coal dust, wet wool, and the onion tart someone had sneaked down from supper. The round window near the ceiling let in a disc of late afternoon light the colour of old brass. He had his ash-linen notebook open, three nibs laid out in a row, and the Grinlow Leaves propped on a folded towel so the damp from the stone table would not creep up into them.

He rested the heel of his burned palm on the lower margin of a page that had shown him nothing useful all week.

The mark in his skin heated at once. Not the ordinary smart of a healing burn, not the deep tug it gave him when he gripped a door handle too hard, but a hot, exact line as if someone on the other side of the page were tracing his palm with a blunt needle. The vellum darkened under his hand. Then pale notation rose through it in slow threads, not ink exactly but something like bruising surfacing under thin skin.

He took his hand away. The page went blank.

He put it back. The buried writing came up again, line after line, as patient as frost.

For a moment he only stared. What he felt first was not triumph. It was a sour, almost childish resentment. He had taken the thing to Neville, endured a roomful of people looking at him as if he were trying to dodge real courage by inventing larger, cleverer problems, and all the while the manuscript had been keeping its mouth shut because he had been touching it wrong.

Prue Hollis's voice came back to him whether he wanted it or not. Which edition had that footnote been in, then?

He pressed harder, because annoyance was as good a reason as any to keep moving. More text appeared in a cramped side hand around the diagram already faintly visible on the page: a dead stem laid across a ruled frame, three bracketed marks at either end, and a note in glossed runes he could only half parse. His reading of runes had never been special. He was decent at schoolwork because he hated getting things fractionally wrong in public, which was not the same as being gifted. But the margin notes were giving him more than the runes alone had. The burn did not merely reveal them. It seemed to supply a sort of pressure, a suggestion of how the marks leaned.

He copied as fast as he could before they sank back.

`least exercise for opened admission`, he wrote, then crossed out `opened` and tried `admissive opening`, then swore under his breath because neither phrase sounded like anything. Another gloss gave him `spent runner only`. Another, more useful, gave `fixed frame required`. A third resolved after several tries into `ask the frame, not the fibre`.

That one made him sit back.

The ordinary logic of school magic was target, intention, execution. You aimed at the thing you wanted altered. Even when the spell behaved badly, it behaved badly at the thing. The manuscript was not describing aim. It was describing terms.

He put his palm down again until the heat sharpened and the next line lifted out. Half of it remained obstinately absent, the lower right of the page blank no matter how long he held on. What he had was enough to be dangerous and not enough to be safe:

`For the least amendment use dead vegetal matter in a fixed frame. Let admission be asked by bound marks. Do not--`

Nothing after that. The line simply ended in clean vellum.

He tried another page. Blank. Another. A table of symbols rose, then a tiny sketch of hinges beside a note too rubbed to carry. Hinges again. He flipped back. The first exercise looked almost insulting in its smallness: dead vine, fixed frame, bound marks. Nothing about blood. Nothing about founder stones or planetary wards or the mad larger claim that magic itself was a kind of fencing round the world. If the book meant to seduce him into larger lunacy, it was doing so by way of something that looked very like a classroom practical gone peculiar.

He should have waited.

That was plain enough even as the thought came. The lower note was missing. The page answered only while his hand sat on it like a stamp. He did not know whether the revealed text was complete or whether the burn was forcing partial legibility out of something that expected another kind of access entirely. He should have shown nobody, of course; Neville had solved that question for him. But he should at least have waited another day, copied more, compared pages, found out whether the second diagram matched the first.

Instead he looked at `fixed frame required` and thought of the abandoned greenhouse beyond the working row, the one with the warped ventilators and the potting benches nobody used because the heating charm there ran mean and sour and all the glass wanted replacing. It had old stone footings. It had dead vines in plenty. It had doors and latches he considered, very briefly and very stupidly, part of the virtue of the place. If anything went wrong, what was there to break that did not already look halfway broken?

He shut the manuscript and sat with his hand over the cover until the heat in the burn eased down from pain to warning.

Then he opened his notebook to a clean page and began copying out the exercise properly, in the narrow exact script he used when he wanted a thing to behave.

***

By the time he slipped through the greenhouse door, dusk had already flattened the grounds into blue glass and soot.

The abandoned house sat at the far end of the Herbology row with two panes painted over from inside where something had once spat acid at them. The path was slick from an earlier shower. Owen's shoes left dark chevrons on the flags. He had his satchel under his robe, the Grinlow Leaves wrapped in a pillowcase inside it because he did not trust canvas against rain or himself against panic, and a pocket full of bits he had nicked or borrowed without asking: chalk, two lengths of copper wire, a plant label, a jar lid, and the stump of a candle.

He did not need the candle. The greenhouse held heat like a bad temper.

The moment he shut the door behind him he stepped into damp air thick with old compost and the sweet rot of leaves left too long in trays. The glass overhead had gone cloudy in patches, so the last of the light came through blurred and greenish. One of the side benches had collapsed months ago and been left where it fell. Earthenware pots nested under it like dull skulls. At the back, a run of dead vine still clung to a trellis nailed to the brick pier between two ventilation cranks, all of it crisped brown and silver with dust.

Hot, but neglected. Private, but not tidy. He found this reassuring.

He put his satchel on the least filthy section of bench and took out the notebook first, then the wrapped manuscript. Even alone he felt furtive handling it. The pages gave a faint drag against one another, heavier than paper ought to be. He opened to the copied exercise rather than the original text because he was not enough of an idiot to prop the manuscript in direct potting-shed damp if he could help it.

The procedure, as far as he had it, did not look theatrical. It looked irritating. He had to bind the marks to a fixed frame. He had to use dead vegetal matter, not living tissue and not loose litter. He had to make admission the asked quality rather than the named object. That last phrase still felt wrong in his head. Asked quality. It sounded like something Binns would mumble just before the class died of boredom.

He chose a vine about as thick as his little finger and snapped it free of the trellis. It broke with a dry tick. Under the dust it still held a faint twisted pattern where it had once tried to spiral round wire. He laid it on the flagstone sill under the trellis because the manuscript's drawing had shown a stem crossed by frame lines, and a sill at least had the virtue of being stone and fixed and already part of the greenhouse rather than something portable he might accidentally enchant into the lake.

He drew the first mark with chalk. A bracket, then a second, then three short lines crossing the space between. At either end he wound the copper lightly round the stem and pinned it under the chalk marks with bent bits of label wire. Bound marks. Fixed frame. He even used the jar lid to hold a spoonful of water because one note seemed to suggest the dead thing ought to be reminded what admission had once felt like.

That done, he stopped and looked at the arrangement with the kind of dissatisfaction he usually reserved for nearly-right Arithmancy diagrams. It looked flimsy. It looked like nonsense. It looked, if he were honest, like the sort of thing a desperate person would set up because he could not bear another day of being told to wait.

He almost packed it away.

Then he remembered Neville saying, very evenly, that if Owen had time for manuscripts he had time to carry messages.

That settled it in the worst possible way.

He opened the Grinlow Leaves to the exercise page and laid his right hand against the margin until the hidden gloss surfaced. The heat lanced through his palm hard enough that he hissed. He kept reading.

Ask the frame, not the fibre.
