Owen opened his trunk, slid *Defensive Theory for Beginners* out from under two clean shirts, and shook the folded notes loose.

The copied figures were no longer alone on the page.

Across the lower half, where there had been only blank parchment at supper, a new under-drawing had risen in a powder-pale hand. Not words yet. Not anything he could read at a glance. A route, maybe. A narrow line bending east under a cluster of older marks, with one point scored harder than the rest as if some buried place beneath the castle had heard the Hall ring and answered, quietly, from below.

Owen stared at it until the hum in the walls and the pulse in his palm seemed to become the same thing.

Then he folded the page shut and sat on the end of his bed in the dark, listening for footsteps that did not come soon enough to make him feel better.


# Chapter 7

The Grinlow Leaves did not change all at once. Three nights after Amycus Carrow had turned the punishment dais into a lesson and Owen had answered with the condition of contact instead of any proper spell, a page near the centre of the manuscript began to take ink. At first he thought it was soot from the common-room fire. Then the marks persisted when he shifted the book into candlelight, pale brown lines rising through the paper as if they had been waiting lower down and his stupidity in the corridor had driven them upward. By supper the page held a route under the kitchens, a cluster of founder marks he could now half follow, and one repeated instruction that his mind kept translating into the same plain phrase: ward scar sufficient. East Undercroft answering.

He shut the book twice and opened it again. His right palm, the one the coffer had branded in September, ached against the cloth cover. His wand had been unreliable since the greenhouse and worse since Martha, if he was honest, since Amycus had made her stand there and he had let the whole room watch him fail in public. The castle had answered too. For hours after the punishment the walls had seemed to ring through his feet. Even now, in certain corridors, his molars picked up a dull pressure as if somebody far below was tapping stone with a metal spoon.

By half eight he gave up pretending he could wait for Prue or think sensibly alone. The page wanted a place, and the only person who had ever hinted that the kitchens ran deeper than pipes and coal was Tallow.

The kitchens were nearly finished for the night. Heat breathed out of them in thick wet gusts that smelled of onions, soap, singed pastry, and the heavier stew intended for tomorrow's lunch. Tallow stood at the scrub table beneath the hanging copper pans, polishing pudding spoons one by one with a square of old flannel and rejecting any that showed the smallest twist. He put the bent ones to his left in a separate little line, as if they had disappointed him personally.

Owen waited until the nearest pair of elves disappeared into the scullery with a cauldron between them. "Tallow."

Tallow did not look up. "If young sir wants treacle crusts, there are two under the blue cloth and he may take only one, because greed makes crumbs."

"I don't want crusts."

"That is already an alarming start."

Owen set the wrapped manuscript on the table between the straight spoons and the bent ones. "I need the east-side maintenance run. The older one. Under this level."

At that Tallow's hand stopped. The flannel stayed clamped round the spoon bowl. He lifted his eyes at last, and the skin around them tightened before anything else in his face did. "No," he said. "Young sir needs sleep, and perhaps better judgement if there is any left."

"It's in the Leaves." Owen pulled the cloth back just enough to show the new page. "This wasn't visible before. Not until after the corridor."

Tallow stared without touching. His damp hands hovered an inch above the paper and then retreated into the flannel. "Yes," he said softly. "The house heard about the corridor. Every knife in the wash-rack sang. The tureens knocked their lids off. That doesn't make it a place for students."

"It means the site answered."

"It means you rang something old and stupidly loud."

Owen felt the correction arrive in him like a click. "Not loud, exactly. Specific. I think the scar matched whatever threshold this uses. It says sufficient." He tapped the founder marks with one ink-stained finger. "Or near enough. I need to know whether the larger claim is real."

Tallow set the spoon down with deliberate care. "Young sir keeps saying larger claim as if that is tidier than saying what he means. The old work hurts my kind. The deeper routes hurt worse. There are passages house-elves use and passages we know better than to use. This is one of the second sort."

"I know."

"No, you don't. Knowing a word for pain isn't the same as having it behind the eyes."

Owen nearly answered too quickly and stopped himself. "Right. No. Sorry. I know that I don't know it the same way. But the page changed now, not in September, not after the greenhouse. Now. If the disturbance has opened the undercroft, then either the manuscript's true and there's something under the castle worth hiding, or I've burnt my hand and half broken my wand for a very elaborate lie. I need to see which."

Tallow made a small sound in his throat that might have been contempt or worry. He looked past Owen at the banked ovens, at the stacked plates, at the ceiling as if the school above them had become heavy enough to notice. "There are rules," he said.

Owen waited.

"There's no wandlight in the run. The old seams don't care for school spells. There's no touching side marks unless I say. If the house gives warning through the floor, we stop. If I say back, we go back. You won't argue just because boys who love books think pain is an opinion."

"I won't."

Tallow's mouth pulled sideways. "That answer would be prettier if I believed it." He folded the flannel, slid it into his apron, and swept the rejected spoons into a drawer with more irritation than force. "Come on, then. Before I remember sense."

He led Owen past the main ovens, through the buttery, and into a root store where sacks of potatoes leaned against the wall in soft collapsing towers. Tallow paused long enough to straighten a crate of onions whose corner had tipped into damp. Only after he had wedged a folded rag under it and nodded to himself did he tug aside an empty flour bin and uncover a squat iron door low in the stone.

The hinge made no sound when he opened it. Cold came out instead, thick and mineral. Owen crouched, then had to fold lower still as the passage dropped away under a barrel curve of stone no full-grown adult wizard would have chosen willingly. The grease lamp Tallow carried threw a short amber circle that moved over wet mortar, old pipe clamps, and occasional founder marks cut into the wall like maintenance instructions left for hands smaller than his. The air narrowed as they went. Kitchen noise sank into the stone behind them until all that remained was their own movement, Owen's sleeves brushing grit, Tallow's bare feet placing and replacing themselves with careful precision.

After twenty yards the pressure started in earnest. It was not pain exactly at first. It was the sensation of having bitten hard on tinfoil and never quite stopped. The roots of Owen's teeth hummed. His right palm grew hot through the bandage. He kept one hand on the floor for balance and felt, through cold slime and grit, a faint recurrent thud as if something far off were hitting the castle with the side of a fist.

Tallow slowed. Owen heard his breathing change.

"Are you all right?"

"Tallow is walking," he said, which was not an answer. Then, plainer, "Don't ask again unless you mean to turn round for the answer."

The passage bent east. Twice it narrowed enough that Owen had to put the manuscript ahead of him and worm after it on one shoulder. A hot line ran through the wall here, not pipe heat but stone heat, dry and almost feverish under the damp. At one bend he saw old broom wear on the floor, centuries of slight curved scratches where something had once been dragged regularly through this route. It was not secrecy for its own sake, he thought. It was service and upkeep. Whatever lay below had needed mops and buckets and hands.

That was what steadied him more than any amount of diagram. Hidden things in stories were always altars, tombs, prophecies. This place smelled of wet lime and old soot and of things kept working by people no one had thanked properly in several hundred years.

The passage opened just enough for him to straighten at the waist. Ahead, the lamp found a wall that was too smooth to be natural and too featureless to be a door unless one already knew it was there. The founder marks on his page matched the shallow cuts round its edge exactly. At the center sat a thumb-deep hollow lined with the same dull white metal that threaded certain pages of the Leaves when the light caught them wrong.

Tallow had stopped several feet back. His singed ears were pressed flat. "Quickly, if it's going to open."

Owen wedged the grease lamp on a stone nub and peeled the cloth off his right hand. The old burn across his palm had faded from blister-red to a stubborn brown gloss, but near the life line a fresher mark showed, not on skin but in sensation, the place where the corridor working had rung back through him. When he pressed his hand into the hollow the metal went hot at once. It was not enough to burn. It was enough to tell him he had been expected.

Something shifted inside the wall. Pins withdrew one after another with small dense knocks. The seam breathed out dust and colder air. Then the slab rolled inward on hidden weight.

The East Undercroft was not a crypt. Owen had to revise the thought immediately because crypt was the sort of word stories provided when one had not yet seen the room. This was a workshop, or a pumping station, or the buried engine room of something large enough to use a castle as weatherproofing. The chamber was roughly circular and high enough to lose the ceiling in shadow, but the floor was cut with drains and service channels, not graves. Benches of the same black-green stone as the threshold stood against two walls. Iron rings had been bolted into one section of floor for hauling or bracing. Shelves, long emptied, still kept the ghost-shapes of stored tools in dust less thick than it ought to have been, as if the air here rejected ordinary dirt.

At the centre rose the anchor stone.

It came out of the floor to just below Owen's chest, a faceted mass of green-black rock the size of a coal cart and too exact in its geometry to belong to any quarry he knew. Four broader bands left it through the stone channels like roots or conduits, vanishing under the walls toward the cardinal directions. Finger-width veins of silver and the same white metal ran through the block in ruled circuits, dipping below the surface and emerging again. One whole face had been repaired at least twice. He could see older staples of some dark iron buried under a later skin of leaded sealant, and over that, founder-era rune cuts recarved where a crack had interrupted them. This was not mystical mood. This was apparatus under maintenance, damaged, patched, still in use.

Tallow stayed near the doorway with one hand braced to the jamb. Sweat had darkened the soot at his temples. "Mind the drains," he said. "They look dry because nothing living is foolish enough to drip here."

Owen barely heard him. He moved around the stone slowly, forcing himself not to lunge the way he wanted to. Each facet carried notation. Some he now recognized from the Leaves: conditions of masking, load transfer, thresholds for signal suppression. Others remained beyond him, dense as mathematics glimpsed through dirty glass. At the east side a section no bigger than a school slate had hairline fractures spidering across the white metal. That had to be the ward-scar, or at least the local consequence of it. The corridor working had not damaged a metaphor. It had struck an actual thing.

On the far wall stood the table the manuscript had tried and failed to describe until now. It was not wood and not quite metal. A shallow slab of bronze or brass sloped out from the masonry on thick fixed supports. Its surface was inlaid with silver points, threaded arcs, tiny black inserts like blind eyes, and a washed pale disc at the centre that took him a moment to understand as not sky but world. It was not a map for travellers. It was a service chart, a load table. The stars around it were not decorative but relational, lines of sight and pressure and reference against which the central mask could be kept true.

When he laid his unbandaged hand against the table's edge, the silver inlays brightened under the skin of the metal, not with light exactly but with legibility. A cluster to the northwest of the central disc sharpened until it matched the local geometry on the anchor stone. That had to be Hogwarts. It was not named in letters he could read, but it was fixed by relation, by angle, by the particular founder mark repeated both here and at the chamber entrance. It was one repair node among others. There were more points across the table, most dim, two nearly dead, one flaring faintly and irregularly in a way that made his stomach drop.

He heard himself laugh once under his breath, not from amusement but because his body had chosen the wrong sound for shock. "It's true."

Tallow looked up sharply. "Which part?"

"All of it. Or enough." Owen leaned closer, eyes stinging. "This is a planetary masking ward system. Not a school defence, not just the castle. Hogwarts is one anchor. Maybe one of several, I can't tell the whole spread yet. The manuscript wasn't being grandiose. It was being literal."

Tallow's face did not brighten with triumph. He looked at the stone as a cook might look at a cracked range that had just been confirmed as load-bearing for the entire house. "Literal is often the rude option."

Owen scarcely processed the reply. Along the lower lip of the table ran three neat columns of marks, finer than the load notations and grouped in repeated sets. He knew some of the symbols now from the newly surfaced gloss pages. They marked capacity, interval, transit, and after-remnant load. He read the columns once, mistranslated them as inventory, then read again and felt the floor tilt under him.

It was not a death register.

The numbers stepped down in ordered stages over a span of years. Against each reduction sat the same directional mark, outward from the world disc, then the same maintenance note about masking load after transfer. The final lines did not describe collapse or loss of contact. They described closure and planned departure, the sort of ugly practical notation one left when moving a household or shutting an old line after the last train had gone. They did not mean everyone was dead. They meant everyone was gone.

He put his fingertips against the metal and tried to make himself slower. "They left on purpose," he said. "The Meridian Commonwealth. This isn't ruins talking to themselves after the fact. They built the ward, shifted the population, and left it running around the planet. They meant to go."

"Left what behind?" Tallow asked.

Owen turned his head but not enough to lose the lines. "Us, apparently."

The lamp flame bent sideways though there was no draft. For a second the black inserts around the edge of the table darkened further, then blurred into a ring of moving pressure. Owen thought he had gone light-headed. Then the manuscript page in his pocket gave a hot twitch against his ribs, and the outer band of the table resolved into notation at last.

There was very little poetry in it. That frightened him more than any grand warning would have done. The repeated mark in the margin meant something like notice or lock-on, but written in the tone of maintenance instructions. The warnings were brutally plain: recurrent predatory convergence at high discharge, mask integrity critical during active event. Another cluster cross-referenced the anchors with the same practical coldness: excess signal, local flare, reacquisition risk.

There was no demon name and no noble battle between light and darkness. There was only the calm brutal record of a system built because bright magic was visible from far away and visibility invited the wrong kind of attention.

Owen followed the corresponding lines outward with his eyes. Beyond the world disc the silver arcs thinned, broke, gathered again at points where the metal had been worried almost white. It was not a creature's outline. It was not anything so kind as outline. It looked more like the table had been touched in the same places over and over by something that recognized pattern, not mercy. A hunter did not need a face to be a hunter. A gull looking down at surf did not hate fish. It just saw motion.

Behind him, far above and thickened by stone, something heavy struck in the castle proper. A door slammed, or Amycus hit a boy into a wall, or a suit of armour toppled in one of the corridors. The undercroft returned it as a muffled body-blow through the floor. Owen flinched because his nerves did, not because the sound meant what it would have meant an hour ago.

Tallow heard it too. "School again," he said wearily.

The word settled it. The noise above them was not diminished, forgiven, or made less true. It was school again. It was Carrow's shouting, Snape's surveillance, Neville trying to hold frightened children together with string and nerve, Martha bleeding in public while Owen had sorted a rule inside his head. All of that remained exactly as ugly as it had been. But it was local ugliness. Above them the occupied castle was making a human amount of noise inside a machine built to hide a planet. The proportion had changed and would not change back.

He became aware of Tallow's breathing, thin and wrong. Owen looked over. The elf's free hand was clamped hard enough round the doorjamb to blanch the knuckles under soot.

"We should go," Owen said.

"Tallow said back if the house warned."

"You were right."

"Tallow is often right in kitchens and seldom thanked for it."

Despite everything, the line nearly pulled a sound from Owen that could have been a laugh. He stepped away from the table and back toward the centre stone. Up close, on the cracked eastern facet, he could see where some older repair had failed and been reworked by less skilled hands. A smear of founder-era tool scoring wandered off true by less than the width of a quill. The whole planet, he thought with a clarity that felt obscene, might presently depend on margins like that.

He rested his burned palm lightly against the anchor stone. Heat answered from deep inside it, not surface warmth but stored strain. The contact traveled up his arm to the socket and sat there. He did not know how to repair it yet. He barely knew how to read it. But the chamber had ended the last refuge of disbelief. There was work under Hogwarts, real work, older than house rivalries and ministers and Dark Lords. The war above had not become small in consequence. It had become provincial in scale.

When he finally turned away, he took the image of the table with him so hard it hurt: the world held at the centre of a maintenance chart, the other dim nodes, the whitening marks outside the mask where notice gathered whenever magic flared too brightly. The castle overhead groaned once more, some ordinary violence or ordinary panic, and for the first time since September Owen heard it as interruption.

Tallow sealed the door behind them with both hands and a muttered string of house marks Owen could not follow. By the time they had crawled halfway back to the kitchens, the pressure in his teeth had eased just enough for thought. He knew he should be sick with fear. Instead he felt the colder, steadier thing that had replaced fear whenever the manuscript stopped teasing and started proving.

At the flour-bin entrance Tallow paused to set the iron panel back into its slot and wipe his palms on his apron as if he could remove the undercroft by ordinary kitchen means. "You won't bring a crowd next time," he said.

"There won't be a crowd."

"No. I thought not."

Owen tucked the Leaves under his arm. Beyond the root store the kitchen clocks ticked, pans cooled, and somebody in the scullery dropped a ladle with a familiar domestic clang. The sound was so normal it almost offended him. He stood listening to it a second too long.

"Young sir," Tallow said, quieter now, "whatever that stone is, the house has been carrying it a very long time. Mind how you use what it gives you."

Owen nodded, but his eyes had already gone back to the wrapped shape of the manuscript. The sentence he ought to have answered with did not come. Above them the school continued, all punishments and rumours and cold meals and brave stupid plans. Under it, older machinery strained to keep the world from being noticed.

He went upstairs knowing which problem had won.


# Chapter 8

Christmas thinned Hogwarts without making it kind. The stairs still shifted underfoot with their usual private malice and the portraits still watched from their frames like informers with gilded borders, but the corridors between meals held more cold in them than before. Sound carried farther. A laugh from two landings up came down the stone stripped of any warmth it might once have had. Even the Great Hall, with its bewitched candles and the paper chains somebody had bullied into service again, looked less like a feast than a room trying to remember what feast meant.

Owen left it as soon as he decently could. The kitchens below were wet with heat and honest work, and after the East Undercroft he had wanted one room in the castle that admitted, without theory, what people were for.

The air changed halfway down the service stair. Cold stone gave way to yeast, onions, damp wool steaming itself dry, and the sweet metallic tang of washed copper. By the time he ducked through the painting and into the kitchens proper his glasses would have fogged, if he had worn any. The room hit him with light and condensation. Huge pans hung over the ranges, every polished curve blotched white with steam. Holly had been jammed into cracks in the beams where it would do no structural harm. Someone had stuck cloves into the peels of two oranges and set them by the big proving drawer, so the whole place smelled faintly festive and mostly of soup.

Tallow stood on an upturned crate at the nearest stove, dipping a ladle, watching the soup sheet back into the pot, and muttering at an open cutlery drawer. He had tucked a blue pencil behind one singed ear and left a greasy thumbprint on the side of his own cheek. On the crate beside him lay three bent dessert spoons and two more in a neat accusing row, as if he meant to hold a hearing later.

"No, young sir," he said before turning around. "Don't stand there trailing corridor cold over the tiles. Sit down. Soup's right now and if it gets any thicker it'll have to be sliced."

He turned then, saw Owen properly, and clicked his tongue at the look of him.

"You're pale in a peevish way," Tallow said. "That means hungry. Sit."

Owen sat. The table was scrubbed pine with knife-nicks all along one side where years of hurried slicing had gone through bread and into wood. It looked ordinary enough to hurt a little. Somewhere behind him, out of sight, another elf laughed. Something went into a hot pan with a violent hiss.

"What's wrong with the spoons?" Owen asked.

Tallow shot the drawer a black look. "What's wrong with the spoons is that students think cutlery is portable by divine license. Five dessert spoons gone in three days and one soup ladle singed black. No house can keep standards if every pudding is attended by theft."

He stirred, tasted again, then added a cap of cream with the solemnity of a man correcting a legal injustice.

"If the school gets much quieter," he said, "the missing spoons will start accusing one another just to hear company."

"Then interrogate the ugly ones first," Prue said from the next table. "They're the likeliest to break."

Owen had not seen her at once. She was half-hidden behind a stack of plates, tall even sitting down, her dark curls pushed back with a ribbon that might once have been navy and now had the colourless defeat of too many washings. One hinge of her spectacles had been repaired with a twist of copper wire, and she kept nudging them up her nose with the heel of her hand so she would not get grease on the lenses. There was ink on both cuffs. There was always ink on both cuffs.

Tallow sniffed. "Miss Hollis speaks as if the handsome spoons are any more loyal."

"I don't trust pretty silver," Prue said. "It's vain metal."

That got a brief snort from Owen before the kitchen door banged again and Martha came in sideways, shoulder first, with her scarf half off and her plait loosening down her back. She had a heel of bread in one hand already, which meant Tallow had either given up on stopping her years ago or never tried in earnest. She shut the door with her boot and pointed the bread at the pot.

"If that's the parsnip one, I want the thick bit," she said.

"Miss Sorrell wants the thick bit of every situation," Tallow said, but there was more approval than complaint in it. "Sit before you eat the tablecloth as well."

Martha flung herself onto the bench opposite Owen. She was still compact even in all the winter layers, broad through the shoulders, face pink with cold and temper. One cuff had split and been mended in green thread that did not remotely match the jumper. She saw him looking at it and tucked her wrist under the table without comment.

"You escaped the Hall early," she said.

"So did you."

"Amycus was trying to sing. I drew a line."

"Sensibly," Prue said.

Tallow brought the pot over himself rather than trusting any of them with it. The soup came out thick and gold-brown, steaming hard enough to blur the scratched windows over the sink. He followed it with a plate of bread, a dish of butter gone soft at the edges, and a smaller plate on which sat four mince pies with badly pinched lids.

"Those are for later," he said at once, because Martha had looked at them.

"I know what 'later' means."

"You know what it means when someone else says it. That's not the same."

Martha rolled her eyes and tore bread anyway. Prue took one corner of a slice and set the rest back down with exactness. Owen wrapped both hands around his bowl. The heat went through his fingers and found the burn in his palm at once. The scar did not like sudden warmth. It brightened under the skin with a sensation that was not pain exactly, more a private wrongness, as if the hand remembered the coffer and mistrusted all containers since.

For a little while they ate like people who had not come to accomplish anything. Martha blew on each spoonful too hard and splashed the heel of her thumb, swore, licked it clean, and kept going. Prue scraped cream from the rim with a piece of bread because she said skin was only acceptable if attached to a person. Tallow, passing between counters, kept glancing over as if he expected one of them to insult the meal by turning solemn in its presence.

It was Martha who preserved the ordinariness first.

"Dad does bubble and squeak on Boxing Day whether anyone wants it or not," she said into her bowl. "He says if you've made it through Christmas you owe the leftovers proper respect."

"Your father sounds doctrinaire," Prue said.

"He's about frying. Not about anything useful. He does it in the big iron pan because he says the little one lies about heat. Burns the first side every year and calls it flavour."

Tallow made a scandalised noise from the sink.

"That's not flavour," he said. "That's impatience with grease attached."

"Tastes good, though." Martha tore another piece of bread. "He puts in too many sprouts on purpose because he knows my aunt will pick them out and complain and then he can complain back. Whole afternoon sorted."

"That does sound useful," Prue said.

Owen said, before he could stop himself, "Does it actually squeak, or is that just the name?"

Martha looked at him over the rim of her spoon, then laughed, properly laughed, the sound bright enough to make the room larger.

"You poor sheltered thing. Yes. A bit. If the potato's wrong."

"Or if the cook is wrong," Tallow muttered.

"The cook is always wrong, according to someone," Prue said.

"According to evidence," Tallow said.

The talk moved sideways the way talk did when nobody was trying to win it. Martha described her father's habit of cooking in one sock and one slipper because he never noticed when laundry baskets ate the other pair. Prue said her mother overboiled every green vegetable in existence and had therefore ruined saltless food for her on principle. Tallow said a school full of adolescents could empty six bread bins in an evening and still look as if it had been starved by policy. Martha asked if there was any crackling left from upstairs. Tallow said there had been and now there was not and stared at her until she stopped smiling quite so hard.

Owen let the voices pass over him. He answered when spoken to. He ate more than he had meant to. He watched steam run up the windows and vanish. The kitchen had its own weather and its own law. Under the table his shoes were damp from the thaw coming off them. Somewhere below the flags, and not as far below as anybody here would have believed, the anchor stone sat in its chamber holding a wound in the world together by rules nobody at this table had asked to learn.

The thought came and stayed. The anchor did not care that it was Christmas. It did not care about soup, or spoons, or paper chains in the Hall. The fact of that should have made the room seem smaller. Instead it made it seem precious in a way that irritated him, because precious things were distracting.

Tallow came back with the mince pies after all, one on each chipped plate, and set them down without admitting that he had planned to do so.

"There," he said. "If anyone says the kitchens are ungenerous, Tallow will require names and dates."

Martha bit into hers at once and hissed through her teeth when the filling burned her mouth.

"Serves you right," Prue said.

"I'm hungry, not strategic."

"A distinction many movements fail to maintain."

"Listen to yourself," Martha said. "You've spent too long with catalogues."

Prue smiled into the pastry. The smile lasted half a second.

"Did Miri come down last week?" Martha asked Tallow abruptly. "The little Ravenclaw one. The one who used to nick the sugared orange peel from the tray when she thought you weren't looking."

The change in the room was small enough that no sound announced it. Tallow's hand stopped on the back of Martha's bench. Prue lowered her eyes to her plate.

"No," Tallow said after a moment. "Miss Kell had not been in the kitchens since the train came back."

"She's not in her dorm either," Prue said. Her voice had gone flatter, not colder, just more careful. "Her bed's stripped. Her trunk's gone."

Martha swallowed, too fast this time. "Did someone fetch her?"

Prue gave a little helpless lift of one shoulder. "If they did, they didn't sign the night ledger."

Tallow resumed moving. He picked up the empty soup bowls one by one though nobody had quite finished.

"The little miss left two gloves in the warming cupboard in November," he said. "Green ones with the thumbs stretched. Tallow kept them back in case. They're still there."

No one said anything to that. Across the room the other elves kept working. A pan clattered. Somebody laughed again, not having heard this end of the table or having heard and declined to carry it.

Martha worried at the crust of her pie until it broke apart under her fingers.

"Hester's brother didn't come back either," she said. "At least, not after the first week. They told the table he'd got dragon pox, which was stupid because Hester said her family doesn't even keep a kettle hot unless they have visitors. Nobody with dragon pox in that house would survive long enough to infect anyone."

Prue made a noise that might have been a laugh in another month. It was not a laugh now.

"That's very nearly medical reasoning," she said.

"It's kitchen reasoning. Better science."

Owen looked from one of them to the other. The names meant almost nothing to him. Not nothing. He remembered a small Ravenclaw girl with fast hands and sugar on her mouth once, maybe that had been Miri, and some boy Martha had pointed out in the corridor as having a contraband set of Gobstones in his sock. But what sat in the room now was not really those people. It was absence given weight. He could see that. He just could not quite feel it in the same units.

"A lot of people go home over Christmas," he said, because silence had begun to seem like an accusation.

He heard the inadequacy of it before the others did. Or perhaps at the same time.

Martha stared. "Owen."

"I only mean we don't know. Some of that's ordinary. The trains, and families, and-"

Prue set her spoon down with care. "That wasn't the thing she meant."

"I know, I just meant it isn't necessarily one category."

"People aren't categories," Martha said. It came out tired rather than fierce.

Tallow gathered the plates, not quickly, not theatrically. His ears had flattened back a little.

"Young sir," he said without looking at Owen, "there's room in a house for uncertainty and still no room at table."

That ought to have embarrassed Owen into silence. Instead it made him want to defend the principle, which was worse.

"I'm not saying it doesn't matter."

"All right," Martha said at once. "Don't."

She bent over her ruined pie and ate the rest in two practical bites as if the conversation had not happened. Prue took off her spectacles and wiped them with the least greasy corner of her napkin, though there was nothing on the lenses Owen could see. Tallow carried dishes away and began, with unnecessary energy, to inspect the cutlery drawer again.

The meal did not collapse. That was the strangest part. It went on. Martha asked whether anyone had seen snow settle on the greenhouses. Prue said only on the north side and only badly. Tallow complained that students tracked salt over the flagstones and then blamed the floor for being gritty. Owen answered a question about whether the fifth-years were still being made to polish trophy cases with toothbrushes. He said yes. Martha said bastards. Prue said yes, that too.

But the ease had been nicked open. Everything that came after had to cross the little cut.

When they were done, Prue folded her napkin around the remains of her pie crust and stood.

"I've got to get back before my dorm decides I've defected to domestic service," she said.

"Leave the plate," Tallow told her. "If anyone is defecting it won't be with crockery."

"I make no promises."

She slid her spectacles back on, gathered her scarf, and then hesitated just long enough to look at Owen, not accusingly, not kindly either. More as if she were rearranging something on an internal shelf and finding that it no longer fit where she had put it.

"Good night," she said.

"Night," Owen said.

Martha said, "See you tomorrow?"

"Probably. Unless the library collapses from sentiment."

It was a decent joke. On another evening Martha would have thrown something small at her for it. Tonight she only nodded. Prue left. Cold knifed in with the opening door and was gone again.

Martha stayed seated another half minute, rolling the stem of an empty spoon between her fingers.

"Walk up with me," she said at last.

Owen said yes before he considered whether he wanted to.

Tallow handed Martha a square of paper with a mince pie in it and, after a moment's thought, handed Owen one as well.

"Take your scarves," he said. "And if either of you pockets so much as a teaspoon, Tallow will know."

"You always say that."

"And I'm always right eventually."

* * *

They climbed out of the kitchens into the narrower passage where the heat gave up quickly. The stone there held yesterday's cold and intended to keep it. Orange peel and roasting fat fell away behind them. Ahead lay the dark service corridor toward the Hufflepuff cellar, lit every twenty feet by lamps with mean little blue flames. Martha wrapped the pie in both hands for warmth before taking a bite.

For a while they just walked. Their footsteps had a different sound out here, thinner and more solitary than the kitchen clatter. From somewhere above came a burst of song from the Great Hall, late and ragged and overforced. It sounded like people trying to prove to one another that the season still existed.

"He's right about the sprouts," Martha said with her mouth half full. "My dad puts too many in."

"Apparently your whole family cooks by provocation."

"Yes." She swallowed. "Works well till it doesn't."

They turned into the lower corridor where narrow windows looked out at black grounds dusted grey with old snow. The glass had filmed over at the corners. Someone had scratched a star into one pane and then tried to rub it out again.

Martha slowed. "I got a letter this morning," she said.

Owen looked at her. "From home?"

"Obviously from home. Nobody else sends letters that smell of paraffin and dog."

"Right."

She picked pastry off the paper with her thumbnail.

"Dad wrote about the weather for most of it," she said. "The canal freezing at the edges. Mrs Greeley's hens not laying. My cousin taking a shovel to the privy step because of the ice. Then two lines about me not to worry and to eat enough if the school was feeding us. Which sounds funny till you remember who's in charge of the school."

"Letters are being watched," Owen said. "He was probably being careful."

"I know he was being careful."

Martha did not snap it. That made it worse. She kept her eyes on the window as they passed, her own reflection gliding beside them in warped black patches.

"That's not the difficult bit," she said. "The difficult bit is that I couldn't tell whether he was being careful because he's frightened or because he's always careful now. Those are different things and they look exactly the same on paper."

Owen had an answer to that, three answers perhaps, and every one of them was wrong. Carefulness did not map cleanly to danger. Delay in post did not imply seizure. Families under scrutiny learned compression. His mind offered these facts with the useless eagerness of a bad student raising his hand.

He chose the least awful one and managed to make it awful anyway.

"You probably can't tell from one letter," he said. "Not on its own."

Martha laughed once, under her breath.

"See, there it is."

"What?"

"That." She turned to face him properly now, back against the cold wall. "I say I'm scared and you start trimming the edges off it till it'll fit in a notebook."

"I didn't mean-"

"I know you didn't mean anything by it. That's the problem."

The lamp nearest them guttered blue. Owen could hear the faint hiss from it. At the far end of the corridor the barrel-door entrance to Hufflepuff sat in shadow, iron hoops catching a little light.

"I'm only saying one letter isn't enough to conclude-" he started.

"Conclude?" Martha said. "For fuck's sake."

She pushed away from the wall, then stopped herself from walking off and came back a pace as if sheer fairness compelled her.

"I'm not asking you to conclude anything," she said. "I'm asking whether it gets to you. Properly. The missing people, the letters, the way everyone keeps talking lower every week like if we whisper enough none of it will be true."

Owen looked at her. There were flour smudges on her sleeve from the kitchen table. One bit of pastry clung to the scarf at her throat. Her plait had come half undone, strands sticking to her cheek where the cold had dampened them. She looked fifteen and frightened and furious at herself for letting the frightened part show.

Of course it got to him. Amycus got to him. Martha nearly taking curses in November got to him. The undercroft got to him so thoroughly he could no longer stand on one floor of the castle without thinking about the floor beneath. But what had happened under the castle had broken scale. He kept looking at the school as if it were both everything and a small bright corner of something much worse. The trouble was that both thoughts were true. The uglier trouble was that the second one kept swallowing the first.

"It does," he said, and heard how bloodless that sounded. He tried again. "I just can't, if I let every- sorry. That's not what I mean. I mean if I start treating every gap and rumour as the whole shape of things, I can't think straight enough to be any use."

Martha closed her eyes.

"Any use to what?"

He should have lied. Not cleanly, not well, just enough. To us, perhaps. To the school. To you. Instead he hesitated. The hesitation itself answered.

When she opened her eyes again, whatever softness had been left in them had gone flat.

"Right," she said.

"Martha-"

"No, I heard it."

"You didn't, not exactly."

"Then exactly what did I hear?"

He had nothing fit for air. The corridor waited with her. The blue lamp hissed. From very far away, or very deep below, the castle gave one of its settling groans.

"There's something I'm trying to hold onto," he said at last. "And if I lose the thread of it because I'm- because everything here is loud all the time-"

"Everything here," Martha said slowly, "is loud because people are getting hurt."

"I know."

"Do you?"

He flinched, not in body so much as in attention. It felt like missing a stair in the dark and only learning it from the jolt in the knee. She saw that too.

"You know what's stupid?" Martha said. She looked away, out through the filmy window where the grounds were only a deeper black. "I keep thinking if they came for me I'd want thirty seconds to make the bed. Just that. Pull it straight. Hide the socks. I know that's idiotic. I know if they come they're not going to stand there while I tuck corners. But I keep thinking it anyway."

She rubbed the side of her thumb over the paper that had held the pie until it tore. Tiny flakes drifted down to the flags.

"And when Hester's bed got stripped and Miri's trunk vanished and my dad writes about hens as if that's enough information for a person to live on, I think maybe the whole world is turning into that. Beds made for nobody. Meals for whoever's left. I just wanted-" She stopped and shook her head. "I wanted you not to answer it like a timetable."

The sentence landed without force, which made it land harder. Owen stared at the shredded paper in her hand.

He could have said he was sorry. He was sorry. But even now the apology kept trying to become a clarification: sorry, but there are other pressures; sorry, but if you could see; sorry, but. Every version betrayed itself before it reached his mouth.

He managed, "I don't know how to do both."

Martha let out a breath that clouded in front of her.

"That's at least honest," she said. "Miserable, but honest."

They stood in silence. In the kitchens below, someone dragged a crate across stone. The sound came up through the floor faint and grating. Owen thought absurdly of Tallow lining up spoons in their drawer, counting what the room still possessed.

"Prue notices it too, you know," Martha said.

He looked up. "What?"

"This thing with you." She made a small, irritated motion around his face, his shoulders, perhaps his whole person. "You stand there and you are here, technically, and then after about ten seconds you go somewhere else. Not physically. Worse."

He wanted to deny that. He wanted, more secretly, to ask whether Prue had said anything specific. Both impulses were mean.

"I've just been tired."

Martha gave him such a flat look that the lie died where it was.

"Fine," she said. "Be tired, then."

She folded the torn paper into a square so small it disappeared in her fist.

"I keep bringing the wrong things to you," she said. "That's my mistake."

"Martha, that's not-"

"No. Leave it."

She tucked her scarf up under her chin with two hard little jerks, the way she did when both hands wanted a job. Then she stepped around him and started down the corridor toward the barrel door.

Owen moved after her once, then stopped. "Will I see you in the morning?"

She did not turn round.

"You'll see me somewhere," she said.

It was not dramatic. It was almost nothing. She lifted a hand without looking back, gave the Hufflepuff barrel the correct knock as soon as she reached it, and slipped through when the wood swung aside. The door shut behind her with its usual thick, stupid warmth.

Owen stood in the blue lamp light with the untouched mince pie cooling in his hand.

He knew enough to know he had answered badly. He knew, too, that Martha had not asked for brilliance, only proportion. The corridor smelled of old stone, paraffin, and the spiced fruit Tallow had sent upstairs in paper. Below him the kitchens went on with their washing, their scraping, their small repairs. Below that, deeper than appetite and friendship and the season itself, the anchor stone held its damaged silence.

He stayed where he was until the pie crust went hard through the paper and the cold found his fingers again.


# Chapter 9

By February the castle's cold had stopped behaving like weather and become one more school habit. It found the wet band above a sock, the strip of wrist between cuff and skin, the spot at the back of the knee where stone got in and stayed. Even in the kitchens there was no proper defeating it, only heat from ovens on one side and the draught from the service arches on the other, as if Hogwarts meant to keep every comfort half-argued.

Owen sat at the end of a scrubbed table with his ash-linen notebook under one hand and a heel of bread under the other, pretending to copy Herbology notes while he stared at the same three crooked marks for the fourth time. The marks had come out of the Grinlow Leaves that morning only after he pressed his burned palm flat across the margin and waited through the now-familiar wrongness in his teeth. South warming passage, fourth hinge from scullery arch. The English gloss in his own notebook looked childish beside the older notation, but he had not found a better one.

Tallow was on a stool by the nearest range, sorting spoons into piles with the concentration of a man handling bones. He muttered numbers under his breath, stopped to sniff one of the spoons in suspicion, then polished it on his apron anyway. The kitchens smelled of onions, wet stone, yeast, and a pan of something sweet catching at the edge.

Martha came in through the fruit portrait with sleet in her hair and a tea towel over one shoulder. Her dark plait had half come loose. One of her knuckles was split and freshly mended, the skin still shiny. She had a crate of potatoes braced against one hip as if she had forgotten it was there.

"You're hiding in plain sight again," she said.

Owen looked up. "I'm in the kitchens."

"Yes. Exactly." She set the crate down by Tallow's stool. "Have you eaten?"

"Some."

She pushed the bread heel nearer him with two fingers. "That doesn't count."

Tallow clicked his tongue without looking round. "Miss Sorrell is correct, and Tallow dislikes repeating an obvious fact in his own kitchen."

Martha pulled a face at the range, affectionate enough that Tallow allowed it. Then she turned back to Owen, and the quick ordinary irritation in her expression thinned into purpose. "Need you tonight."

He glanced at the notebook before he meant to. Martha noticed everything practical and most things that were not. Her eyes went there and back.

"For what?"

"Lower west corridor. After curfew." She hooked the tea towel into her waistband. "We've got two first-years stowed in the disused classroom by the staff lavatory, and Hester with them trying not to make herself useful. They bolted after Dark Arts this afternoon and somebody needs them out before Amycus does his late sweep and finds them where they ought not to be. Neville's putting a noise together at one end, we move them through the other, and then everybody gets downstairs before anyone important starts feeling clever."

Owen kept his face still. So it was lower west. The service line in the margin note had to be part of that run. The burn in his right palm gave one small hot throb, enough to make him close his hand.

"Why me?"

Martha stared at him as if the question itself were rude. "Because you know the kitchen side better than half the people doing this. Because you can get through a narrow gap without sounding like a wardrobe falling over. Because there are three twelve-year-olds in a room that smells of bleach and mop water and fear, and I'm short a person." She wiped her wet hand on the towel. "And because I asked you, Owen. That one was meant to matter a bit."

He took a bite of the bread to buy time. It was still warm in the middle. Across the table Tallow lifted a spoon, frowned at a bent handle, and put it in a different pile as if the castle's fate might yet be decided by cutlery.

"What sort of noise?" Owen said.

"The ordinary sort. Armor, lavatory, corridor mess, a few things where they shouldn't be." Martha leaned against the table. "Nothing grand. We don't need grand. We need four minutes and nobody fainting."

"If it's only that, why short a person?"

"Because people keep getting caught, or hexed, or moved to detentions, or sent home in bits." She said it flatly, not for effect. "Because one of the sixth-years from Ravenclaw can't put weight on his foot after Tuesday. Because normal reasons."

Owen nodded. He could already see the corridor in the notebook's margin, not the real one but the older line beneath it, founder stone dressed over and over under newer doors. If he got there under cover of somebody else's operation, he could test the warming passage without having to invent a reason for being out after curfew. He could be down to the service run and back before Amycus finished shouting at the toilets.

He ought to have said no.

The Leaves had been clearer with every error: divided attention was not harmless merely because it felt efficient. Category did not care what he had meant. The corridor would be full of doors, latches, metal plates, old stone. They were shared structures, the exact sort of place where a careless amendment travelled farther than his nerves.

Martha was still watching him, though not hard, not yet. There was more hope in it than she would have liked him to notice.

"All right," he said.

Her shoulders changed first, dropping by about half an inch. Then the rest of her face followed. "Right. Good."

"I'm not promising I'm useful."

"No, I've met you before." For a second she nearly smiled. "Just turn up and do what you're told. That'll already be an improvement."

Tallow slid off the stool and crossed to a shelf without wasting movement. He handed Martha a folded cloth parcel that had not been there a moment ago, or at least not where Owen had seen it. The parcel smelled of cheese and pepper.

"For the little ones," he said. "And if anyone puts muddy feet on the west passage wall again, Tallow will know."

"They won't." Martha took the parcel with more care than she had shown the potatoes. "Thanks."

Tallow gave Owen a look from under singed brows. "The drain by the fourth arch is backing up. Mind where you stand if you don't want the castle on your stockings."

The fourth arch.

Owen said, too quickly, "Which fourth arch?"

Martha's eyes narrowed a fraction. Tallow only sniffed.

"From the scullery side," the elf said. "The one that always smells of cold iron when the weather turns. If you had been listening to the house instead of your notebook, you would know it."

"I was listening."

"Mm."

Martha picked up the potato crate again. "Second bell after lights-out. Old Charms storeroom, the one off lower west. Don't be late."

She started for the portrait, then stopped and looked back. Her voice, when she spoke, was lighter on purpose. "And do try not to bring that face with you."

"What face?"

"That one. The one that says every other person's emergency is interrupting a timetable only you can see."

He opened his mouth, shut it again, and Martha went through the portrait before he found an answer that was not either defensive or true.

Tallow resumed sorting spoons. The kitchen noise filled back in around the gap she had left: pans shifting, water hissing on hot iron, some distant rattle from the scullery where another elf was losing patience with a tray.

Owen put his hand over the notebook until the page stopped looking exposed. The bread had gone cooler. The line in the margin had not changed. South warming passage, fourth hinge from scullery arch.

He told himself he could do both. He could help with the corridor. He could use the corridor. He could keep the amendment narrow. He could keep everyone moving.

That was the sort of sentence the Leaves liked hearing before they proved him wrong.

* * *

The old Charms storeroom had not held charms equipment in years. It held broken desks, two boxes of warped copybooks, a coat stand missing three hooks, and eight students trying not to breathe loudly.

Neville stood in the middle of them with a bit of chalk and the back of a cabinet door laid across two chairs. He had the corridor drawn on it in blunt white lines: one turn from the stair, the lavatory, the disused classroom, the fruit portrait passage. He looked more tired every time Owen saw him now, not in a noble way, only in the way of somebody whose sleep kept getting stolen by practical failures. There was a yellowing bruise along his jaw.

"Again," Neville said as Owen came in. "We're not here to win a war. We're here to get Hester and the two first-years out of a stupid place before Amycus finds them there. If anything goes wrong, the younger ones go first and the rest of us become scenery."

One of the younger ones, a second-year girl with one mitten safety-pinned to her sleeve so she would not lose it, raised her hand without waiting to be asked. "What if the scenery's already on fire?"

Martha, crouched by a canvas bag of junk at the wall, said, "Then run round it, Hester."

Hester nodded as if this was useful and final.

Beside her a first-year boy in a jumper two sizes too big kept smoothing his fringe down with spit and failing to make it stay. The third child, another first-year, was holding his wand and a wrapped sandwich in the same hand because he seemed unable to decide which mattered more. His spectacles had been repaired with thread so dark it looked almost blue.

Len, lanky and hollow-eyed, kept winding and unwinding a bit of string round his wrist until the skin went pink. By the door Jo worried at the sticking-plaster round both thumbs with her teeth, then caught herself and shoved her hands into her sleeves.

Owen had not expected sandwiches. That, more than anything, made the thing feel like Martha's work as much as Neville's.

Neville tapped the chalk map. "Len, stop worrying that string and trip the left-hand armor when Amycus comes round from the stair. Hester, you pull the spoon from the cistern brace only when Martha says, not before. Don't improve on the plan because you suddenly feel inspired. Martha takes the younger ones from the classroom across to the fruit portrait. Jo, keep those bandaged thumbs out of sight and stay on the portrait. Once they're through, everyone peels off separate."

He looked at Owen.

"East end," Neville said. "You keep the service press clear and the passage dark. If somebody comes up from the scullery side early, you cough twice and we all pretend we're idiots for another reason."

"Right," Owen said.

Neville held his stare for another beat, checking for whatever it was he had wanted from Owen back in the Room and had still not found. Then he went back to the cabinet door.

Martha lifted things out of the canvas bag and handed them round as she talked: a spoon bent nearly in half, string, a bottle of ink, three Filibuster scraps no bigger than cigarettes, a rag that smelled of vinegar, two rusted screws, one lump of soap shaved into slivers. It was not glamorous sabotage. It was school sabotage, the sort made by children with access to cupboards and grudges.

"The armor only needs to start moving," she said. "It doesn't need to kill anybody, so don't overdo it. The ink goes under the lavatory seat, not in the bowl. If it goes in the bowl, it just looks sad. Jo's got the portrait. Neville's got the stair. Owen's got the east end." She glanced up at him. "Have you got the east end?"

"Yes."

"Good."

The wrongness began before he left the storeroom. It was not much, only a pressure in the fillings of his teeth and a faint hum in the bones behind his ears. The lower west corridor was old enough, then. It was old in the useful way.

When they filed out two at a time and flattened themselves into the stone shadows, the corridor looked narrower than his memory of it. There were six doors in the space of thirty feet if he counted the lavatory stalls as doors, which he probably ought to have done sooner. The walls sweated faintly. One suit of armor stood by the lavatory corner with a dent in its breastplate and a halberd none of the original owners could have meant it to keep. Another stood opposite the service press at Owen's end, smaller and more decorative, its visor wired on years ago and badly polished.

The fourth arch from the scullery side smelled, exactly as Tallow had said, of cold iron. It was exactly the fourth arch. Owen stopped under it as if he were only checking sightlines. The service press set into the wall there was newer oak on older stone, the frame built into a seam that went down farther than the joinery had any right to know. Someone had patched the lower hinge in brass. The patch sat over scratch marks that were not accident.

His palm burn woke all the way.

Martha had gone to the far end with the two first-years. Neville was at the stair turn. Hester was kneeling by the cistern brace with the expression of a child trying very hard not to sneeze. Everyone else's attention was fixed where it ought to have been.

Owen took the notebook from inside his jumper and opened it against the wall. The corridor torchlight was bad for the older marks, but he had already copied what he needed. Three small signs, a narrower joining line, then the human note beside them so he would not lie to himself later about what he had intended: admit on first pressure, suppress complaint.

He should have stopped at the first sign.

He should have gone and stood where Neville had put him and done the stupid useful thing in front of him.

Instead he rubbed soot and tallow together with spit on the pad of his thumb, pressed the paste into the groove beneath the brass hinge-plate, and traced the signs no bigger than nail crescents. The stone answered at once, too quickly, with that deep unpleasant sense of a room taking notice.

He froze.

Nothing else happened yet.

From the far end Martha gave him the smallest possible jerk of the chin, telling him to get into position.

Owen slid the notebook away and put his fingers on the service press handle. It loosened under his hand so beautifully that for a fraction of a second he was stupid enough to feel pleased. There was no squeal and no resistance. The latch yielded like cloth.

Then, three doors down, another latch clicked.

He looked up.

The disused classroom door opposite opened itself an inch.

At the lavatory the nearest stall banged back hard enough to hit tile.

The little decorative armor at his shoulder lifted its visor on a wire snap that sounded exactly like a mouse trap going off.

Owen took his hand off the press. "Oh, for fuck's sake," he whispered.

* * *

The corridor did not explode all at once. It developed opinions.

The classroom door that had opened an inch decided an inch was cowardice and flung itself full wide. A mop bucket inside toppled over and rolled into the passage. Hester jerked round toward the sound, lost hold of the spoon brace in her other hand, and the lavatory cistern gave a liquid cough before she had touched it properly. Across from Owen, the service press yawned open to blackness. The small armor suit, offended by sudden passage on all sides, took one mincing step forward and discharged the Dungbombs wired into its greave directly into its own feet.

The smell hit half a second later.

"No," Owen said, already moving.

He reached for the classroom door. It shut under his hand, then sprang back open again as soon as he let go. The pressure in the stone thickened, spreading under the flags and up the frames. It was not one threshold. It was the whole run. Of course it was the whole run. The founder seam went uninterrupted under the corridor, and he had written into the line, not the hinge. Threshold was not a door. It was any made place where one condition passed into another: hinge, latch, visor, sluice, seat, bolt, valve. It was anything the castle understood as passage.

At the far end someone yelped. It was the younger ones. Owen turned just in time to see the disused classroom where Martha had hidden them spit its door open so hard the handle cracked the plaster. The boy with the thread-mended spectacles stumbled out clutching his sandwich. Behind him the boy in the too-big jumper slid in spilled water and nearly lost both shoes.

"Move," Martha snapped. "No, this way. This way, don't stand there looking at it."

Neville came off the stair in three strides. "Kids first. Go."

Then the toilets joined in.

Every cistern along the lavatory wall gave up on containment in the same ugly instant. Chains rattled against porcelain. Lids jumped. Brown-grey water surged over the back lip of the nearest one and slapped across the floor, carrying ink from the rigged seat with it in long purple-black fans. The stall doors slammed open and shut as if a dozen invisible people were too polite to pick one side. Somewhere behind tile a pipe knocked like somebody punching wood.

The large armor suit by the corner woke under Len's string before Len had pulled it, because the changed corridor had decided any suggestion of passage counted as enough. It lurched forward with murderous dignity, halberd lowering, and walked straight into the lavatory doorframe. The blow tore the halberd free, spun the suit sideways, and sent the cistern brace Hester had been meant to use skittering under Neville's feet.

For one impossible second the whole business was merely humiliating. It was filth, ink, armor, children in wet socks, and a corridor behaving like a drunk cupboard. One of the first-years laughed in pure fright and then burst into tears in the same breath. Hester, still on her knees, shouted, "I didn't pull it yet," as if anyone might still blame technique.

Then the second classroom door flew open and exposed the rest of the route.

Two third-years who had been waiting there with blankets and contraband bread stared into the corridor, stunned, their cover gone. One of them had flour on his sleeve from the kitchens. The other was barefoot and holding a tin of dittany against his chest like a rescued pet. Any hope of keeping the operation small vanished with the open doorway.

"Shut them," Neville said.

Owen had his wand out already. "Colloportus."

The spell came off the wand sideways, a thin spray of red sparks that stitched harmlessly across the opposite wall and knocked a framed fire notice crooked. His ordinary magic never failed in large dignified ways. It failed like this, embarrassingly, as if the wand were offended by the company it kept.

"Owen," Martha snapped.

"I know."

He dropped to one knee in the dirty water and went for the stone with his bare hand instead. A countermark might have worked if he had had a clear surface, if the corridor had been still, if First Art respected panic less than it did. The flagstones throbbed under his palm with the same sick answering pressure he had felt in the greenhouse and under the undercroft arch. There was too much motion and too many interrupted lines. His own pulse kept slipping into the shape.

He scratched the first half of a narrowing sign with his thumbnail. The service press beside him answered by swinging wider, and a rush of colder air breathed out of the dark beyond it. For a bright contemptible instant he could smell deep stone and old damp below the kitchens. The route had opened.

Martha had one arm round the crying first-year and the other shoved against the disused classroom door, trying to force it closed by body weight alone. "Neville, take Hester. Go. Go now."

Neville did not waste words on objection. He caught Hester by the sleeve, snatched the sandwich boy with his other hand, and drove them toward the fruit portrait. Len, swearing, wrestled the big armor suit by the halberd stump long enough to send it crashing into the opposite wall. The smaller decorative suit took another two furious ballet steps and planted both feet in Dungbomb sludge, where it remained vibrating with thwarted intent.

The corridor might still have been saved into something only ridiculous if Amycus had taken even thirty seconds longer.

He did not.

His boots hit the stair turn with speed enough to show he had been running. He came in half belted, robe collar crooked, one bootlace tucked into the top of his boot instead of tied. There were tiny shaving cuts on his jaw and the sweet rotten smell of drink under whatever mint he had sucked on to hide it. For one beat he simply stopped and looked.

The corridor looked back at him in sewage, ink, and cheap sabotage.

"What the fuck," he said, almost admiringly.

Then he lifted his wand and blasted the large armor suit apart.

Steel rang off stone. A greave skimmed past Owen's shoulder and hit the wall hard enough to take plaster with it. The first-year with the spectacles screamed. Martha shoved him bodily toward Neville's end and turned back because she was Martha and there were still too many bodies in the corridor.

"Run," she said to the third-years in the exposed room. "Now. Move."

Amycus saw her say it. That was enough.

He crossed the distance in four heavy strides and caught the back of her robe before she could turn. The cloth bit into her throat. She went backward, boots skidding in lavatory water, and hit the tiles on one hip so hard Owen felt it in his own teeth.

"You," Amycus said, delighted now. "There you are."

Martha twisted, got one foot under herself, and nearly made it. Amycus kicked the back of her knee. She went down again. The corridor seemed to recoil around the impact, doors still banging and water still spilling and all the while the actual human damage narrowing to a single body on stone.

Owen pushed up from the floor, filthy hand slipping on the flag. He could say it. He could say enough. Not all of it, not the Leaves, not the undercroft, but enough to pull Amycus's attention off her and onto the thing he had actually done.

He opened his mouth.

Amycus dragged Martha upright by her plait.

Owen did not speak.

* * *

Amycus held her there long enough for the corridor to quiet by degrees around them. One stall door still knocked. Water kept running somewhere behind the wall. From the other end came the muffled slap of the fruit portrait closing and the hard-breathed retreat of the children Neville had got out. The operation had shrunk to aftermath and selection, to deciding who would be left with the blame and who would not.

Martha's face had gone white under the wet. Strands of hair had pulled free from the plait and stuck across her mouth. She spat one away and looked straight at Amycus, not bravely exactly, only refusing to help him by pretending.

"Funny little mess," Amycus said. He was breathing hard through his nose. "Funny little rebels. Doors opening all over, piss everywhere, children out after curfew. You people do like making work."

He wrenched her head back by the hair and looked past her at the corridor as if the answer might be written on the wall. His gaze snagged on Owen.

"Phipps."

Owen did not move.

"Still here," Amycus said. "That was my first surprise tonight. Come on then. Use your eyes for once. Which one set it off?"

Owen's notebook felt enormous inside his jumper. The chalk paste under his nails had gone gritty with dirty water. He could still smell the colder air from the opened service press behind him, faint now under sewage and scorch. The route was still there. If he gave Amycus the whole thing, the route would go with it. The manuscript would go with it. The hidden work below the kitchens would go with it. Everything that mattered at the scale he had finally begun to understand would go with it.

Everything that mattered at the scale in front of him was in Amycus's fist.

"I don't know," Owen said.

Amycus barked a laugh. "You don't know. You were standing in the middle of it."

"I saw the armor go first."

It was the most literal answer. It was also true.

Amycus's face changed, not into belief but into the special contempt reserved for students who imagined truth could be arranged like cutlery. He let go of Martha's hair only to hit her across the mouth with the back of his hand. Her head snapped sideways. Blood appeared immediately, thin and startlingly bright against all the dirty water.

"Wrong answer," he said.

Owen flinched half a step without meaning to. Amycus saw and enjoyed it.

"Ah," he said softly. "So you do know something."

Martha made a short rough sound that might have been a laugh if it had not come through blood. "He doesn't know his own timetable half the time."

Amycus turned and drove her down to her knees with a hand on the shoulder. Broken porcelain from one of the cistern lids crackled under her robes. She sucked air through her teeth but did not cry out. He set his boot on her left hand, not hard enough yet to break anything, only enough to make the threat precise.

"Let's try the girl," he said. "Who else is here?"

"No one."

He put more weight on the hand. Owen heard the tiny ugly grind of skin against porcelain before he heard Martha's breath go wrong.

"Who else."

"No one."

Amycus looked up at Owen again as if inviting him into the lesson. The corridor light shone wetly on his shaving cuts. "See, Phipps, this is why everybody hates group work. One stupid child decides to decorate the school and then another one has to explain it with her fingers."

Owen said nothing.

He could feel the pressure of the old line under the flags, still faintly active, still waiting for a cleaner command. If he dropped to the stone again and tried to change it, he would do it under Amycus's eyes with no preparation and no guarantee except that the castle was already wounded enough to answer too loudly. If he confessed, Amycus would have the corridor and then, very quickly, anything else Owen had touched. If he did neither, Martha would be the one taken.

He chose neither loudly enough for anyone to mistake it. He simply stayed where he was.

Martha's eyes found his for one second. There was pain in them, and fury, and an exhausted disbelief that was somehow worse than either. Then Amycus hauled her up again and the look broke.

"Right," Amycus said. "You can be tomorrow's example. We've all earned that, haven't we."

He shoved her toward the stair. She caught herself on the wall with her good hand, smearing blood and dirty water over the stone. Amycus yanked her onward by the arm.

"Anybody I find out after curfew from this minute," he called over his shoulder, "can join her and bring their own teeth in a bag."

The threat ran down the corridor into the walls. Then his boots and Martha's stumbling steps receded toward the turn and were gone.

For a moment nobody moved.

The little armor suit finally settled with a metallic sigh. Water dripped from the broken cistern in fat slow taps. Owen could hear people breathing in the classroom, behind the wall, inside the whole damaged hush of the thing.

Neville came back first from the portrait passage, wet to the knees and breathing through his mouth. He took in the corridor in one sweep, ending on Owen. Len appeared behind him carrying the tin of dittany that had survived somehow. Hester, pale and furious at herself, clutched her pinned mitten with the free hand like it might keep the rest of her attached.

"The little ones are down," Neville said. He looked at the blood on the wall, then at Owen again. "Was that you?"

Owen knew better than to ask which part.

"Part of it," he said.

Len swore under his breath.

Neville's face did not change much. That was the worst of it. If he had shouted, Owen might have answered back, or at least known where the line was. Instead Neville nodded once, as if a piece on a board had moved exactly as its shape required.

"For what?" Neville asked.

Owen said, "I was opening the press."

"For us."

The opened service press behind him breathed cold from the dark.

Owen did not answer in time.

Neville looked away first. "Right."

That single word carried more rearrangement than a speech would have. He crouched by the broken cistern, snapped a bit of plaster off the floor, and pressed it to the running crack as if damage still deserved practical attention even in the middle of betrayal.

"Len, get the wall. Hester, help him. Jo can come back up for the bucket. Nobody says press, sabotage, corridor, any of it if they're spoken to." He stood and faced Owen again. "And you don't come near another job of mine if you can't tell which side of it you're on."

Martha's blood was drying already in a crooked handprint where she had caught herself.

Owen said, because it was weak and because it was true, "I didn't mean this."

Neville's mouth went tight with something flatter than anger. "No. That's the problem."

He turned away and started giving people tasks. Hester fetched rags from the classroom. Len scraped soot and dung off the armor's decorative foot with the disgust of somebody cleaning up after a dog he did not own. Jo slipped back through the portrait with a bucket and would not meet Owen's eye. The corridor filled with work, quick and low-voiced and competent.

No one asked Owen to do any of it.

He stood by the still-open press with dirty water drying on his socks and the notebook heavy under his jumper while the others mopped, lifted, wiped, hid, and repaired around him. The cold from the service dark kept touching his ankle. Somewhere down the stair Amycus shouted, and farther off than that, almost below hearing, Martha made no sound at all.

By the time the last of them went back down to the kitchens in twos and threes, nobody had asked Owen to carry so much as a bucket.


# Chapter 10

By midnight the scullery off the kitchens smelled of wet stone, old onions, and lamp oil going sharp in the heat. Owen had the Grinlow Leaves spread on an upturned flour bin because it was flatter than the table, and flatter mattered. The page he wanted would hold for perhaps six breaths if his burned right palm pinned the lower margin and the lamp sat exactly level with the nick in the opposite wall. On the seventh breath the ink went pale and the lines slid back under the vellum as if the manuscript had thought better of showing him anything.

He had been stuck there three nights.

His ash-linen notebook lay open beside it, dense with cramped tries and cross-outs. He had written that the quarter mark repeated at ninety degrees, that the thing was not stars but indexed by stars, not a calendar. He had written the last sentence four times in four different places as if changing where it sat might make it truer.

The kitchens beyond the scullery still muttered at low volume: pan bottoms ringing softly, water thrown into a basin, one of the younger elves laughing and then stopping at once when an older voice shushed him. The ordinary work of the castle went on under the occupation because somebody had to scrub the roasting tins and bank the ovens even when Amycus Carrow was upstairs teaching children to scream properly.

Footsteps came along the service passage. They were not Tallow's quick soft slap but human footsteps, cautious and tired.

Prue came in sideways through the narrow door with a roll of transparencies under one arm and a folded bundle of charts clamped to her chest. She had not bothered with gloves. She never did when paper was involved; wool caught on thin pages and drove her mad. Her fingers were pink with cold and already marked with blue ink near the nails. Over her school jumper she wore an old bottle-green cardigan with one wooden replacement button at the throat, and she worried that button between two fingers while she looked at him and the book and did not yet put anything down.

"Neville said you wanted the clear ones," she said.

"I did."

"That was his charitable summary."

Owen nodded once. "I couldn't get the projection to stay put."

She set the bundle on the bin beside the manuscript without sitting. Her spectacles were smudged at one corner. "Martha's still limping."

The sentence landed in the room and stayed there. Owen looked at the page because the page at least obeyed some kind of rule.

"I know," he said.

"Do you?"

"Yes."

She let out a breath through her nose. "Right."

Neither of them said sorry. It would have been too early for a useful apology and too late for a good one.

Tallow slipped in behind her carrying a tray with a dented kettle, three thick cups, and a plate with two heels of bread that had been toasted too long on one side. He had wrapped kitchen string around the handle of the kettle because it had cracked in January and he refused to throw it away on principle. He turned each cup so its chip faced the wall before setting it down, a small private courtesy to damaged things.

"No ink on the bread," he said. "If there is ink on the bread, Tallow is not pretending not to notice."

"Wouldn't dream of it," Prue said.

Tallow gave her a look that suggested her dreams could not be trusted around paper. His singed ear twitched. "Old stone is sharp tonight. If you two are going down, you will step where the light steps, and if one of you falls and cracks a tooth Tallow is not fetching Madam Pomfrey to explain why."

Prue turned to Owen. "Going down where?"

He had meant to say it more carefully. He had meant to ease her to the edge of it, perhaps show her the anchor page first and only then admit that the page now wanted the undercroft more than it wanted the scullery. Instead he heard himself answer in the most literal shape available.

"Below," he said. "The page won't stabilize properly anywhere younger than founder stone."

She watched him for a moment. "And this is the first time you thought to mention there was a below?"

"It's not safe in the sense people usually mean."

"Mm. Helpful distinction."

He nearly started explaining what the distinction actually was, then stopped because that was exactly the sort of answer that made her face go flat like this. Tallow poured tea to spare them from themselves. It was over-brewed and black as old nails.

"Drink first," the elf said. "Thinking goes rotten faster in the cold."

Prue took the nearest cup and sipped. Her whole mouth tightened. "This is vile."

"It is hot," Tallow said. "Not every useful thing needs charm."

That almost got a smile out of her. Almost.

Owen drank because it was there. The tea scalded the back of his throat and made his empty stomach wake up. He had not realized until then that he was shaking a little. Lack of sleep or too much of the book, impossible to tell.
