Prue unrolled her transparencies across the flour bin. Thin sheets of smoked glass and marked parchment clicked softly together. She had copied sky grids, declination lines, and an old equatorial projection in a hand so neat it looked engraved until one noticed the cramped corrections at the corners. On top of that she laid a folded tracing of the world, oceans and coastlines reduced to the minimum a pattern needed. She smoothed it flat with the side of her wrist to avoid leaving fingerprints.

"You said projection," she said. "Show me exactly where it slips."

He put his burned palm to the lower margin. The skin there was always hotter than the rest of him now, as if the coffer had left a coal under it. Lines came up through the vellum in a slow black seep: circles within circles, radial marks, four larger sigils that refused at first to mean anything except repetition. Prue leaned in until her shoulder brushed his and held one smoked sheet above the page. The ink steadied. He felt her freeze, not from fright exactly but from the sudden technical greed of seeing a system begin to answer.

"There," he said quietly. "If the third ring is treated as sky, not geometry."

"It isn't sky," she said at once. "Or not only sky. Hang on."

She shifted the transparency left, then back, then rotated it by the width of two fingernails. The marks on the page snapped into a different relation. What had looked like decorative hatching at the outer rim became latitude bands. The inner knots opened into four anchor points set around a sphere that had not been drawn as a sphere because the manuscript assumed the reader already knew how to unfold it.

Owen had known, roughly, that the table was large. He had not known it was world-sized until Prue's hand made the page admit it.

"Oh," she said.

It was not an exclamation. It was the sound of somebody stepping off a stair she had expected to be there.

The ink went pale again. Owen swore under his breath.

"No, wait," Prue said. "Again."

They did it again, and again. Each time the image held a little longer, enough for her to mark one relation on the tracing, enough for him to copy terms that he could half-parse and half only feel in his teeth: holdfast, veil hinge, southern return, school not by name but by category, schola, rendered through the manuscript's logic as a place where repetition and concentration had been built to stack.

By the fifth attempt his wrist ached and Prue's curls had escaped their tie and were catching lamp glow at the edge of his sight. She smelled of cold wool, library dust, and a cheap soap that left almost no scent at all. The closeness was not romance. It was concentration. It was what happened when there was only one warm patch of air in a room and both of them needed to occupy it.

"This isn't enough," Owen said. "It keeps degrading."

Prue did not look up from the page. "Then perhaps your mysterious below stops being mysterious now."

Tallow had been standing with his tray like a witness called to the wrong hearing. At that he clicked his tongue, gathered the kettle, and jerked his head toward the dark service slit behind the scullery shelves.

"Bring the lamp," he said. "And don't touch the wall on the narrow turn unless you enjoy blisters in odd shapes."

The passage was too small for dignity. Owen went first with the lamp, the Grinlow Leaves wrapped in oilcloth under his arm and his notebook tucked into his waistband. Prue came after, carrying the transparencies high and flat as if she were transporting surgical instruments through a drain. Tallow led them far enough to show the safe places and then stopped at the last low arch where the air changed.

It always changed there. The warmth of the kitchens fell off, and the old work began. Tooth-pressure gathered behind Owen's molars. The stone seemed to thicken the sound of their breathing until each inhale came back at them from the walls.

Tallow set the kettle down at his feet and rubbed his damp hands hard on his apron. "Further is yours," he said. The formality had come into his voice, which meant he was frightened enough to use manners as a shield. "Tallow will wait where the house still feels like itself."

Prue looked from him to the dark stair beyond. "Does it hurt you?"

He gave a little offended shrug. "Everything hurts somebody if it gets old enough."

Then, less tidily: "It is worse for elves. Mind the third step. It is hollow in the middle."

That, more than any warning, convinced her. She nodded once. "All right."

Owen took her down the stair.

The route curled through the castle's forgotten structure rather than its planned one: under a laundry flue warm as a throat, along a ledge where the wall sweated black, through a slit door whose hinges had fused long ago and now opened only because he had learned where the founder stone had cracked around the pin. Prue did not complain. She touched the places he told her to touch and ducked where he ducked. Once, when the ceiling dropped lower than he remembered, she caught the back of his robe to stop him from moving on too fast.

"You could have mentioned the crawling," she said.

"I forgot that bit."

"That's not reassuring."

The undercroft opened abruptly after all that constriction. The chamber was not large, but after the passage it felt like a lung. Damp mineral cold came off the floor. The anchor stone sat in its shallow setting at the center, not dramatic except to anyone who knew what it was: a broad disk of dark founder stone with lines incised so fine they vanished until light hit sideways. The star-table around it had been cut directly into the floor, rings and spokes and notation worn by age but not by use, because almost nobody had stood here for centuries.

Prue stopped just inside the threshold.

The old-work pressure reached her then. He saw it in the minute way her mouth changed shape behind her hand, in the swallow she had to force.

"Is it meant to feel like that?" she asked.

"Yes."

"Good. I hate surprises when they're physical."

He set the lamp on a dry shelf of stone and knelt by the anchor. The chamber answered the manuscript before he touched it. The oilcloth twitched once in his hands, eager and repellent at the same time.

"Don't stand directly on the inner ring," he said. "It isn't dangerous exactly, but you'll lose the thread."

"That sounds very much like danger in a cardigan."

"I know what I mean."

"I know you do."

This time the flatness in her voice had edges.

He unwrapped the Leaves and laid the page across the anchor stone. The effect was immediate. Ink rose black and sure through the vellum, not seeping now but taking its proper lines as if it had been waiting all evening to stop pretending. Prue let out a quiet breath and dropped to her knees opposite him, too interested to bother staying offended for one second longer than interest required.

They worked.

That was the chapter's real weather: not speech but labor. Owen held the page and translated what Meridian notation he could. Prue tested each relation against her grids, her world tracing, and the old astronomy tables she had copied so many times at school that she now trusted the shapes more than she trusted most people. When one approach failed she did not dramatize it. She simply pushed her spectacles up with the back of her wrist, crossed out the line, and tried the next premise. Owen kept narrowing his claims in the notebook and then crossing out the narrowing because the stone made it obvious when he was being cowardly with a conclusion.

The four anchor points came first as pattern, then as place.

One lay under Hogwarts, or rather under the region Hogwarts belonged to in the older system, the northwestern school holdfast built into the castle's foundations and disguised for centuries beneath all the smaller magic that had crusted over it. Opposite it, at a rough southern turn of the globe, another sat under a red inland waste so broad the manuscript marked it with heat bands instead of names. A third held in a high western spine where the air, according to the note Prue finally coaxed clear, bit water into stone. The last was pinned beneath an old salt plateau in the great interior of Asia, all dryness and glare even in the reduced language of the table.

Prue traced each point onto her world sheet with ink made thin by cold. "Four," she said, mostly to herself. "Not a web. More like a tent with the whole planet hanging in it."

"A quadrant hold," Owen said. "Or that's the nearest English to it."

"Nearest isn't the same as right."

"No."

"Still. Tent will do for tonight."

He liked that she could be exact without pretending exactness was free.

When they had the points, the stress marks began to make sense. The outer rings were not decorative. They were load records, not dates in any ordinary form but recurrence intervals, surge tolerances, places where the field had brightened and then failed to settle. Prue found the rhythm first because she had spent half her life making dots and arcs mean time.

"Look," she said, tapping a cluster of fine slashes around the Hogwarts point. "This isn't random deterioration. It jumps. Holds. Jumps again. Closer together later."

Owen bent over the page until his nose almost touched it. The chamber smelled of old mineral water and hot oil from the lamp. He could hear the castle above only as occasional thick thuds through many yards of stone, like furniture being shoved in another house.

"Those are local events," he said. "High-force loads."

"Meaning what, exactly?"

"Curses. Punishment magic. Anything heavy enough to strike the old structure rather than just the rooms built on top of it. Repeatedly."

Prue's finger moved to a darkened notch deeper than the rest. "And this."

He knew that one. It marked late November, the punishment scene when Amycus threw force across a row of students while Owen, desperate and half-ill, changed contact just enough to make curses skid. He had felt the castle answer then like a bell hit in the middle.

"That was me as well," he said.

Her head came up. "As well."

"Not the punishment. The amendment. It leaves a scar. Every successful one does."

"You have been cutting the same place you're trying to mend."

"Shallowly."

"That's not very comforting, Owen."

"I didn't say it to be comforting."

She looked at him for a long second, then went back to the page because the page was, for the moment, the only thing either of them could make move. They marked the other nodes. The western spine showed long old damage and recent brightening. The red inland anchor pulsed weakly but steady, a bad sign because weak and steady suggested attrition rather than a single wound. The salt plateau had a fracture pattern like dried glaze.

Hogwarts was worse than any of them.

It was not merely brightest. It was agitated. Each fresh load sent hairline spidering outward from the point, then drew back. It was a site being prodded awake by brutality, a school turned signal flare by a regime too stupid to know what its own violence was doing to the stone under its boots.

Prue sat back on her heels and scrubbed her hands over her face. There was ink on the side of her wrist now and a black half-moon near her jaw where she had touched herself without noticing.

"They're doing this," she said quietly.

"Yes."

"All year."

"Yes."

Another thud came through the chamber, heavier than the others. A shout followed, too muffled to make words of, only human shape and distress. On the page the Hogwarts point darkened and threw out one more fine line.

Prue stared at it. "That just answered something upstairs."

"Probably."

"Probably."

Owen looked at the mark. "Yes."

She laughed once, without humor. "You only go vague when it's awful."

He almost corrected the statement because it was not always true. Then he let it pass because it was true often enough to count.

They went on. He needed the full geometry now, not merely the fact of four anchors. The table around the Hogwarts point carried subsidiary notation, local clauses or route conditions or repair permissions; he could not yet tell which. Prue saw the same set of nested signs and shifted closer to catch the lamplight better. Their knees knocked. Neither moved away. Her left shoulder settled against his for balance while she copied a run of symbols into the margin of his notebook. The contact was practical and not practical. It warmed a strip of him he had not known was cold.

"This one repeats after every surge mark," she murmured. "Not all four nodes, only here."

Owen traced the phrase with the nail of his left thumb. "Hostile distinction. No, hang on. Directed force within bound institution."

"That's very school, however you translate it."

"Yes."

"Can it be used?"

He should have hedged. He should have said he did not know yet, which was true. He should have said perhaps, in theory, under conditions so narrow they might never occur, which was also true. Instead the stone and the sleeplessness and the relief of finally not working alone made him answer the question she had actually asked.

"Almost certainly," he said.

She stopped writing.

The silence after that was small but exact.

"Used for what?" she said.

Owen kept his eyes on the page. The notation sat there indifferent to human proportions. "Local redirection, maybe. Suppression. If the clause means what I think it means, the anchor can distinguish between ordinary spellwork and hostile wand force inside the school's bounds."

"Inside the school?"

"Yes."

"You mean detentions?"

"Not only."

"Corridors. Raids. Carrow turning a class into a butcher's yard."

"Potentially."

She took her shoulder off his. The warmth went with it.

"And if you can do that," she said, "if you get far enough into this to do that, you could stop them."

He heard the trap and disliked that he heard it as a trap when she was only following the line where it went. "Not cleanly."

"That was not my question."

"No working like that is clean. It would draw on the same node. It would scar it further unless the larger repair happened first or at the same time, and I can't do both blind."

"So if somebody is being hurt upstairs and this helps, you still might not use it."

There it was. Plain enough now that even he could not pretend the question meant something else.

He put his left hand flat on the stone to steady himself against a fresh pulse of tooth-pressure. "If using it locally risks the whole field, I can't promise that."

Prue did not speak.

He went on because once he had started he could feel the argument building in the only order that made sense to him, and sense had always been the thing he trusted when other people were looking at him as if he had gone wrong.

"There are four anchors left doing the work of whatever system used to cover the planet. One of them is under this school and it's already fraying from every bit of heavy magic thrown through it. If I spend what little purchase there is on one corridor, or one detention, or even ten of them, and the larger failure widens because of that, then I've solved the nearest thing and failed the actual problem."

He looked up then, because the sentence sounded colder aloud than it had in his head and he needed to see whether that was only tone.

It was not only tone.

Prue had gone very still. It was not dramatic stillness. It was the opposite: the kind that came when a person stopped wasting movement on any hope of hearing something better in a second attempt.

"The nearest thing," she repeated.

"I know how it sounds."

"Do you?"

"Yes."

"I don't think you do."

The chamber gave another blunt thud from somewhere above. Neither of them looked at the ceiling this time.

Prue took off her spectacles and pinched the bridge of her nose. Without them her face looked younger and more tired, the dark hollows under her eyes less disguised. "Let's not make this prettier than it is," she said. "You're saying there may come a point where you can stop what's being done here, to actual people, and you'll stand there weighing that against the larger repair as if we're figures in the margin."

"That's not fair."

"Isn't it?"

"No. Because I'm one of the figures in the margin. It's not contempt. It's scale."

She gave a short breath that might have been almost a laugh if anything in the room had been funny. "Those aren't different enough for comfort."

He wanted to tell her she was compressing the logic badly. He wanted to say that of course local suffering mattered, that only an idiot or a monster would claim otherwise. But the stone was under his hand and the page was open and the proof of the larger danger sat between them in black lines, and he could not make himself lie in either direction.

"I don't know what promise you want from me," he said at last.

"A human one."

He had no answer to that because the answer, if he gave the honest version, was that human promises were exactly what the work had started feeling too small to govern.

Prue put her spectacles back on. Her fingers shook once, from cold or anger or both. When she spoke again her voice had gone careful, which was worse than shouting.

"All right," she said. "Then I understand."

He knew enough to hear the damage in that.

She pulled the notebook back toward them. "This mark here. The subsidiary ring. It's still repeating whether or not I approve of you, so move your hand a fraction left and let's finish the page."

It was a brutal kindness, or perhaps only discipline. Either way he obeyed.

They finished the page.

The last hours before dawn thinned into routine: test, copy, verify, correct. Prue's distrust did not make her less exact. If anything it made her quieter and better. She cross-referenced the subsidiary ring to the load intervals and proved that Hogwarts was not only one anchor among four but the most immediately unstable because the school had become a machine for concentrated magical violence. Owen charted the stress sequence into his notebook until even he could see the relation at a glance. There were four anchors: three distant failures under ice-biting height, red desert heat, and salt glare, and one failing school node in Scotland, brightening every time the occupation leaned harder on children or he himself touched the First Art without enough control.

By the end they had something legible where there had been only the manuscript's spiteful half-disclosures: a world tracing pinned under Prue's transparencies, the four anchor points marked in black, the Hogwarts node webbed with fresh stress lines, the subsidiary local clause copied twice in case one version turned out to be wishful reading. The page on the stone had begun to fade again with the coming light. Perhaps dawn weakened it. Perhaps the chamber had merely given what it meant to give for one night.

Owen flexed his right hand and felt the burn pulse back at him.

Prue gathered the transparencies one by one. Her movements were neat from habit, not calm. She paused only once, at the threshold, and looked behind her toward the route they had come by as if fixing it in memory against a future need she did not yet want to name.

"I can find my own way back to the kitchens," she said.

"You haven't done it before."

"I do now."

He stood, slower than he meant to. "Prue."

She did look at him then. There was still care in her face, which made the rest of it worse.

"I know," she said. "The problem is that I know."

Then she took the lamp and went into the passage, leaving him with the anchor stone, the completed map, and the first method he had trusted enough to build on. The undercroft felt larger after she left, and emptier in a way that had nothing to do with sound. Above him the castle carried on being a school and a prison and, now that he had the page to prove it, one of four failing holds between the world and whatever hunted brightness in the dark. He put his hand over the marked Hogwarts point on the tracing until the ink warmed under his skin and wished, uselessly, that the true arithmetic had come with any smaller answer.


# Chapter 11

By the time the actual parchment reached him, Owen had already been told twice that the Headmaster wanted him.

The first warning came from a narrow portrait on the second-floor landing, a witch in tarnished blue silk whose painted pearl earring had been scratched off years ago so that one bare ear looked oddly accusatory. She had been darning a stocking in her frame with short offended jabs. As Owen came up from the cellar after dawn, satchel dragging at his shoulder and the whole night with Prue still caked under his eyes, she looked up and said, "Mr Phipps. Do stop behaving as if staircases can protect you. The Headmaster has sent for you."

He had kept walking because portraits repeated things for pleasure and because he still had the Grinlow Leaves in his satchel, wrapped under his spare shirt beside the ash-linen notebook. He had not trusted the manuscript to the dormitory after the night's work, not with the map of four failing anchors still half alive in his head and Prue's last, flat silence following him down the corridor more cleanly than anything she might have said.

The second warning came outside the Great Hall. Edith Wren, a Hufflepuff sixth-year with a voice made papery by a winter cough, caught his sleeve as he went in. Her badge had been sewn back on with blue thread because she lost pins constantly and refused to admit it.

"Filch was asking after you before breakfast," she said. "And one of the Slytherin prefects. Proper asking, not corridor gossip."

"Right," Owen said.

Edith let go of his sleeve and studied his face for a second too long. "You look dreadful."

"Didn't sleep."

"That'll help."

She moved on, coughing into her fist. Owen stood one beat longer in the doorway while the Hall noise struck him full on: plates, chairs, the soft public thunder of a few hundred people pretending school still had a shape. Then he went to the Hufflepuff table because not going would only have made him more visible.

The parchment waited under his spoon.

It was folded once, no seal, only his name written in a severe hand that managed to look disappointed by the existence of ink. His bowl of porridge steamed around it. Somebody had placed it there after he sat down or before he arrived; either possibility was bad in a different direction.

Across from him, Martha looked from his face to the note without pausing in the work of buttering bread. Two of her knuckles were split and yellowed with ointment. She had started favoring her left leg after the corridor fiasco, not enough to call it a limp if one was being polite, more than enough to notice if one knew her old stride.

"Open it," she said.

Owen unfolded the parchment.

`Mr Phipps will attend the Headmaster immediately.`

Nothing else. No threat, no explanation, only the command and the signature below it: `S. Snape`.

Martha tore her bread in half with more force than necessary. "Well."

"Yes," Owen said.

"If you're going to say you don't know what it's about, don't. Nobody's that unlucky by accident."

"I don't know which thing it's about," he said before he could stop himself.

That got a short look out of her, not surprised exactly, more disgusted that he had managed to confirm there were multiple candidates. Three places down the bench a fourth-year stopped talking to his friend and glanced over. At the Ravenclaw table Prue was bent over her tea as if it required classification. One lens of her spectacles was clouded where she had wiped it badly on her cuff. She did not look up.

Owen folded the note smaller and slid it into his pocket. His satchel strap had wound itself round his ankle under the bench. He thought, briefly and very stupidly, of leaving it there. But the table was full, people moving in and out, Amycus somewhere in the middle aisle with his mug and his appetite for interruption. There was no route back to the cellar that did not pass other eyes first. The manuscript was safer on him than off him, which was another way of saying there was no safe version at all.

Martha had already started on her porridge. "Are you going to eat?"

"Not now."

"Fine."

She did not tell him good luck. He did not wait for her to.

The walk to the office should have been long enough to think. It was not. Everything in the castle seemed arranged to remove thought and leave only awareness.

Two suits of armour stood outside the Hall doors. Usually they watched the room with the bored vacancy of school fixtures. This morning one of them had its visor angled fractionally down, enough that Owen felt inspected while he passed. On the third-floor landing a pair of Slytherin girls broke off talking when they saw him and stepped aside too quickly, as if proximity itself might make them part of whatever was happening. A staircase that normally preferred dithering students over punctuality settled under his feet at once and carried him straight upward without the usual malicious pause. He was so tired that each small convenience felt worse than obstruction would have done.

His right palm had been hot since dawn. Not the clean old burn from the coffer now, but the newer cross-lines that had risen after corridor work and undercroft strain. He kept the hand in his pocket and the pocket clenched.

At the headmaster's staircase the stone guardian admitted him before he had fully stopped. The concealed door folded back on a draft of colder air, and the spiral stair began to turn with the faint grinding sound of old machinery deciding it had waited long enough.

Owen put one hand on the rail and rode upward with the satchel pulling his shoulder crooked. He had imagined, in the worst moments since breakfast, that he might be sick on the stair. Instead he was too frightened for that. Fear had gone dry on him somewhere between the portrait and the Hall. It sat now as a series of practical recognitions. The manuscript was in the bag. His wand was unreliable. If Snape searched him, the rest became fast. If Snape did not search him, that might be worse.

At the top, the office door stood open.

He had never been inside under Snape. The room still had the circular authority of the place under Dumbledore, but whatever softness had once kept it from feeling administrative had been pared away. The windows let in a washed grey morning. Most of the silver instruments on the side tables had gone still, as if their owners had been told not to chatter. Several portraits of dead headmasters appeared to be sleeping with theatrical innocence. Two others were plainly listening. The air smelled of beeswax, cold ash, and old cloth that had been shut away from daylight too often.

Snape stood beside the desk, one hand on a stack of parchments he was not reading. He looked more tired than Owen had ever seen him at a distance, which was to say he looked exactly as severe as usual with a little more of the skull showing through. His black hair hung straight and damp at the temples, as if he had come in from weather that only he could detect.

"Close the door, Mr Phipps."

Owen did.

"Put your satchel on the chair."

The words landed low and clean. Owen obeyed because there was no form of hesitation that improved them. He set the bag on the hard wooden chair opposite the desk and sat nowhere near it.

Snape watched the movement. "You may stop looking at it. If confiscation had been my immediate object, you would not still be carrying it."

The line went through Owen like a nail drawn down glass. He kept his face as blank as he could manage, which was not very blank at all this tired.

Snape went on as if he had merely corrected posture. "Within the last several months this school has acquired a greenhouse with impaired ideas about closure, a punishment platform that briefly mistook contact for permission, and fresh stress marks in wards old enough to predate standardized wand instruction. Your own spellwork has degraded in parallel. Shall we continue pretending these are separate accidents."

It was not phrased as a question. Owen answered anyway.

"Things have gone wrong," he said.

"Yes." Snape's mouth moved by less than a smile. "That is what I have just summarized."

Owen looked at the desk because looking at Snape made him too aware of the satchel at his side. The desk surface was almost bare. A school ledger, a quill, two sealed memoranda, and a fine grey film of dust in the carved groove at the back where even house-elves could not be bothered with ceremonial furniture this year.

"I haven't done anything to the office," he said, and heard at once how childish it sounded.

Snape's eyes narrowed not with anger but with the concentration of a man finding a smaller and more exact disappointment than the one he had expected. "No. Fortunately for both of us, you have so far confined yourself to less politically complicated sections of the building."

He came round the side of the desk, not quickly. Owen was suddenly aware of how little the room offered in the way of useful movement. The portraits, the door, the stair below it, the still instruments, all of it had the same quality as the summons had carried up the corridor: surveillance that no longer needed to hide it was surveillance.

"Your right hand," Snape said.

Owen did not move.

"Mr Phipps."

He took the hand out of his pocket and laid it palm up on his knee. The old coffer burn crossed the center in its dark glossy ribbon. Over it and off it ran the newer red-brown branches, finer and less regular, as if some second hand had tried to write through the first in a language skin was unsuited to.

Snape did not touch. He bent slightly, studying the palm with the same expression he might have given a contaminated potion sample.

"A primary brand," he said after a moment, mostly to himself. "Then load response. Crude, but not random." He straightened. "What you have been using is older than wand discipline and less forgiving. Older scholarship usually calls it amendment."

The word hit harder than any shouted accusation could have done. Owen had not realized, not really, how much of his courage depended on the possibility that no one else in Hogwarts possessed even the right nouns.

"I don't know what you mean," he said.

Snape looked at him with frank contempt. "Then your standards for lying have worsened along with your wandwork."

Owen shut his hand again.

Snape returned to the desk but did not sit. "Let me save us both time. I do not know which manuscript, table, ruin, or founder-era leftover you have put your hands on. If I did, this conversation would be shorter and your morning markedly worse. I do, however, know the traces left by pre-wand amendment traditions when they are practiced by someone with more access than discipline."

Owen's mouth had gone dry enough that swallowing felt public. "You've seen this before."

"I have read around it," Snape said. "Which is not the same thing and should not reassure you."

The room seemed to contract by a measurable amount. One of the supposedly sleeping portraits shifted under its painted blankets and then went still again.

Snape's voice lost a degree of its classroom edge and became colder for that loss. "Ordinary spellwork applies force within agreed boundaries. Amendment does not. It asks a prior question. What counts as shut. What counts as contact. What belongs within a wall and what does not. The older notes sometimes describe these as questions of the world." He let the phrase sit. "The world's chief defect, in this context, is that it answers literally."

Owen heard the greenhouse latch, the punishment dais, the stupid comic burst of doors and toilets and armor in the corridor sabotage, all of them suddenly classified inside a vocabulary that was not his alone.

"I wasn't trying to do any of that," he said.

"Of course you were not. Intent is often the least interesting part of the damage."

Owen looked up despite himself. "If you know this much, why haven't you taken it."

Snape's face altered by almost nothing. It was enough.

"An admirably direct question," he said. "For you." He turned one parchment over, though there was nothing on the back worth seeing. "Because the Carrows notice broken children, missing food, and overt insubordination. They do not notice stress in old ward seams unless somebody educates them. I prefer to preserve that ignorance."

The answer was not an answer. It admitted strategy and nothing underneath. Owen tried another angle.

"So you want me to stop."

"I want you," Snape said, "not to make the school structurally more ridiculous than it already is."

For a second Owen almost laughed, not because the line was funny but because it struck the exact scale at which he had been trying and failing to think since the undercroft. School, planetary masking ward, Carrows, manuscript: all of it had been refusing proportion. Snape had reached into the mess and selected a sentence small enough to function.

"I can't promise that," Owen said before good sense caught up.

Snape's head tilted. "No. I had not expected promise from you. Only information, which I am also apparently not to have."

That was a mistake, Owen knew it while making it, but fear had gone on hardening and had taken some of the ordinary manners with it.

"If you already know the traces," he said, "then you know enough without the source."

One of the portraits gave a tiny sound that could have been a smothered cough or a laugh. Snape did not take his eyes off Owen.

"Do not mistake my current ignorance for your safety," he said.

"I'm not."

"Good."

He moved to the window and looked out over the grounds, though the tone of the room did not suggest scenery had much to do with it. From where Owen sat he could see only a slice of pale lawn and the black lines of wet branches.

"There is another matter," Snape said.

Owen waited.

"Hogwarts is not inert stone. The founders wrote conditions into the place because they trusted architecture more than descendants. Stairs, thresholds, classroom limits, permissions, exclusions, old service seams, all of it. Most of those conditions have been layered over by centuries of ordinary magic and adolescent misuse. They are still there." He turned back. "If you keep worrying the same old work, the castle will not remain scenery to your experiments. It will begin answering on its own terms."

Owen felt the undercroft in his teeth again, the metal-spoon tapping through the floor, the sense of the building as machinery instead of backdrop.

"What's that mean."

"It means," Snape said, each word clipped clean, "that a school may decide what counts as inside. Or safe. Or hostile. Or admitted. It means doors may become arguments. Corridors may remember the wrong rule. Rooms may begin completing your imprecisions in ways you did not intend and cannot reverse. The castle is a fixed frame large enough to mistake for a world if one is young and foolish. Do not ask it questions you cannot survive having answered."

The warning went through Owen more coldly than the threat had. For the first time since he entered the office he forgot the satchel for a full second.

"You know about the undercroft," he said.

Snape's expression shut at once. "I know enough to recognize when the school has acquired a new source of strain below the public level. Do not flatter yourself by assuming omniscience where attention suffices."

There it was again, the gap. He knew traces, not site. He knew tradition, not scale. He had vocabulary, not the whole map. Owen did not have the luxury of relief; even partial knowledge in Snape's hands felt like standing near a blade while someone else tested its weight.

"Who else have you told," Snape said.

Owen thought of Prue's face over the table, ink on her cuffs, the hurt in her silence when he would not promise human use for human danger. He thought of Martha. Neville. The Room in September. He answered the most literal version available.

"No one who can do anything with it without me."

Snape's gaze sharpened. "That was not reassurance either."

"It wasn't meant to be."

For the first time something almost like genuine irritation crossed Snape's face. "No. I see you have reached the age at which boys confuse exclusivity with competence."

He came back to the desk and finally sat, which somehow increased rather than reduced the pressure in the room. Seated, he looked less like an administrator and more like a knife somebody had remembered to fold.

"Listen carefully," he said. "If Amycus or his sister develop curiosity about your hand, your wand, or the bits of corridor damage that coincide suspiciously with your movements, your life will become shorter and louder. If you are foolish enough to continue this work, keep it off public routes. Keep it off moving architecture. Keep it away from children who have already got sufficient cause to scream. And if the building starts answering before you have finished asking, you will stop. Immediately. I will know the difference."

Owen's throat felt lined with grit. "How."

Snape looked at him as if the question had failed an examination before it was fully set down. "Because I am Headmaster," he said. "Because every damaged ward in this place eventually reports as inconvenience to someone. Because there are very few categories of disaster left to surprise me."

The last sentence was flatter than the rest. It did not invite sympathy. It did not even make sense as confession. It only sat there as fact, a hard little object nobody touched.

Owen heard himself ask, "Are you going to tell anyone."

Snape's answer came without delay. "No useful person."

Again it was not quite an answer, or only the answer of a man who had arranged his whole year around speaking at slants. Owen had no way to test it and no power to require a straighter version.

Snape picked up one of the sealed memoranda and set it down again. It was dismissal, almost.

"You may go, Mr Phipps."

Owen stood.

"And Mr Phipps."

He stopped with the satchel strap in his hand.

"Whatever you are carrying," Snape said, "the fact that it has chosen a fifteen-year-old boy as its current custodian should not be taken as endorsement by history, providence, or this office."

There was no reply to that that was not either stupid or adolescent in a way Owen could not afford just then. He lifted the satchel, felt its familiar bad weight drag at his shoulder, and left.

The spiral stair carried him down too smoothly. By the time he reached the corridor his knees had started shaking with a delay that felt insulting.

He had walked into the office with the manuscript, the notebook, and every obvious fear laid out in advance. He had walked out with the manuscript and the notebook still in his possession. The fear had altered shape instead of lessening. It had picked up detail.

* * *

By supper the meeting had gone common.

Nobody came up and said so. That would have required a level of leisure the year did not provide. But the knowledge moved through the school in the way useful dirt always did: under doors, through lines, across tables, inside jobs people were already doing with their hands.

A second-year Ravenclaw who normally borrowed ink from anyone looked at Owen's bottle in Charms and asked the person two seats over instead. On the stairs after lunch two Gryffindor boys stopped a murmured conversation the moment he rounded the bend, one of them crushing a folded scrap of parchment into his fist so fast the edges cut his palm. In the library Madam Pince took back a stack of indexes from him without comment and then, after a beat, locked the damaged north bay with a key rather than the usual charm. That one hurt for reasons too particular to explain.

At the Hufflepuff table the gap around him was only half a seat wider than usual. It still registered. Martha sat farther down with two younger girls and a loaf she was slicing too thin because that was the only way there would be enough. She did not look up once while he ate.

Prue was absent entirely. Owen found himself checking the Ravenclaw table three times anyway.

Halfway through the meal a first-year Gryffindor stopped beside him with a folded note. She had sewn stars in white thread onto the frayed edge of her cuff, uneven little five-pointed things that made the school robe look briefly like something she had chosen.

"Neville says after," she muttered.

"Where."

"Barnabas tapestry."

She put the note down and went before he could ask anything else. The paper was blank inside. It did not need words.

He knew, walking there after supper, that the worst part would not be accusation. Accusation could be answered, even badly. The worst part would be arithmetic.

Neville had chosen a disused classroom off the seventh-floor turn, not the Room itself. That was sensible. The corridor smelled of damp stone and cabbage from somewhere below. When Owen pushed the door open, Neville was inside with a crate of pilfered supplies on the desk: bandages, soap, three apples, and a jar of peppermints that had probably been taken from some office meant to keep them for guests. Martha stood by the windowsill tearing an old sheet into strips with both hands and her teeth when the fabric refused the line. She kept working when he came in.

Neville shut the door behind him.

"How long were you with him," he said.

There was no greeting and no theatrics. Owen almost preferred that.

"I didn't time it."

"Long enough that people saw you go in and come out."

"I was summoned."

"Yes," Neville said. "I know how offices work."

Martha ripped another strip and wound it into a roll. "Shut up and answer him properly."

Owen looked from one to the other. Martha's plait had come half out and been redone in a hurry. There was dried soap at one wrist where she had clearly tried to wash something off and been called away before finishing. Neville looked as if he had slept in sections no longer than ten minutes each for a month. A bruise had worked out along his jaw in yellow and green. He still stood with the steadiness of somebody forcing the room to borrow it from him.

"He'd noticed damage in the wards," Owen said. "And my hand."

Neville held out a hand. "Let's see."

Owen hesitated, then showed him. Neville did not touch either. He just looked, jaw tightening once.

"That from Snape."

"No."

"From your private project."

"Yes."

Martha snorted softly without amusement. "At least we're saying it plain now."

Neville's eyes stayed on the hand a second longer, then moved to Owen's face. "What did he want."

There it was, the question with no good answer.

"He knows some of it isn't ordinary magic," Owen said. "Old stuff. Older than wands, or older than how we use them. He knows enough vocabulary to recognize traces."

"What traces."

"Stress marks in the castle wards. Places things went wrong."

Martha dropped a bandage roll into the crate. "Places you made them go wrong."

"Not always on purpose."

"I know," she said. "That's one of the less comforting bits."

Neville took that in and let it sit. "Did you tell him anything he didn't know already."

Owen thought of the undercroft, the anchor stone, the four dim nodes on the table. Thought of Snape saying manuscript and not book, old work and not system, enough but not all. He answered carefully.

"Not much."

"Not much isn't a measure."

"No locations. No other names. Nothing that lets him use it."

Neville's face did not change. "Use what."

Owen knew at once he had stepped in it.

"The work," he said. "Whatever the old system is."

"Right." Neville rested both hands on the desk. "So here's the problem. You've got some hidden bit of magic you've been keeping from everyone. It leaves marks on you and on the building. Snape notices, calls you in private, and you come out with your bag still on your shoulder and no blood on your face. Then you tell me not to worry because he can't use it. Do you hear how that sounds."

"I didn't say don't worry."

"You didn't need to."

There was a short silence. From somewhere down the corridor came running feet, then laughter cut off too quickly. Martha kept tearing sheet into lengths and winding them, tear, pull, fold, wind, as if the work licensed her not to look directly at him.

Owen tried again because the alternative was standing there and watching the arithmetic close.

"He warned me," he said. "About the castle. If old work gets pushed too hard, the place can start answering on its own. Doors, rooms, rules. He knows that part."

Neville's expression stayed flat, but something in it cooled another degree. "And you thought this helped your case."

"It means he's dangerous."

"He was dangerous before breakfast."

"Not like this."

"Fine." Neville's voice sharpened for the first time, not louder, just cleaner. "Then he's dangerous in one more direction. What exactly do you want me to do with that."

Owen opened his mouth and had nothing except the wrong scale again, the same failure as the Room months earlier, only now with worse evidence and less trust.

Martha supplied it before he could.

"He wants us to understand the special horrible thing on top of the ordinary horrible thing," she said. She still was not looking at him. "And maybe thank him for the update."

"That isn't fair."

She turned then. "No. It isn't. None of this is."

The plainness of it shut him up better than shouting would have done.

Neville straightened from the desk. "Listen. I don't know whether Snape's using you, watching you, or just cataloguing one more school disaster for later. Maybe you don't know either. What I do know is that I can't have someone in planning who goes in and out of his office on private business and then tells me not much when I ask what happened."

"It wasn't private business. I was hauled in."

"That doesn't make it safer."

"I didn't choose it."

"You chose everything before it."

That landed. Not because it was entirely fair, not because it accounted for every turn the year had taken, but because it was near enough to true that arguing over percentages would only make Owen sound exactly like himself at his worst.

He heard the thin scrape of his own breath and hated it. "So what. You're throwing me out."

"I'm keeping you off routes, meetings, and message work until I know what this is." Neville nodded once toward the satchel at Owen's side. "And until I know whether Snape does."

"He doesn't."

"You don't get to certify that."

Martha dropped the last roll of bandage into the crate and wiped her hands on her skirt. "You really don't."

Owen looked at her. "You think I told him."

"I think you keep making your own mess and then act surprised when the rest of us are standing in it." Her voice was tired rather than hot now, which hurt more. "You came out of Snape's office carrying the same bag you went in with. Good for you. From where anybody else is standing, that looks filthy."

He wanted to say he had been protecting them by not naming Prue, by not naming the undercroft, by not dragging anyone else into Snape's vocabulary. He wanted to say the work under the school had become large enough to deform every ordinary decision around it. He wanted, stupidly, to say that Snape had not taken the manuscript, as if that constituted innocence instead of one more problem.

None of those sentences belonged in Neville's room, with the apples on the desk and the torn sheets and the smell of soap. They were all the wrong scale again.

So he said, "Right."

Neville rubbed a hand over his face. "If you need somewhere to sleep, you still know where the room is. If there's immediate danger to students and you know it, you tell me. Quickly. Plainly. Beyond that, stay out of the work."

"Your work," Owen said, and heard the pettiness too late.

Neville dropped his hand. "Yes," he said. "Mine, because somebody has to keep count."

No one spoke for a moment after that.

Then Martha picked up the crate. "I've got to get these downstairs."

Neville moved to open the door for her. She went out without brushing past Owen, though the room was small enough that avoiding him took a visible angle.

At the threshold Neville paused and looked back once.

"This isn't a punishment," he said. "It's me not having room for doubt."

Then he left too.

Owen stood alone in the disused classroom with the torn-thread dust from the sheets still drifting in the late light. On the desk one peppermint had rolled out of the jar and come to rest against a crack in the wood. He stared at it for a second as if he had been assigned the task of accounting for sweets and had failed that as well.

When he finally went downstairs to the Hufflepuff common room, the round room was full in the ordinary way: homework, wet shoes by the fire, somebody arguing over a Transfiguration essay, bread smell coming faintly through the stones from the kitchens. The scene should have been a relief. Instead it felt arranged.

At the long central table two third-years were bent over a hand-drawn map of corridor turns. One of them, a boy with a patched sleeve and a habit of chewing the inside of his cheek, saw Owen come in and put his hand over the paper at once. He did it efficiently, not guiltily.

The other boy folded the map and slid it under his Potions text.

Neither of them said anything.

Owen crossed the room to his usual chair and set the satchel down between his shoes. The manuscript was still there. The notebook was still there. So was everything Snape had not taken, everything Neville would not now let near his own work, everything large and hidden and structurally wrong.

Around him the common room kept being warm, round, and Hufflepuff in the old stubborn way. Somebody laughed by the hearth. Somebody else asked for the butter. No one asked him to pass it.


# Chapter 12

Owen knelt in the black mouth of a service recess with his notebook open on his thigh and listened to Martha hit the wall again.

The wrecked corridor still smelled faintly of burst cisterns and wet plaster from the sabotage, though the house-elves had got the worst of the flood out days ago. Damp had dried in dirty crescents on the skirting. A cracked suit of armor lay in sections along the flagstones where it had been pulled apart and never properly put back: gauntlets by the first window, breastplate by the third door, one leg propped against a stack of boxed school records that had gone soft at the corners. The far stair should have led down toward the moving portraits and then the turn for the Trophy Room. Instead the damaged thresholds folded the passage back into itself. Anyone who took the last door arrived again by the broken armor and the white scuff of Amycus's boot.

"Up," Amycus said. His voice carried lazily in the empty hour after curfew. "Don't lie there and make me fetch you. If I have to fetch you, I'll do it badly."

Metal grated on stone. Martha got both hands under the breastplate and dragged it in against her knees. Even from the recess Owen could hear her breath catching. She had lost her plait halfway through the detention. Dark hair stuck to her face and neck with sweat. One sleeve was wet through at the cuff where she had gone down in the old toilet water on her second pass. There was blood on one shin now, not much in amount but bright enough to look separate from everything else in the corridor.

"It weighs the same every time," Amycus said. "Thought Hufflepuffs were sturdy."

"Thought teachers could count," Martha said, and her voice came out rougher than usual but still hers, practical and annoyed before it was frightened. "You've had me carry it six times."

Amycus laughed through his nose. He had a habit of working at the side of one back tooth with his thumbnail when he was enjoying himself. Owen could see the glint of the nail as he leaned in the middle of the corridor with his wand tucked under one arm like a cane. He smelled of sweat, old wine, and whatever over-spiced meat the staff had eaten for supper. "Then you ought to be getting better at it."

He flicked his wand. The breastplate jumped in Martha's hands and slammed hard into her thigh. She swore, bent double, nearly lost hold of it, then straightened because he was waiting for her not to.

Owen looked down at the page under his left thumb.

He had spent three nights preparing it in smaller and smaller claims until the line would hold. Not an escape charm. Not rescue. Nothing as clean as that. The First Art would not answer to a wish dressed up as certainty. This was a local amendment written over a copied threshold table from the lower castle, soot and chalk and a sting of his own blood worked into the linen surface until the notation darkened and sat still. If he pressed the page to the third lintel, where the corridor's broken return had the oldest stone under it, and let his palm burn open against the lower hinge, the passage should answer to its shortest true course for perhaps forty seconds. A minute if the castle was merciful. Long enough for a body already pointed toward the stair to go down it instead of being spat back into Amycus's hands.

That was all.

That was not all.

The same page, spent on the lower maintenance door off the kitchen runs, might finally persuade the sealed turn beneath the East Undercroft to answer as a door again instead of a wall. He had the route, the copied fractions, the mortar seam, the half-legible margin from the Leaves that only made sense when laid against founder stone. He had the corridor above empty because Amycus had cleared everyone off for this little show. He had, for once, enough quiet to try the deeper work without tripping over prefect patrols or other students wanting warmth or bread or honesty he had no intention of giving them.

If he fired the amendment here, the ward scar would ring through two floors of castle. Snape would feel it if he was awake. Owen's wand would go wrong for hours afterward. The lower door would keep being a wall. The route would wait, yes, but routes did not merely wait. They got noticed. They got bricked. They got cleaned, catalogued, assigned some tedious school use that made secret work impossible. Old opportunities died by inches.

Martha lurched past the second window with the breastplate clanging against her shins.

Amycus had turned the corridor into a lesson. That was the ugliest part of him. He never wanted pain by itself if he could help it. Pain was better when it taught the room what he wanted the room to learn. Two fourth-year Slytherins had been made to watch the first half hour from beyond the rope at the stair mouth. One of them, a sandy boy with a runny winter nose and a cardigan sleeve unraveling at the wrist, had tried not to look and been struck so hard round the ear he fell into the wall. Amycus had sent them off only after he got bored with their faces and decided Martha alone would do.

Now he was making her carry the armor from one end of the broken corridor to the other, through a door that kept returning her, and every time the space cheated he acted as if she had failed some simple instruction. When she slowed he hit her calves with short blunt jinxes that made the muscles jump under the skin. When she slipped he made her start again from the armor's scattered heap instead of where she had fallen. Once, twenty minutes before, he had kicked the helmet at her and split her lip when she did not catch it properly.

Owen knew all this because he had been there for twenty minutes and had not left.

He could keep the facts narrow if he kept the language narrow. The page would not save Martha for good. At best it would get her one clean run at the stair. Amycus might catch her on the landing. She might not understand quickly enough what had changed. Snape might come. The corridor might over-answer and unfasten the wrong turn. Any of those things could happen, and if one of them did the only result would be more pain, more scrutiny, less chance of reaching the anchor before the castle's damage worsened again.

All of that stood up well enough in the mind.

It did not change the smaller, meaner fact that the page under his thumb would have helped her tonight.

Martha got to the far door. She shifted the weight of the breastplate, boot sliding on grit, and tried to shoulder it through with the last of her balance. The door took her. For one second she was gone from the corridor. Then the broken threshold returned her neatly to the starting stretch by the first window.

The breastplate slipped from her hands and crushed down across her foot. She made a short animal sound and bit it off.

Amycus actually clapped once. "There she is. We were losing our rhythm."

She bent to lift the thing again. One of her hands failed. He had not seen that happen before. Her right hand opened and closed uselessly over the rim as if the fingers belonged to somebody standing next to her. She stared at them, annoyed, then tucked the bad hand against the metal and levered the weight up against her hip with her forearm instead.

He could end this pass. He could do it now, while Amycus was still talking.

His palm itched under the old burn. His teeth hurt with the nearness of worked stone. The page was ready. The copied line had settled. He even had the third lintel chosen already, had measured the cracks around it with his eye when Amycus first hauled Martha into the corridor and slammed the warded door behind them.

Martha dragged the breastplate forward, limping properly now.

"Come on," Amycus said. "You've still got the stairs to miss."

She reached the place in the corridor where the service recess ran behind the wall in a slit too narrow for anyone but a child or a starved older student to use comfortably. Owen had his body pulled back from the gap, but not far enough. Her head turned on the sound of the armor scraping. Her eyes snagged on the black seam in the panelling and found him at once.

She did not stop. She did not call out. Her expression changed less than he would have thought possible. There was a flicker of recognition, then something flatter and much colder than surprise. She shifted the breastplate higher, took one more limping step, and looked away from him so she could see where she was putting her feet.

Amycus had not noticed. He was digging again at his molar with the nail of his thumb.

Owen could still have used the page.

He waited for the heat of shame or panic or decency to make the choice for him. Nothing obliged. There was only the corridor, the notation, the weight of the founder seam below the kitchens like a second pulse under the stone. He thought, with the ugly exactness that came to him when he wanted to feel less instead of more, that Martha was conscious, still upright, still capable of taking the stair if given it, and that a leg could be mended better than a lost route.

He folded the page once, very carefully, so the wet-dark line in the corner would not crack, and slid it back under the oilcloth.

Amycus said, "Again," and Martha, without looking toward the recess this time, bent for the helmet.

Owen backed out of the slit, turned into the service run, and went down.

The kitchen-side passages were warm only by comparison with the rest of the castle. Steam from the scullery pipes slicked the stone. Farther in, past the last place house-elves stacked fireblackened pans, the warmth died and old pressure took over. He moved with his lamp hooded and low, counting the turns he had already marked in pencil on the inside cover of his notebook. Two left under the cask room. One descent by the split step. Keep his hand off the west wall where the mortar sweated in cold weather and left salt in the skin. The work steadied him because it always did. The work asked for sequence and weight and what answered to what. No part of it required that he return to the corridor above and act like a person whose first duty was to the nearest body.

At the maintenance door below the East Undercroft he knelt again.

The door had no handle left, only the memory of one, a long vertical discoloration in the stone where metal had once fixed into a slot. He laid the prepared page against that mark and put his burned palm over the center. Pain arrived cleanly, as if the old coffer had woken under his skin. Not heat this time. Pressure, then a sharp inward pull that seemed to take the hinges through his wrist and up into the roots of his teeth. The copied line in the page went slick and alive beneath his hand.

He said the condition exactly as he had narrowed it, no bigger than necessary. The linen darkened. The stone gave a little under his palm.

Above him, somewhere very far away and still far too near, there came a muffled clang that might have been armor or a body or both.

He did not stop.

The seam of the door shivered. Dust dropped in a thread from the lintel. A strip of black opened, no wider than two fingers, and colder air licked across the back of his hand carrying lake damp and the old mineral smell from deeper under the castle.

For one second relief was stronger than anything else in him.

It was working. The page had held. The lower turn had been answering as wall for months and now, under the copied line and founder pressure, it was willing to remember otherwise. Not open yet, not fully, but enough to prove the route real. Enough to keep going.

He breathed once, twice, feeling the ache race up his arm. The castle rang around the amendment in a pitch only half sound. It was softer than the contact working in November had been. Better controlled. Lower down. He could tell himself that mattered.

By the time the linen page went slack and dead under his hand he had widened the gap to the width of his fist. Beyond it lay a descending dark and the cold wet smell of stone that had not seen ordinary school air in centuries.

He sat back on his heels with blood from the reopened burn creeping into his lifelines and thought, not for the first time and not for the last, that this was what justified everything.

When he climbed up an hour later, the corridor above was empty. Amycus had gone. The rope had been taken down. The armor lay in a different pattern now, as if the pieces had been kicked apart for sport. There was a smear of blood low on the wall by the third door where a hand had caught and slipped.

Owen did not touch it.

***

The kitchens had gone quiet by the time he found Martha.

Most nights there was always some clatter left in them: spoons in sinks, pans stacked to dry, the low collective muttering of house-elves who worked as if silence itself were a spill needing mopping. Tonight the big ovens only hissed. A skin had formed on the pan of onion broth nearest the banked fire. Tallow stood at the central table sorting bent forks into two neat piles, his singed left ear tied up with a strip of checked pudding cloth so it would not brush his shoulder. He looked up once when Owen came in and then looked down again at the cutlery as if deciding whether Owen belonged with the salvageable things or the scrap.

Martha sat on a flour bin by the wall with one leg stretched out on an overturned crate. Madam Pomfrey had done what she could and no more. The knee was bandaged thickly under a borrowed pair of cut-off pajama bottoms, but the joint beneath the wrapping was still grotesquely swollen. There were green-brown bruises already climbing her calf where Amycus's jinxes had landed. Somebody had cleaned the blood off her lip. Her hair had been gathered into the first failed stages of a plait and then abandoned, so half of it hung loose over one shoulder. She held a bowl in her left hand because the right was strapped from knuckle to wrist and did not want to close properly.

She was peering into the broth with faint irritation.

"It's got skin on it," she said to Tallow. "I know that's not your fault. I'm still saying it."

Tallow flicked one bent fork into the good pile. "Miss Sorrell is welcome to complain after she drinks it. Complaining before drinking it is vanity."

"It's not vanity if I'm correct."

"It often is."

She gave a short laugh that caught in the middle and turned into a wince. That hurt Owen more than the sight of the knee had done. Not because the pain was worse. Because the laugh was ordinary. Because she was still doing it.

Tallow took a second bowl from the hob with a folded cloth and set it on the far edge of the table without looking at Owen. "If Mr. Phipps has come to stand in a doorway till the draft does the rest, Tallow asks him to choose a hobby less damp."

"Thanks," Owen said.

Tallow made a small noise that might have meant nothing. He went on sorting forks.

Owen took the bowl and sat on the bench opposite Martha because there was nowhere else to sit that would not make a point of itself. The broth smelled of onion and pepper and a little too much bay. Heat rose into his face from it. He had not realized how cold the undercroft had left him until then.

Martha sipped carefully, swallowed, and only then looked at him.

"So," she said.

"So."

"How far did you get?"

He did not pretend not to understand her. "Past the maintenance door."

She nodded once, as if confirming a sum she had already done. "Right."

Tallow's hands did not stop moving over the bent cutlery, but the sound of metal on metal got very exact.

Owen put his bowl down. "Pomfrey said your knee should mend."

"That's nice of it."

"She said the hand's mostly swelling and strain."

"Amazing. Have you got any more medical miracles, or was that the lot?"

"Martha."

"No, go on. You seem to have come with notes."

He had, in a sense. He could hear himself choosing smaller claims before he made them. "I had a line ready."

"Yes," she said. "I saw."

That should not have surprised him, but it did. The cold in him moved lower.

Martha shifted the bowl from one palm to the other because the heat was getting into the bad hand through the wrapping. "I wasn't sure at first if it was you or just a shape in the wall. Then you moved your thumb. I know your stupid hands."

He looked at his fingers. Ink in the cuticles. Dust ground into the creases. A little dried blood at the heel of the palm where the burn had split again.

"It wasn't certain," he said. "I could have straightened the corridor for a bit. Maybe. If the lintel answered properly. But if I'd done it there, with Amycus right on top of it, the scar would've rung half the floor. Snape's already watching. And the same line was the only stable version I had for the lower door. I needed-"

"I know what you needed."

"You don't know the whole-"

"I know the size of a corridor," she said, still very calm. "I know the size of my knee. I know you had something in your pocket that would've changed the room and you left it there because you wanted the other thing more. That's plenty, Owen."

He tried again because method was easier than silence. "If I'd spent it there, the route below would've stayed shut. I had the ratios from the table at last. I had the quiet for it. Amycus had cleared everyone off. I couldn't guarantee the corridor would've held long enough for you to get clean away, and if it went wrong you could've been hurt worse than-"

She laughed once, not because it was funny. "Worse than what, exactly?"

The question sat between them with the kitchen heat on it.

He looked at the swollen knee and failed to answer.

"No, really," she said. "I'm curious now. Worse than that? Worse than him dropping the breastplate on my foot because I couldn't feel my hand? Worse than being sent back through the same bloody door till I started knowing which patch of wall I was going to hit before I hit it? Give me the chart. I want to see where the red line is."

Tallow turned away to the oven, lifted the broth pan, and gave them privacy so pointedly that it was almost indecent.

Owen said, "I'm not saying it was fine."

"You are, a bit."

"I'm saying I couldn't do both."

"No." Martha put the bowl in her lap and held it there with both forearms because the bad hand would not trust itself. "See, that's where you start cheating. You keep saying both like the choice was me on one side and the whole world on the other. It wasn't. It was me in a corridor and the work you wanted to be doing instead. Those aren't the same thing."

"The work is bigger than-"

"Stop." The word was not loud, but it cut him off cleaner than shouting would have. "Don't hide in that. Bigger isn't magic. Bigger isn't holy. Bigger doesn't wash your hands for you."

He stared at the steam coming off his untouched broth. The skin on it had started to wrinkle.

"I know how it sounds," he said.

"I know how it is."

There was a long enough silence for the oven to give a settling tick and for a pan somewhere near the sinks to drip twice into another pan. Martha shifted her leg a fraction and shut her eyes against the pain before opening them again.

"If you'd frozen," she said, "that would have been one thing. If you'd run because you were scared and then hated yourself after, that would at least be ordinary. If you'd not been there at all, maybe I could tell myself you hadn't known. But you were there. You worked it through. You looked at me and kept the page for later."

"It wasn't later. It was immediately after."

"That's not helping."

He shut his mouth.

Martha drank more broth, because even now she was the sort of person who would finish hot food before it went to waste, and wiped her upper lip on the back of her wrist where the split had started bleeding again. "Do you know what I kept thinking in there?" she asked.

He shook his head.

"I kept thinking you'd do something annoyingly technical and then apologize for interrupting me while I was being punished." Her mouth twitched once. "That sounds stupid when I say it out loud, but there it is."

"It's not stupid."

"No. It was stupid, actually. That's on me." She adjusted the bowl again. "I've kept making you smaller than the thing you're turning into because I liked the old size of you. I thought if I dragged you to enough meetings and enough meals and enough daft jobs you might stay where people could reach you."

He felt the shape of the service recess again under his shoulders, the black slit in the wall, her eyes finding him and then going flat.

"Martha-"

"No, listen now. You don't get to tidy this up into fear or bad timing or you being secretly noble in a way I can't quite see. It isn't that. You've gone wrong in the scale of things. Ordinary-sized human pain doesn't weigh enough for you anymore if there's a diagram under your hand. That's what tonight was. Not cowardice. Not confusion. Just that."

The words were plain. That made them worse. If she had shouted, if she had called him monstrous or useless or cruel, he could have argued with the shape of it in his own head. But this was smaller and more exact. This he could fit against what had happened and find no slack in.

"I was trying to get to something that might matter for more than one corridor," he said, hearing as he spoke how little the sentence had in it besides himself.

"And I was in one corridor." Martha leaned back against the wall, suddenly pale under the kitchen heat. "People are always in one corridor, Owen. Or one bed, one room, one ditch, one queue for soup, whatever it is. That's where life happens. If your great important thing keeps asking you to step round the person in front of you, then it isn't making you better at seeing. It's teaching you not to look."

Tallow came back with a small plate of bread crusts rubbed with garlic and set it by Martha's good elbow. He did not set any by Owen. "Miss Sorrell needs salt," he said to the room at large. "Arguments make young creatures leak."

"Thanks," Martha muttered, and picked up a crust awkwardly between her left thumb and forefinger.

Owen almost said thank you too and stopped because the words would have sounded like theft.

Martha chewed, swallowed, and then said, more tiredly than before, "I can't keep doing this with you."

He knew what she meant and still asked, "Doing what?"

"Being the bit of ordinary life you visit between descents." She looked at him straight on. There were shadows under her eyes that had not been there in September. "I'm not your reminder to eat. I'm not your excuse for still being at our table. I'm not going to keep hauling you by the sleeve and pretending you'll come back if I say the right thing plain enough."

"You don't have to haul me anywhere."

"Good. Then we agree."

He felt the answer land before she spoke it.

"We're finished, then," she said. Not dramatic. Not vicious. The sort of sentence someone used when a chore had gone on too long and there was no point wasting more soap on it. "Properly. Sit somewhere else. Work with whoever still wants to work with you. If Neville asks why I don't send for you anymore, I'll tell him myself."

He sat very still. The bench under him had a wobble in one leg. He had never noticed that before.

"I'm sorry," he said, and because even now part of him wanted precision, he added, "I know that isn't the useful bit."

Martha nodded. "No. It isn't."

She turned her attention back to the bread crust in her hand, then to the bowl, blowing the skin aside with one tired breath before taking another sip. She did not dismiss him. She did not need to. The whole kitchen had already adjusted around a fact that no longer required discussion. Tallow moved behind the table with his careful cutlery and did not once ask Owen if he wanted more broth. The ovens hissed. Somewhere in the far pantry a stack of plates settled against itself.

Owen looked at Martha's bowl, at the smear of butter on the crust she had not quite managed to spread evenly with one hand, and thought absurdly of all the small things she used to notice first: whether the porridge had caught at the bottom, whether somebody had left a scarf on a damp peg, whether he'd gone a whole day on tea and crusts and called it enough. She had always dragged the world back down to touch and food and bruises before it could float off into talk. He had liked that in her because it saved him trouble. He saw now what else it had been.

He stood.

Neither Martha nor Tallow looked up.

His own bowl sat full on the table, film forming over the broth. He left it there and went out through the scullery door into the colder dark beyond the kitchens, and for the first time since he had found the footnote in the old Binns edition he understood that a piece of work could go forward exactly as planned and still leave something ruined in its wake that no later correctness would mend.


# Chapter 13

By April the East Undercroft felt less like a discovery than a job Owen had not meant to take and could no longer leave half done. The rest of Hogwarts still interrupted him like a school: bells, essay deadlines, Amycus roaring in a corridor, somebody at breakfast asking whether he meant to eat that bit of toast. The undercroft only demanded exactness. Its damp came up through the knees of his trousers when he knelt by the plinth. The old pressure built behind his molars until he either got the lines right or went back upstairs with a headache that made parchment shine. Martha had stopped speaking to him except when a doorway forced the issue. Prue still spoke, but not with any waste in it. Down here the stone did not ask what kind of person that made him. It only waited to see whether he had understood.

He had been wrong about the lower tables for nearly three weeks.

The mistake annoyed him in a practical way that was worse than embarrassment. He had copied the pages twice into the ash-linen notebook, once in his own cramped hand and once with the signs separated into columns the way Prue had suggested, and still he had kept treating the damaged block beneath Hogwarts as if it were a repair rubric with half its instructions lost. It was not damaged. The repetition had hidden it. The same cluster sat beneath all four anchors, shifted one place each time, which meant it was not a local instruction at all. It was a relation: held, thinned, under weight, in live water.

It was not a spell. It was not even, properly, a direction. It was a condition under which one site answered to another.

He put the notebook flat on the anchor stone and checked the copied marks again, rubbing the heel of his hand over the place where the page had softened from damp. The Hogwarts column did not end where he had thought it ended. It turned. The star-table cut around the plinth, which he had been using as a map, had a second use embedded inside the same geometry. The old builders had not bothered to make a separate door for departure any more than they would have made a separate alphabet. If you were permitted one, you were presumed able to read the other.

The thought made him sit back on his heels for a moment, cold seeping through the stone into his shins. He had spent hours tapping walls, measuring seams, tracing drafts like an idiot in a children's adventure. The chamber below was not hidden behind the undercroft. It was produced by it. The undercroft was the anchor aspect of a structure whose other aspect lay farther down, toward the lake, and only existed as accessible space when the site was made to answer as transit instead of holding.

"Right," he said quietly, because hearing it aloud made it less likely to wriggle away again.

His own voice came back to him as a damp blur from the stone.

He unrolled the strip of thin paper he had been using as a protective overlay and laid it against the groove on the plinth's lakeward side. The groove looked decorative until you followed its returns with the burned centre of his right palm. Then the line answered with a faint heat unlike the cold around it, the way the manuscript sometimes answered by becoming marginally more itself under his hand. He took up the pencil and marked the three relation signs into the blank spaces he had left weeks ago, checking each one against the notebook, forcing himself not to hurry just because his pulse had finally started behaving as if he were right. The sequence stayed the same: held, thinned, under weight, in live water.

The last sign was the awkward one, all bent stems and a small cut across the curve. He had once taken it for damage from mineral drip. Prue had said damage did not usually repeat with such good manners. He wished, not for the first time, that he had let himself be grateful in a less useless way when she said things like that.

When the last mark sat properly in its groove, nothing happened for two whole breaths. Then the damp sheen on the wall opposite the plinth brightened as if a lamp had been turned behind the stone. Owen stood too quickly, banged his knee on the plinth edge, swore under his breath, and went still again.

It was not a door opening. The wall simply lost its certainty. The seam he had taken for a natural meeting of blocks thickened into a pale vertical band. Water threaded down it, silver and steady. The pressure in his ears rose, not sharply but insistently, like a hand cupping the back of his skull. A section of stone no wider than a wardrobe withdrew from the category of wall and stood there as depth.

Steps appeared inside it, worn into a narrow turning stair of old black stone. A bronze rail, green with age, ran down one side. Cold air came up from below with the mineral reek of flooded cellars and the stale-metal smell of something that had not moved in centuries until now.

He had wanted certainty about the outer wards. What he had now was a stair under the lake.

Owen picked up the notebook, tucked the paper overlay back into it, and put the pencil between his teeth so both hands were free. His wand sat heavy and temperamental in his sleeve. He did not trust it enough for anything beyond light, and even that might go strange if the site objected.
