After a moment's thought he took the candle stump from his pocket instead, struck it with a match cupped against the damp, and watched the flame crawl up crooked and yellow. The light made the new stair look narrower rather than safer.

He descended sideways.

Water had found the steps years ago and never stopped using them. It sheeted over the treads in a skin so thin it did not look dangerous until his sole shifted on the second turn and his shoulder hit the wall hard enough to send the candlelight jumping. The stone was colder than the winter flagstones in the Great Hall, colder than the metal taps in the Hufflepuff loos at dawn. It had the impersonal cold of depth, a temperature kept by weight rather than weather. By the fifth turn the cuffs of his trousers were soaked. By the eighth the skin along his burned palm had begun to sting as if the whole route knew exactly where he had been marked.

The sounds were wrong in a way the undercroft's sounds were already teaching him to expect. Above ground, a bang remained a bang and a shout remained a shout. Down here every noise arrived as pressure first. A distant shift in the lake came through the wall as a great padded thud. His own steps returned from below as if somebody larger and slower were climbing up to meet him. Twice he stopped dead because the hairs on his arms lifted under his sleeves and he was certain he had heard breathing, and twice it was only water taking a new line through stone.

The stair emptied into a passage just tall enough for him to stand straight if he kept his head clear of the dripping vault. The floor had once been level, perhaps polished even, but the lake had pushed through hairline cracks and laid a skin of pale grit over everything. His candle showed grooves worn in parallel along the middle of the floor. They were too even for erosion. Something wheeled had gone back and forth here many times, heavy enough to bite rock and regular enough to make the same journey matter.

That steadied him more than any prayer would have done. The grooves meant carts, weight, and repetition. Departure, then, had not been a legend whispered over firelight by people in robes wanting to sound older than they were. It had been traffic.

The passage bent, widened, and gave him the chamber.

For a moment he did not take in the lens at all. What he saw first were the benches.

They ran along the walls in two long ranks, their wooden seats long since rotted away so only the iron brackets remained, each pair bolted at the same careful height. Above them, shallow niches had been cut into the stone with little raised lips to keep objects from sliding. Most were empty. One still held a corroded buckle, green and blunt with age. Another contained what looked like the mineral ghost of some folded fabric that had turned, over a vast stretch of damp, into a crust the colour of old teeth. On the floor near the far wall lay the snapped frame of a trolley or barrow, not magical in any showy sense, just practical metal with its wheel gone. The chamber had the stripped, after-use look of a station closed in a hurry and never reopened, except that hurry had occurred at a civilizational scale.

He imagined, unwillingly and all at once, coats dark with lake weather, children made to wait quietly, somebody counting loads, somebody swearing because a fastening had jammed, and then he cut the picture off. The place did not need his invented ghosts to prove what it was.

The star-lens occupied the chamber's far end like an altar built by surveyors.

It stood in a cradle of bronze ribs as tall as the classroom windows on the third floor, tilted upward into the black curve of the vault. Its face was a dark mineral disc, not quite glass, with a white crack running from the lower rim toward the centre and stopping there in a crooked hook. Three outer rings surrounded it, each cut with notation so worn he could only read every fourth or fifth mark without touching. One ring sat misaligned on its bearing, shoved half an inch off true. Salt had bloomed along the base in thin furred ridges. The whole thing looked as if somebody had built a telescope with the assumptions of stonework instead of lenses: weight, angle, pressure, endurance.

Beside it, a waist-high desk of the same black stone carried more tables, these shallower and narrower than the anchor stone's, as if intended for monitoring rather than for real work. Owen set his candle in a dry pocket of rock and bent over them until the flame painted the grooves gold.

He knew enough now to stop hoping for easy prose. The notation was all function, no kindness. But the structure was familiar: site, load, visibility, threshold, answer.

His scalp prickled.

The lens was not for looking outward in any ordinary astronomical sense. It was for reading the ward skin from the inside and measuring what answered beyond it when the skin brightened. The cracked disc made sense suddenly, and horribly. If this chamber had been part of the departure infrastructure, then the people who used it had not merely wanted a route off Earth. They had wanted to know when the route needed to be taken.

He set his notebook beside the desk and copied the nearest string into the margin with fingers that had begun, annoyingly, to shake. It was not exactly fear. It was not only fear. The feeling had the childish, nauseous excitement of finding that the impossible thing had a manual after all.

There was a receiving groove across the desk, empty except for grit. He cleaned it with the edge of his cuff, then laid his burned palm across it because nothing else in the room was likely to count as a valid key. The skin answered at once with the deep needle-hot ache he had learned meant yes. The skewed outer ring of the lens jerked. Metal grated against metal with a noise like a chair dragged slowly over stone. The disc darkened until it no longer reflected the candle.

He ought to have stepped back. Instead he leaned closer.

At first he thought the lens had failed. It showed only blackness, shallow and matte, like a slate rubbed with old grease. Then the black adjusted in layers. Faint points came through, not stars as seen from a tower on a clear night but stars as coordinates, stripped of twinkle and atmosphere. Curving across them, almost invisible until his eyes took the scale of it, ran a film of light so thin it might have been a flaw in the mineral itself.

It enclosed.

It did not show the whole planet in any naturalistic schoolbook way. The lens was not interested in continents or oceans. It showed relation. The film was the ward field, a skin of answer wrapped around Earth's magical signal, and at one point on that skin a line shone harder than the rest.

It was Hogwarts.

He knew it with the immediate bodily certainty of recognizing his own handwriting. The stressed segment lay in the same orientation the star-table used for the school's anchor relation, and the light there was wrong. It was not bright in a triumphant sense. It was raw, rubbed thin. The white hook of the crack in the lens aligned with it, and for a disgusting second he could not tell whether he was seeing damage in the instrument or damage in the thing it read.

He touched the first load-mark on the desk.

The bright line quivered. Around it the ward skin thickened and dimmed in pulses, each one corresponding not to a single curse or spell but to some broader class of magical pressure: the Carrows' punishments, the winter corridor catastrophe, and whatever old work he himself had already forced into the castle. Hogwarts did not flare continuously. It took impacts, held, and then shone where it had been struck.

"All right," he whispered. "All right. Show me."

The next mark along the desk was not labelled in any language he knew, but the grouping was unmistakable now that the lens had taught him what sort of instrument it was. It indicated escalated local conflict, concentrated exchange, more than two hands, more than ordinary discipline. He pressed it with two fingers.

The line at Hogwarts brightened until it ceased to look like a line. It opened into a white seam.

Something answered.

He did not see a face. He did not see claws, mouths, or any of the rubbish storybooks reached for when they meant danger and could not imagine it except with teeth. What changed beyond the ward was the black itself. A spread of darkness that had seemed evenly empty resolved into structure by moving the wrong way. Several distant occlusions altered together, not advancing across space so much as turning toward the seam with the horrible efficiency of one thing distributed over too much distance. The stars behind that region dimmed by degrees rather than disappearing, as if attention had weight and had been laid across them.

Owen's heel slid in the lake grit. He caught himself on the stone desk with his left hand, pain ringing up his wrist. The lens went on showing him the answer without any interest in whether he was prepared to look at it.

Predation was too small a word, really. Foxes and hawks were individual animals with stomachs. This was the relation a leak had to whatever lived by finding leaks. The brightened seam did not attract rage or curiosity. It attracted use.

He forced himself to look back down at the marks instead of freezing there with his hand cramped against the desk edge. The table did not end at escalated conflict. There was another bracket, wider, that meant siege and massed hostile working at a single node. The old builders had planned for castle battles because of course they had. If you built a school as a fortress and wired it into a planetary ward, you did not get to act surprised when war and infrastructure eventually touched.

He did not want to touch that mark. He touched it anyway.

The lens answered so fast it felt like a blow.

The Hogwarts seam did not merely brighten. It whitened past legibility, a rent now, no longer the sort of damage that could still plausibly be called a crack. The ward field around it flared outward in a branching glaze. Beyond it the not-empty dark cohered further, enough that the chamber seemed to acquire more cold from the sight alone. The distributed thing did not rush. Rushing belonged to hunger that doubted itself. This attention only committed. Owen had the awful impression that if such a flare occurred in truth, there would be a period of minutes or hours in which the breach was still becoming real and the answer beyond it would already be on its way, not through air, not by broom or beast or ship, but by whatever means made distance a secondary question.

He snatched his hand off the desk. The image thinned at once. The white rent fell back to a strained line, the dark beyond loosened into scattered absences again, and the chamber's ordinary cold came back as if someone had taken a blade from the back of his neck.

For a little while he could not do anything except stand there and listen to water working through the stone.

He had been right.

That was the first fact, stupidly, because some childish bit of him had continued to reserve a corner for the possibility that the whole structure might yet reduce to symbolism, old ward weather, the usual noble rubbish about balance and vigilance and legacy. It had not. The thing outside was not metaphor, and the school's violence was not merely ugly local history inside a sealed world. Hogwarts was flashing through the mask every time the year turned savage enough, and a real battle here would not just bruise the wards. It would announce Earth.

The second fact arrived behind it, colder and more useful. A battle at Hogwarts was not an extravagant hypothetical anymore. The whole year had been converging toward one whether the people in corridors admitted it or not. Harry Potter was still at large. Voldemort had made the school a fortress and a hostage both. Neville was stockpiling people, food, exits, stubbornness. Snape was watching too closely for peace. Even the ordinary business of lessons had started to feel like a thin coat of paint over a wall already cracking at the bricks. The castle was going to become a battlefield. He did not know whether that meant in a month or three days or the last night of term. He only knew the lens had just reduced every other timetable in his life to a joke.

His hand hurt. He looked down and found the groove on the desk had reopened the old burn in two fine splits across the centre. There was not much blood, but there was enough to bead.

He wiped it on his handkerchief and opened the notebook to a clean page.

What he wrote first was not fear. It was work.

The anchor would have to be stabilised before any battle load hit the node. It did not need to be tidied, studied, or admired. It had to be stabilised. He wrote the word twice, once in English and once beneath it in the closest Meridian structure he could manage, because the second version carried load and sequence inside it. Then he listed what he had and what he did not: existing plinth responses; partial transit access; one functioning read instrument, damaged but legible; insufficient command of seam language; no margin for another local amendment used on corridors, punishments, or rescue. He stared at that last line until the letters blurred slightly, then forced the pencil onward.

He would need longer blocks below ground and fewer visible errands above. He would need chalk that did not shed too much grit, salt, clean water, bandages, and something thin enough to fit the fine grooves without snapping. He would need to stop pretending homework mattered except as camouflage. He would need sleep when his body refused to stand, not when some school bell said it was night. He would need to tell no one what the chamber had shown him.

That last point clarified itself with brutal speed. If he told Prue, she would hear battle and mean Prue's friends, Martha's limp, Neville's impossible little army, the first-years who still flinched when Amycus raised his voice. She would not be wrong. That was precisely the problem. Anyone he told above ground would understand the danger at the scale of the school because that was the human scale, and the human scale was exactly what had already failed him once he learned how large the other thing was. He could not spend the next weeks arguing about whether it was monstrous to think in planetary units when the planet was the unit at risk.

The thought should have shamed him more cleanly than it did. Instead it settled into place with the rest of the arithmetic, ugly and useful.

He kept writing until the candle shrank enough to spit tallow down the side.

Only then did he look once more into the lens, this time without touching any load mark. The ward skin lay around the stars like breath on black glass. The stressed place at Hogwarts remained visible, hairline-thin and viciously bright. Beyond it the dark gave nothing away now except the knowledge that it had, under the right provocation, become less like darkness and more like reply.

He found himself looking for sentiment in the chamber and despising the urge. There were no grand farewell inscriptions and no noble lament for a world reluctantly abandoned. The people who built this place had left benches, grooves, instrument tables, a damaged reader, and a route. They had taken what they meant to take and gone. Perhaps that was cruelty. Perhaps it was clarity. Either way, they had not mistaken logistics for prayer.

When he finally lifted the candle and turned back toward the stair, the chamber had already rearranged itself in his mind. It was no longer the place where he had learned something astonishing. It was the place from which a timetable now emanated. Every hour above ground would be measured against the white seam in the lens. Every detention, every mealtime, every argument in a corridor, every piece of ordinary school nonsense still dragging itself through April would sit inside the same smaller bracket: before battle, or after it.

He climbed slower than he had descended because the cold had got properly into him now. Water had soaked through both socks. His fingers ached around the notebook. Twice he stopped to brace himself against the wall while the route's old pressure built in his ears and released again. When he reached the undercroft, the borrowed opening was already losing definition. The stair still existed, but it had resumed being reluctant. He stepped out onto the plinth-side stone just as the wall hardened back into itself and the pale line of damp sank to an ordinary seep.

For a moment he stood in the dark with the notebook clutched to his chest and listened to the castle above. They were not voices exactly. He heard weight, a door somewhere overhead, a far-off run of pipes, and one deep movement in the stone that might have been the lake or the school settling into night. He knew now that if enough magic were thrown through these foundations at once, the lens would show that sound differently. The whole castle would become a flare.

He pinched out the candle. The smoke smelled greasy and domestic, absurdly kitchen-like after the chamber below, and the ordinaryness of it nearly made him laugh.

By the time he reached the service stair and the first faint warmth from the kitchen runs, he had reduced the problem to the next usable units. Tomorrow he would need a better copy of the seam table and a plausible reason to miss part of supper. He would need to watch the castle's magical weather more carefully and stop wasting strength on any intervention that did not directly preserve the node. He would need to finish his Arithmancy essay because unfinished work attracted precisely the sort of notice he could not spare. He would need, above all, to get to the anchor before the war did.

That was the whole calendar now.


# Chapter 14

By April the greenhouses had started sweating before breakfast. Condensation ran down the panes in fat, slow tracks, and the first breath inside Greenhouse Three always hit like a hand over the mouth: wet soil, tomato leaf, damp rope, old compost warming up. Professor Sprout had propped two broken panes with mismatched bits of glass and a strip of tin, and new growth was already climbing over the repair as if damage were only another sort of frame.

Nobody spoke much at first. They had trowels in one hand and essays in the other. Someone had abandoned a Transfiguration parchment on the potting bench and pressed a crescent of dirt straight through the title. A pair of third-years on the far side were arguing in undertones about whether the vanishment chart was due Thursday or Friday, with the low, desperate seriousness of people to whom the answer mattered. It still mattered. The castle had not stopped producing deadlines because it was occupied. There was a two-foot essay due on controlled vanishment, and if the school cracked open over their heads the essay would probably still have been due on controlled vanishment.

Owen worked a tangle of seedling roots free of their tray and tried not to think in systems. Spring made systems too easy. Every bench was a proof of persistence. Nasturtiums had gone reckless over one corner, bright enough to look rude. Shrivelfig cuttings that had sat all winter like dead fingers were furred silver-green at the tips. Someone had left the greenhouse door open a fraction, and cold air kept moving across the backs of his wrists in little draughts that smelled of thawed mud and the lake.

Sprout clapped once. "Mind the roots, not the leaves. The leaves are showing off."

A few people laughed. Not much. Enough.

Owen shifted another plug of soil into a pot and looked, against his better judgment, out through the sweating glass toward the lawns. The grounds were greening in uneven swathes. Black patches still held winter, but between them the first clean colour had come up in a hurry, so that the paths seemed to have been drawn through a place briefly willing to forget itself. Beyond the slope the lake moved under a hard white sky. Wind roughened it in dark strips. From here he could not see the chamber below it, the broken lens, the torn brightness in the old work. He could see only water, and the giant beech near the shore carrying buds at every branch tip like a promise made too early.

His right palm ached where the old burn crossed it, not from pain exactly but from recognition. Some days the hidden work sat in his body before he thought of it, a change in pressure behind the teeth, a hot awareness along scarred skin. Today even the little school magic around him seemed louder than it had a month ago. A girl at the next bench muttered a coaxing charm over a tray of knotgrass and the syllables rang against the greenhouse glass with a density that made him glance up. Nothing happened except the knotgrass straightening obediently in its damp earth. That was the problem. So much of the world still worked.

After Herbology the castle smelt of thawing stone and boiled cabbage. Students moved through the corridors with books clamped to jumpers, skirts damp at the hem from the grounds, ties loosened by degrees as the day warmed. Someone had opened a high window near the moving staircase and the whole landing was full of March-cold air, sharp enough to make noses run. A second-year went past carrying three library books and a loaf heel wrapped in a napkin. Two boys from Ravenclaw were having an earnest row over Arithmancy tables with such total focus that one of them walked into a suit of armour and apologised to it.

It would have been easier, Owen thought, if everything had flattened. If the school had become purely a machine for fear. But ordinary life kept insisting on itself in scraps and chores and stupid routine. Ink still dried on cuffs. People still nicked extra butter at breakfast. Peeves still tipped water out of a trophy cup onto anyone silly enough to run under him. The occupation sat over all of it like rust over metal, but the metal was still there.

At lunch he took his tray to the Hufflepuff table and found space halfway down the bench. The soup was green enough to suggest cress and thin enough to suggest otherwise. Bread came round in torn chunks rather than slices. Somebody had salted the radishes too hard. Over at the Gryffindor table Neville was bent over a bit of parchment with two younger students crowded in, talking with his finger, not his voice, moving from line to line as if the plan would hold together better if he could pin it to the wood. He looked tired in the compact, competent way he had looked all year: collar not quite straight, bruise yellowing at the jaw, hands too rough now for a boy who had once mostly handled plants.

Martha crossed the aisle carrying a second bowl she clearly had no business carrying on her bad leg. She favoured the left side when she thought nobody was looking and then over-corrected when she caught someone noticing. Today she had her dark plait wound up in a knot that had already begun to collapse, and when she sat she tore the crust off her bread by habit and slid it into her pocket instead of eating it. Later, for somebody who missed supper or got shut in somewhere. Or just later. Owen knew the movement before he let himself know he was watching.

She did not look at him. That was not new. It still landed fresh.

Prue arrived late enough that most of the decent bread had gone. She dropped onto the Ravenclaw bench with a stack of books under one arm and pushed her spectacles back up by the bridge without using a hand, an old little trick of her face. Her cuffs were inked blue again. She carried pencils loose in her pocket and they always worked free and jabbed through the lining; once, waiting in the library, Owen had watched her pull out three snapped points and curse at them with extraordinary quiet intensity. Today she set a folded scrap of parchment beside her bowl, glanced once down the hall as if checking whether anyone was watching the wrong thing, and then looked across at him.

She did not smile. She tipped her head very slightly toward the doors.

Later, that meant. Walk, or talk, or both.

He looked down at his soup and nodded once.

The afternoon went on being itself. A corridor smelled of floor polish and wet wool. Somebody in Charms had managed to set a quill smoking and got sent out with black fingers. There was homework from Binns as if Binns still inhabited the same century as the rest of them. Owen copied half a page of notes in the library and none of the words sat properly because he kept hearing, behind the ordinary scrape of chairs and turning paper, the pressure change he had felt under the lake when the star-lens flared. Battle-scale magic. Brightness through a tear. An event already waiting somewhere in front of them, coming on whether the school was ready or not.

When the dinner bell went he nearly did not go. Then he went because not going would be visible, and visibility had its own costs now. By the time he came back down toward the cellars the kitchen heat had started to breathe through the stones. Bread, onions, something yeasty and sweet. The scent caught under the ribs with unreasonable force. It was one of the castle's oldest lies, that if somewhere bread was being baked then things might still, with enough care, be kept from breaking altogether.

Prue was waiting at the end of the corridor where the cellar passage turned out toward the yard door. She had traded the stack of books for a single notebook tied shut with string. One curl had worked itself free and was lifting in the draught by her cheek. When he came up she looked at him for a second as if measuring whether he would try to be difficult from the first word.

"You ate?" she said.

"Some of it."

"Good. Come outside before I change my mind."

He went.

* * *

The path above the lake was still half winter. Mud sucked at the edges, and the reeds at the shore lay in flattened brown mats where the ice had crushed them. But primroses had started up in the shelter of the wall and the willow by the boathouse was beginning to haze green. The evening light was thin and bright enough to make the black water look metallic. Somewhere higher up the grounds a group of students were shouting over a game with all the desperate normality of people making use of an hour that might later be taken away.

Prue sat on the low stone wall, gathered her skirt under her, and untied the string around the notebook only to wind it round her fingers again. She always needed one thing in hand when she was thinking. If there were no book she used string, and if there were no string she picked the paper label off her own bottle of ink in strips and regretted it later when the bottle went tacky.

For a minute they said nothing important.

"Did Sprout still have you all elbow-deep in compost?" she asked.

"Mostly seedlings. One tomato section and some shrivelfig cuttings."

"How pastoral."

"You say that as if you'd have preferred Binns."

"I would have preferred a fire and two uninterrupted hours, but the school remains vulgar about my preferences."

The corner of his mouth moved. Not much. Enough that she saw it and looked away at the lake as if not to press too hard on the almost of a better evening.

"I had to spend forty minutes helping Idris Fawley understand why his chart kept slipping a column," she said after a while. "It was because he can't count, but delicately. There's only so gently you can phrase that."

"Did you phrase it gently?"

"No. I'm trying not to lie more than necessary."

The line sat between them longer than it should have. Owen felt the shift and hated himself for hearing it as structure before he heard it as warning.

He said, "Right."

Prue rubbed the notebook string between thumb and forefinger until it bit white into her skin. "I know something changed."

He kept looking at the lake. "A great deal keeps changing."

"Don't." Her voice did not rise. It thinned. "If I ask badly, answer the better version. I know something changed below."

Wind came off the water and found the gap at the back of his collar. He could have lied. Not cleanly, but enough to preserve the shape of the evening for ten more minutes. He had got good at answering the question nearest the question, at making timing sound like the problem when the problem was value.

"I learned some things about pressure," he said. "About what makes the anchor worse."

"And now you look as if you're already somewhere else even when you're sitting still."

"I'm here."

"You know that's not what I mean."

He dug his thumbnail into the old burn at his palm. The skin there had gone smooth and shiny months ago. It still felt, at certain moments, as if it had only just closed. "I'm trying to work out what's urgent."

Prue gave a short laugh with no amusement in it. "No, you aren't. That's the bit you're quite settled on."

He turned then. "That's not fair."

"It is, actually. I just don't think you like the arithmetic."

Her spectacles had slid down her nose again. She pushed them back up and looked at him over the rims with that dry, almost embarrassed exactness she used when she was coming closest to something personal. Behind her the lake took the light and broke it into hard pieces. A gull cried once, ugly and ordinary.

"I'm not asking you to give it up," she said. "I know you won't, and I'm past wasting breath on fantasies. I'm asking for a proportion."

He said nothing.

"Listen." She unwound the string from her fingers and rewound it more carefully, as if the thought needed tidier handling. "The school isn't going to stay like this. Something's coming. We both know that without you needing to start being coy. If it goes bad here, if it goes properly bad, then we help here first. The people in front of us. The living, obvious, inconvenient people. Then, if there's an after and if the lower work still needs doing, we go back to it. Together, if you want. I mean that. I'm not pretending the other thing doesn't matter. I'm saying there has to be an order large enough for human beings to fit in."

The tenderness of it made him feel briefly sick. Not because she was wrong. Because she had made the wrongness impossible to call stupidity.

He looked down at her hand and the string cutting her fingers. He wanted, with a force that felt almost childish, to take it away from her and say yes. He wanted the sentence, wanted the shape of himself inside it: a boy who could promise that when the castle cried out he would come upstairs, stand with everyone else, keep the scale human and local and bearable. He could see it whole. That was the worst part. He could see the life he was refusing.

"Prue," he said, and stopped.

She waited.

"It isn't that simple."

Something shuttered in her face, not surprise, not yet anger. Recognition. "No," she said. "Of course that's what you've got."

"I mean it literally. Timing matters. Conditions matter. If I leave it too late, late may be the point where there isn't anything left to fix."

"For the ward."

He said, after a moment, "Yes."

"And for us?"

He should have answered at once. The pause did it for him.

Prue looked away. When she spoke again her voice had gone quieter still, and more precise for the effort of not letting it shake. "That's what I was trying to make easy for you, by the way. I was giving you the smaller promise. I wasn't asking for declarations or forever or anything else idiotic. I was saying: when the floor gives way under actual people, stand with the people first. Afterwards you can go back to saving the abstract."

"It's not abstract."

"No. That's exactly the trouble. It's more real to you than we are."

"That's not what I said."

"You don't have to say it if you keep demonstrating it."

He opened his mouth and found only method waiting there, narrow and bloodless. "If the condition shifts before the anchor is stabilised, I may lose the only workable approach. It isn't a matter of preference. It's a matter of sequence."

Prue stared at him for a second, then made a short, unbelieving sound. "Do you hear yourself?"

"I'm trying to be accurate."

"I know. God, I know. You always get more accurate when the thing under the sentence is unbearable."

He had no answer that would not prove her right in a different register.

Below them a wave slapped against stone. Somewhere behind the greenhouses someone yelled with laughter and got shushed. The whole school kept making these small claims on existence. Noise. Dinner. Wet socks drying by illicit fires. Revision schedules. Splinters in the rowing sheds. All the ridiculous local weight of being alive in one place. Owen felt it pressing at him from every direction and knew, with the same exhausted clarity with which he had read the lens below the lake, that it would not be enough to move him.

Prue slipped off the wall and stood facing him. She was nearly his height on the path, the notebook tucked against her ribs like a shield she had not meant to use.

"All right," she said. "Then I need the honest version."

He forced himself to look at her.

"If it comes to it," she said, "if the school breaks open and you can't do both, are we still the first thing?"

The wind worried at the loose curl by her cheek. He could see the little smudge where ink had marked the side of her hand. There was a minute in which honesty and cruelty were, for practical purposes, the same object.

"I don't know," he said.

She breathed in once through her nose and shut her eyes. He saw the sentence land in her body not as shock but as impact, the way a heavy tray landed in a pair of hands that had expected the weight and still did not quite absorb it cleanly.

"Yes, you do," she said.

He said nothing.

"You know, and I know, and that's enough." She looked past him toward the castle. "I kept thinking this was partly about fear. About you deciding that if you cared too much for anything upstairs it would become leverage. Which would've been horrible, but at least it would've been ordinary. Instead you've built yourself some private measure and climbed onto it."

"That's not fair."

"Stop saying that as if fairness is what I owe you."

He flinched. She saw and almost softened. Almost. It made things worse.

"I'm not telling Neville," she said after a moment. "And I'm not telling Martha. Not because you deserve protecting. Because the Carrows don't need help and because panic wouldn't improve anybody's thinking. If you need a practical assurance, there it is."

"Prue, I didn't ask you to keep anything."

"No. You ask by standing there and making everybody else do the arithmetic."

The notebook string had cut a red line around her finger. She unwound it at last and let it hang.

"I can still help with text," she said. "If you bring me notation, I can still look at notation. I'm not suddenly stupid because I'm angry. But whatever this was meant to be with us, whatever nearly-shape it had, I can't do it while you keep arranging the world so that every person in reach comes second to a buried mechanism."

He could feel himself losing the only reply that mattered even as he reached for it. Sorry was true and useless. Trust me was impossible. It will be worth it sounded insane, and worse than insane, it sounded proud.

"I'm sorry," he said anyway.

Prue gave him a tired look. "I know you're sorry. That's almost the bleakest part."

She started back toward the castle. After three steps she stopped without turning.

"Help the living if you can bear it," she said. "That is the compromise. It was a real one."

Then she went on, fast enough that he would have had to call after her to stop her, and he did not.

He stood on the path until the cold got through his socks and the primroses at the wall lost what little colour they had held in the dusk.

* * *

The kitchens were louder than the rest of the castle because they had to be. Pans struck stone. Water ran. Someone laughed outright at a joke Owen could not hear. Steam took the low ceiling and came back damp. After the lake the heat felt almost indecent. It smelt of onions, yeast, dishwater, and the singed sweetness of something caught at the edge and rescued in time.

Tallow was at the long scrubbed table by the ovens, sorting bent spoons into three neat rows by damage rather than size. His hands were wet to the wrist. One ear, already singed at the tip, twitched each time a bigger cauldron lid came down somewhere behind him. He had tucked a dish towel into his belt and forgotten it there.

Owen stopped just inside the arch. He had been coming to the kitchens all year and still had not learned how not to enter as if he were asking a favour of a room.

Tallow did not look up. "If you're after hot bread, Mr Phipps, you're late. The good loaves went upstairs. The rest are cooling and thinking bitter thoughts."

"I'm not after bread."

"A pity. Bread is easier to improve than boys."

Owen might once have smiled. Tonight the line only made the inside of his chest feel scraped clean.

He came closer. "I need a route."

That got Tallow's eyes on him at last. They were dark and bright and entirely unamused. "You do very often."

"No, right, sorry. I mean I need to know if there's a lower way from here to the undercroft if the main corridors are blocked."

Tallow picked up a spoon with one twisted tine, pressed the metal against the table edge with both thumbs, and straightened it slowly before answering. "Blocked how?"

"Crowded. Watched. Not usable."

"That is not an answer, young sir. That is three anxieties in a stack."

Owen heard the old habit in himself before he could stop it. "If there was fighting. Or enough people moving at once that the stairwells jammed. Or if the Carrows had half the doors sealed. Or if I had to get below quickly without being seen by anyone who'd think the wrong thing of it."

"Ah," Tallow said. "So. Only the ordinary cheerful possibilities."

He set the repaired spoon aside and reached for another. Around them the kitchen kept going. A tray of peeled carrots thudded down. Two younger elves hurried past carrying soup tureens between them, not glancing at Owen at all. Somewhere near the far hearth a pot boiled over and somebody swore in a mild, concentrated way.

Tallow said, "Miss Sorrell was in earlier. She carried the potato sacks badly because of that leg and then had the manners to look ashamed about it, which was foolish because potatoes have no pride. Miss Hollis put away six catalog ledgers for the library and stopped to dry her spectacles over the pastry oven because she will not admit they fog. Everybody brings something through the kitchen when the house is under strain."

Owen stayed very still.

"I know what you think of me," he said.

"Do you?" Tallow's tone remained light, which only made the rebuke more exact. "Because Tallow thinks many things of you, and some of them do move around."

He took up a spoon so bent it was nearly useless, considered it, and dropped it into a separate bowl.

"The house has had clever boys before," he said. "Clever girls too. Clever is not rare. What the house gets short of, every so often, is children who know the weight of other children while they are being clever. You came asking for routes when there were books. You came asking for routes when there were old stones. Now you come asking for routes because you think the castle may split, and Tallow is meant to clap and say very good, young sir, excellent foresight. But foresight is not the same as care."

The words landed harder than Prue's had because they were smaller. Less generous. Less interested in his soul. Tallow was discussing him as a maintenance problem, a load borne badly.

Owen looked past him at the ovens, at the dough waiting under cloths, at the rack of drying bowls. "If the castle may split," he said quietly, "then the route matters whether or not I deserve it."

Tallow nodded once. "Yes. Better. That is the first sensible thing you've said since coming down those steps."

He wiped his hands on the towel at his belt and hopped down from the stool. Up close the damp in his cuffs had gone grey with flour. He smelled faintly of smoke and washing soda.

"There's a service stair behind the scullery rack," he said. "Not the first rack. Boys always pull the first rack because boys are committed to obviousness. The third. Left side. The hinge sticks in damp weather, so lift before you pull. It goes down past the hot-water mains and the old coal drop. At the bottom you will want the passage with the cracked blue tiles, not the one that smells of lye. If you smell soap too long, you've gone toward laundry and you are an idiot."

Despite himself, Owen said, "Cracked blue tiles. Not lye."

"Then a narrow turn with a drain underfoot. Mind it. The stone sweats there and feet go from under people in a way that embarrasses everybody. You follow that until the wall starts sounding thick on the left and hollow on the right. Count seven iron brackets. At the seventh there is a hatch no taller than your shoulder. The latch is hidden under a lip of mortar because the founders had no trust in anybody's fingers. That gets you into the older run."

"Toward the east side?"

"Toward where the house still remembers itself, yes."

Tallow's face altered a fraction on the last words. Not softer. More tired.

"I won't take you past the mains," he said. "The lower dark bites now. More than it did in winter. It has an appetite on it."

"I wasn't asking you to."

"No. You seldom ask that particular thing. You wait for other creatures to offer it and then behave surprised when they bleed."

Owen shut his eyes for a second. The kitchen noise moved around him, all the labour of feeding a school pretending not to hear. When he opened them Tallow was already at a shelf, reaching up for something.

What he brought down was not a lantern or a map but a heel of yesterday's loaf wrapped in cloth and a little paper twist of salt.

"For later," Tallow said. "Not because you've earned cosseting. Because boys below stairs forget they are animals and then fall over at important moments."

He put the bundle into Owen's hand without ceremony. It was still faintly warm at the centre where the loaf had held its heat.

"Thank you," Owen said.

Tallow gave him a look. "If you break crockery in that lower passage, leave it where it lies till morning. Tallow is not climbing after your guilt in the dark."

It was the nearest thing to kindness Owen had been offered in hours, and even that arrived shaped like a rule.

"Right," he said. "I won't."

Tallow turned back to the spoons. "See that you don't."

Owen stood there another moment, bread and salt in one hand, route beating itself into memory. Around him the kitchens carried on with their hot practical life, and he felt with a sudden, ugly clarity that he had been taking from rooms like this all year under the name of necessity. Food. Passage. Quiet. Interpretation. Warmth when it was offered. He had told himself use was not the same as betrayal because the danger was real. The danger was real. That did not make the ledger clean.

Nobody asked him to stay. Nobody told him to go.

When he left, the ovens were breathing and the spoons on the table made three tidy rows, each one bent to the degree the house could still justify keeping.

* * *

The Hufflepuff common room had gone soft with evening by the time he came back. Firelight, damp shoes by the hearth, the low continuous mutter of revision. Somebody near the far wall was reading notes aloud to himself in a hoarse whisper, getting stuck on the same line every time and beginning again with visible resentment. Two fourth-years were sharing a bowl of stolen treacle tart with the solemn concentration of priests. A pair of socks had been hung too near the fire and were beginning to smell singed.

Ordinary. Not harmless, but ordinary.

Owen stood just inside the round doorway with his notebook under one arm and Tallow's wrapped bread in his pocket and let the room press against him. This was the part Prue had been asking him to admit scale for. Not abstract resistance. Not noble suffering in bulk. This room. Eli Mercer asleep face-down over half-finished History of Magic notes. Mae Doran filing her broken nails with the edge of a ruler because she could not think with roughness catching on parchment. The Hufflepuff clock a minute slow as it had been since October, nobody fixing it because everybody had got used to leaning around the error together.

If he crossed the room and sat, someone would make space. If he asked after the revised watch rota, somebody would answer. If he went looking properly, he could still find the Room of Requirement before curfew. He knew how to get to the Ravenclaw stair without being seen from the main landing. He knew, down to the hinge-creak, the route to every ordinary loyalty still available to him.

He went to the dormitory instead.

The room smelled of wool blanket, soap gone flat in the dish, and the damp mineral note that came in from the lake through cellar stone. His trunk was under the bed. The boards complained when he knelt. He took out the ash-linen notebook, the spare pencil shaved with a knife because sharpeners were unreliable, two lengths of candle, the bit of chalk wrapped in rag, and the cleanest bandage he had left. He added Tallow's bread. Then he stopped with his hand on the latch and looked at what he had made.

It looked, offensively, like preparation. Not obsession. Not panic. A kit.

On the bed opposite, somebody had left a Charms textbook open face-down at a page on shielding practice. A sock hung from the bedpost by one damp toe. Beneath the window sat an abandoned mug with a skin forming over the tea. The room was full of boys expecting tomorrow. Even fear here kept the form of tomorrow: essays, punishments, breakfast, revision, maybe a better day if they were stupid enough or sane enough to keep imagining one.

He sat on the edge of his bed and took out a sheet of parchment. For a long time he did not write. Then, very small, he put down Prue's name.

He stared at it until the letters looked like a copied exercise.

What could he say that was not another demand dressed as disclosure? Warn them, but he could not explain. Trust me, when trust was exactly what he had failed to make possible. I am sorry, which she already knew. Martha's name came to him next without permission, along with the sight of her at lunch slipping bread crust into her pocket for later, saving for scarcity even in a room with food on the table. He thought of the way she had once, in winter, shoved a bowl at him and told him to stop acting like being hungry was a moral refinement. He thought of Prue winding string into her fingers on the lake wall until honesty had cut a mark into her skin.

He turned the parchment over. Blank on both sides now.

Down in the common room someone laughed hard enough to choke. Somebody else told him to shut up. The sound came through the floorboards warm and distorted, human beyond argument.

Owen put the parchment away unwritten.

Then he opened the notebook and copied the route exactly as Tallow had given it, notating the hinge, the tiles, the drain, the iron brackets. Once the directions were down, his hand kept going. He added timings from the cellar stairs, probable choke points if the upper corridors jammed, the likely interval between first alarm and full crowding if the school had to move students quickly. Method came to him with hideous relief. It always had edges. It did not ask whether the thing being done deserved doing. Only whether it would hold.

When the notes were finished he sat with the pencil lifted and listened to the castle.

Above him, footsteps crossed and recrossed. Somewhere deeper on the right a pipe knocked twice, then settled. The lake pressed at the walls in its old slow rhythm. Underneath all that, or in between it, he could feel the other structure he had spent months teaching himself to notice: tension in the older stone, a wrongness not yet motion but preparing for it. The anchor was not a voice. It had no intention he could claim to hear. But it was under strain, and strain had its own texture. He knew it now the way a carpenter knew warp in timber by touch. The school could go on setting essays and baking bread and revising defensive charms right up to the hour something larger used all of that life as load.

Prue had offered him the human bargain. Help here first, then below. It had been a real bargain, not a test. That was what made refusing it so clean. No one was forcing the scale onto him in this room. No one was asking him to prove loyalty by speech. Martha was elsewhere in the castle, angry enough not to want him near her. Prue had walked away on her own feet. Neville barely counted him now except as a problem at the edge of planning. There was still, even so, a route back into ordinary obligation if he wanted it. He could choose to stand upstairs when the thing came. He could choose the school as the whole of the world because it was the world in front of him.

He looked down at his hand. Ink along the side of the finger. Chalk dust in the cut at his knuckle. The old burn in the palm gone pale and shiny where skin had healed wrong. A schoolboy's hand, except for the parts that were not.

Then he closed the notebook, packed the pencil into its spine, and slid the bundle under his bed where he could reach it in the dark.

When he went back through the common room, Mae looked up from her ruler-filed nails and said, "You all right?"

It was the easiest question in the world to answer badly.

"Yeah," he said. "Just tired."

She nodded and went back to her parchment. The room accepted the lie because rooms like this had to.

At the round door he paused. Left would take him toward the corridor that eventually rose to the rest of the school, to watchrooms, whispered plans, other bodies, the whole miserable beloved scale of immediate things. Right would take him past the kitchens, past the heat and the bread smell and the third rack in the scullery, down toward the blue-cracked tiles and the old dark waiting under the house.

He stood long enough that the fire behind him shifted and somebody complained of the draught.

Then he turned right.

Later, if there was one, would judge him or not. The choice itself was smaller and uglier than judgment. It was only a boy in a cellar taking the path he had prepared with his own hands. He went past the warm kitchen wall without stopping, found the service door by touch, and slipped through before he could look back toward the light.


# Chapter 15

The first sign was not the shouting. It was the floor.

Owen was crossing the Hufflepuff common room with his ash-linen notebook under his jumper, meaning to get back to the kitchens before curfew hardened into head count, when a low note went through the stone and up his legs. The nearest table rattled its teacups. A fourth-year girl revising Arithmancy put both hands flat on the page as if the castle might try to slide it away from her. Half a second later the old burn across Owen's right palm turned hot against the notebook's cover.

He stopped.

The room did not. Someone burst in from the cellar passage with his tie half off and shouted, "He's here."

That should have required a question. It did not. Every head came up at once. The common room, which had spent the year learning the downward shape of itself, altered in a single breath.

"Harry," the boy said, louder now because he had them. "Harry Potter. In the castle."

The name ran through the room like a match through dry lint. A first-year actually laughed, one quick bark of sound with terror under it. Two girls by the fireplace grabbed each other's sleeves. Someone said, "No, don't be thick," and someone else said, "Neville said it, I heard him," and chairs were already scraping back before any of them had decided what to do with their bodies.

Owen should have felt the same thing they did. He had spent months waiting for a break in the pattern, for some older and more dangerous person to kick a hole in the miserable ordinary of detentions, searches, and Amycus Carrow's breathing. Potter was exactly that kind of break. Potter in the castle meant the year had finally stopped pretending it could go on.

What Owen felt instead was the second pulse through the floor, harder than the first. The ward-scar in his hand tightened. Somewhere above them, high enough that the sound came down as a dull struck thud rather than a clear bang, old magic had begun taking new orders.

The anchor won't take a battle, he thought, and then, a beat behind it, Harry's here.

He was moving before he had decided to move. By the time he reached the cellar steps the passage was full of students pulling jumpers over their heads, dragging shoes on without laces, shouting contradictory versions of the same miracle.

"He got in through the portrait."

"No, he was in Ravenclaw Tower."

"Alecto Carrow's dead."

"Nobody said dead, don't say dead."

"Snape's gone up."

"Shut up and move."

Hope moved faster than instruction. It made everyone clumsy. It made them bright. A boy from the year below Owen, who had spent most of March speaking with one side of his mouth because Amycus had cracked a tooth, ran past grinning so hard the broken edge showed white in the torchlight. Owen had never seen him grin before. The sight of it landed in the chest like grief.

He climbed into the crush. The stone kept talking through the soles of his shoes. Every time a stair took a new load, every time some higher passage woke an old defensive charm, he felt the answer in his bad hand and along his molars. Hogwarts had room for punishing children, for corridor curses, for the Carrows' little public festivals of pain. It had been carrying those all year. This was different. This was the whole castle drawing breath.

At the first turn above the cellar, a portrait of a Tudor witch flung her own painted door open and cried, "They're rousing the statues," to nobody Owen could see. Three portraits away, a friar was ringing an invisible handbell with such conviction that the frame shook.

By the time Owen hit the wider corridor near the kitchens, the change had become visible. Armour that had spent the year standing decorative and stupid along the walls was no longer still. One suit rolled its shoulders with a metal groan. Another stepped down off its plinth and nearly trod on a Ravenclaw's foot. The torches had gone brighter without anyone touching them. Farther off, up toward the moving staircases, a woman's voice cracked through the air in clipped command and was answered by a thunder of feet.

The castle was choosing sides.

He got carried with the flow as far as the broad landing below the Entrance Hall before he could cut free. Bodies kept knocking him off line: girls in dressing gowns over trousers, boys with school ties hanging loose and wands gripped wrong from haste, a pair of sixth-years hauling a box of green-glass potion phials between them. Nobody looked like a soldier. That was the worst part. Nobody had turned noble or abstract on the strength of Potter's name. They were still the same underslept, half-fed schoolchildren they had been an hour earlier, only now they were moving with direction.

Neville stood three steps up from the landing, making direction by force.

He had lost a button from his shirt and not noticed. His sleeves were rolled unevenly. A broken watch with a frayed leather strap kept sliding out from under his cuff, and every few seconds, between one instruction and the next, he shoved it back with the heel of his hand as if time might start behaving if he bullied it hard enough. His face was bruised yellow and purple under one eye. He looked older than seventeen and much more tired, but there was nothing grand about him. He was simply the person who had been doing this all year and was now doing it with the walls awake around him.

"If you're leaving, leave now," he was saying. "If you're staying, get armed and get back. First-years go with the little ones from Ravenclaw. Herbology lot, west corridor. No, not the front doors, don't be stupid, they'll watch the front doors first. Move."

Martha was below him on the landing with a roll of bandages under one arm and a canvas satchel bumping her hip. Her dark plait had mostly come loose and kept catching at the corner of her mouth when she turned her head. She kept spitting hair out of the way without using her hands because both hands were busy: one holding the satchel open, the other shoving things into it as people passed, dittany, strips of linen, a squat jar of honey-colored salve, a packet of biscuits flattened nearly to crumbs.

A first-year Hufflepuff girl stood frozen at Martha's elbow with tears hanging off her chin. Martha pulled one of the crushed biscuits free, shoved it into the girl's hand, and said, "Eat. I know it tastes like wall, just eat it. Then go with Dennis, all right? Don't stand here looking at me."

The girl nodded and went.

Neville leaned down without breaking stride and caught Martha's sleeve. "Your leg."

"Still attached."

"That wasn't the question."

"It's my hip tonight, actually." She winced as she shifted her weight, then looked annoyed at her own face for doing it. "I'm fine. Keep shouting at people."

For half a second his hand stayed on her arm. It was not leadership or symbol. It was just Neville making sure Martha was still upright before he let the next demand have him. Then somebody yelled from above that the courtyard wanted more bodies and he turned away.

Martha saw Owen in the same instant.

Whatever she might have said to him a week ago was not available now. There was too much happening for the luxury of old anger. Her eyes hit him, assessed him, and moved on again in a single hard motion.

"Phipps," Neville said, seeing him because Neville was seeing everyone. "Good. Get to the kitchens. We need anything they can spare, bandages as well if Tallow's got sense enough not to hide them. Then come up to the courtyard."

The floor answered with another long subterranean shiver. Owen's burned palm flashed hot.

He knew, all at once and with the sick steadiness of a calculation finally resolving, that the first battle-spell had not even been thrown yet. This was only preparation: chairs being dragged, shields waking, professors pulling old protections back into use. The true load had not arrived. If he waited for the first curses to start crossing the grounds, if he waited for what everyone else meant by the battle, he would be too late.

He said, "I need to go below."

Neville was already looking past him to the next face. "Below, above, I don't care, just get through the kitchens faster. Move."

It was so nearly permission that Owen hated him for it.

Martha had heard. He saw that much in the small flattening of her mouth. But two boys came up with a crate between them and one had blood running into his sock, and she bent at once to deal with that instead. Owen stood there long enough to feel useless and seen, then slipped out of the stream and back toward the kitchen passage while the whole landing kept pouring upward.

On the turn down, he looked back once.

Neville was still on the stair, voice gone rough already, one hand lifted to stop a panic before it started. Martha had braced the satchel against her bad hip and was tying a bandage around the ankle of the bleeding boy with quick angry fingers because anger was what she had left for fear. Somebody farther up was laughing too hard. Somebody else was crying and pretending not to. Harry Potter's name kept erupting in scraps as if saying it were the same as loading a wand.

This, Owen thought with a clarity almost obscene in its calm, is what it looks like when the entire school agrees on the wrong emergency.

Then he hated himself for that as well, and kept going.

***

The kitchens were no refuge from it. They had become a lung.

Heat hit him first, thick with steam and yeast and wet wool. Then sound: pans, shouted orders, boots on stone, house-elves underfoot, the hollow iron noise of cauldrons being lifted too fast. The long worktables that usually held bread and pies now carried armfuls of torn linen, baskets of potatoes, stacks of plates nobody had bothered to clear, and one alarming mound of old silver cutlery that had apparently been collected because in a crisis everything became either food, bandage, or weapon.

Tallow stood on a flour bin near the central range so he could see over the human bodies blundering through his workspace. He had tied a strip of blue cloth around one singed ear to keep it folded back from the heat, and his apron was dusted white to the knees. One hand held a ladle. The other held a spoon blackened at the bowl where something had caught and burned. He was staring at a cauldron as if personal insult had risen off it in the steam.

"Lower that flame," he snapped at an older Slytherin who was trying to help and failing. "It's soup, not vengeance. If it splits now, you'll be feeding fighters greasy water and Tallow won't be blamed for your panic."

The Slytherin lowered the flame.

Tallow looked up and found Owen. Whatever warmth he had once reserved for him did not appear.

"Young sir," he said. "You are either late for carrying or early for hiding."

Owen pushed through to the edge of the bin. "I need the route."

Tallow's eyes flicked once toward the ceiling, where a deep impact had just rolled dust from the joints between stones. The house-elf flinched, almost too small to see unless you were already looking for it.

"Of course you do," he said. "Because there is a war in the dining rooms and a clever boy always wants the passage under the spoons."

"Tallow."

"Do not call me Tallow like that as if it shortens the sentence." He set the burned spoon down with exaggerated care. "Third flue beyond the scullery. Left of the coal bins. The house is tightening there, so mind your shoulders. And if a rung gives under you, you deserve the bruise."

Owen started to move. Tallow spoke again, quieter.

"The route is open. That is not the same as blessing it."

There was no answer to that which would not sound childish. Owen gave a brief useless nod and turned away.

Students kept forcing him sideways as he cut across the kitchen. A pair of Gryffindors wrestled a cauldron of boiling water toward the service stair, red-faced with steam and fright. A house-elf ran under Owen's elbow with a sack of crusts nearly as big as his own body. Somebody had started slicing bread on the nearest table and left the knife standing upright in the loaf because there had not been time to lay it down properly. Every person in the room seemed to be hurrying toward the fight or hurrying so that somebody else could.

Owen was the only one trying to pass through it in the opposite moral direction.

That was what made the shame feel clean. It was not abstract or philosophical. A Hufflepuff third-year he half recognized shoved a stack of folded cloth into his chest, assuming he was part of the same traffic. Owen caught it on reflex.

"Upstairs, please," she said, breathless. She had tied her dressing gown shut with green twine. "Hospital wing's already full."

"Right," he said.

He set the cloth down on the next table and hated the soft cowardice of the word before it had finished leaving his mouth.

The scullery passage was narrower and less bright. Steam filmed the stone. The floor had gone slick with old water and fresh ash tracked in from above. Owen ducked past the coal bins, found the third flue behind a stack of dented tureens, and got both hands on the iron ladder bolted into the throat of the wall.

Another impact ran through the castle.

It was not close yet, but it was stronger. The iron bit his palms. In his right hand the old burn answered so hard he had to close his teeth against the sound that wanted to come out.

He put one foot on the first rung.

"No," Prue said from behind him. "No. Stop there."

He turned too fast and cracked the back of his head on the flue wall. For a stupid second all he saw was black brick and orange sparks.

Prue stood at the mouth of the passage with one hand braced on the coal bin as if she had run the whole way from somewhere higher and only just bullied her lungs into silence. Her spectacles were in place but badly smeared, one lens grey with kitchen steam and fingerprint. Her curls had come loose from whatever she had tried to do to them and were frizzing damply around her face. She had an old book satchel across her body, the strap twisted, and three stiff index cards were still stuck under the buckle from sheer habit or accident. In one hand she held her wand. In the other, absurdly, a stub of carpenter's pencil.

He said, "What are you doing here?"

"Following the obvious," she said, still breathing harder than she wanted him to notice. "You vanished the second Neville gave you a job. There are only so many varieties of awful."

Above them, feet hammered the service stair. Someone shouted for more buckets. The sound came through the stone blurred and bodied, as if the castle were swallowing the words before handing them down.

Owen stepped off the rung but did not come away from the flue. "I don't have time for this."

"That isn't a serious sentence."

"It's true."

"Yes, and so is half the rubbish in your notebook. That doesn't make it the point."

He might have laughed on another night. He did not have it in him. "Prue."

"No. You don't get to use my name like a pause." She pushed her glasses up with the back of her wrist because the hand holding the pencil was shaking slightly. "Tell me properly. Are you actually going below now?"

"Yes."

"Because Potter is here."

"Because the whole castle's waking up."

"That isn't different enough."

Another shudder moved through the wall. This one came with grit. Dust loosened from the ceiling and caught in Prue's hair. Owen felt it in his teeth before he felt it in his feet.

"It is to me," he said. "Listen. If they start throwing battle magic over the grounds in earnest, if the professors bring the outer protections all the way up, if the defenders start answering at scale, the anchor will light. I don't mean metaphorically. It'll go bright enough to widen the seam below it."

Prue stared at him. The pencil in her hand left a grey mark on her thumb where she was gripping it too hard.

"You never said tonight."

"I only knew it was battle-scale. This is battle-scale."

"And everybody else gets to hear that now? In a coal passage?"

"There wasn't time before."

"There was all of spring before." Her voice stayed low, which made the anger in it worse. "There were meals and stairs and that whole horrible conversation by the lake where I practically did the work of begging for you. There was time."

A pair of sixth-years came pounding down the service stair carrying a crate of bottles between them. Prue had to flatten against the wall to let them pass. Owen turned sideways as well, and for an instant they were pressed almost shoulder to shoulder, not touching, both forced to face the damp stone while the crate knocked by and one of the boys muttered, "Sorry, sorry, sorry," without meaning any one apology more than the others.

When the passage cleared, Prue looked at him again and there was something flatter now under the anger. Something wounded enough to have gone careful.

"How bad is it?" she asked.

He answered the most literal version, because that was what he did when the real version hurt too much. "Bad enough that if I wait for the first proper exchange outside, I may not get a workable interval below at all."

"That's still not what I asked."

He scrubbed the back of his wrist under his nose. It came away with a thin line of blood. He had been getting nosebleeds for weeks. Tonight the blood looked black in the kitchen light.

"Bad," he said. "The kind where if it opens properly we're not talking about one school or one war anymore."

Prue shut her eyes for a second. When she opened them again she had made herself quieter.

"All right," she said. "Fine. All right. Then hear me before you start explaining thresholds at me. The people upstairs aren't incidental to this because your scale has gone strange. Neville's up there. Martha's up there on one leg and temper alone. Half the school is about to decide, out loud and in public, whether they mean to be brave. If you go under now, you aren't simply being busy. You're leaving."

The word landed cleanly. That was one of the things she was good at: not wit, not performance, just finding the word he had been trying to step around.

He said, "I'm trying to stop something larger."

"Yes. I know. I know that. Stop talking as if I need the premise again." She swallowed, then started over less neatly. "I'm saying the largeness doesn't cancel the rest. It doesn't make this decorative. You don't get to turn everybody you know into background noise because the universe has produced a louder problem."

The next impact was close enough to send a proper tremor up the ladder. Somewhere above, iron clanged on stone in a long skidding complaint. The battle had not fully begun, not yet, but it was touching the edges of beginning. He could feel the old architecture taking weight faster than it could settle it.

He looked past Prue toward the brighter end of the passage. Through the steam and moving bodies he could still glimpse the kitchen mouth, the wild traffic of cloth and water and hands. Somebody laughed again. Somebody was sick into a bucket. Tallow's voice cut through both.

For one terrible second he wanted her to make it easier by being wrong.

Instead she said, "Come upstairs now. Fight, carry, do whatever useless human thing presents itself. Then, if we live long enough for there to be a then, I'll go below with you. I mean it this time too. I'm still saying yes to the bigger thing. But not like this."

She had offered him almost exactly the bargain he had failed to take before, only stripped cleaner now by urgency. There was no spring softness around it, no almost-kiss hanging over the sentence. There were just terms.

If he had taken them a month earlier, perhaps they would have belonged to two people still moving toward one another. Tonight they belonged to witness.

He said, "If I come back after it starts properly, I won't be able to hold the chamber steady enough."

"You don't know that."

"I know enough."

"No, you know enough to prefer your chances below to everybody else's chances above." The pencil snapped quietly in her fist. She looked at the two pieces as if surprised to find them there, then dropped them. "Sorry. That was cheap. It was also true."

He should have defended himself. He had the language ready: load, thresholds, seam, brightness, the way an old system failed not dramatically but by becoming legible to the wrong attention. All of it was real. None of it altered what she had just said.

He managed, "If I don't go now, the repair may stop being possible."

"Then let it stop for ten minutes."

He stared at her.

She shook her head, angry at her own sentence even while she stood by it. "Not forever. I'm not saying abandon it and you know I'm not. I'm saying there are moments when the smaller field is the one you're standing in. This is one of them."

Above them, from somewhere out beyond the kitchens, a surge of sound rose and broke into many separate noises at once: cheering, screaming, a command Owen could not make out, then the unmistakable crack of curses hitting shield or stone. Dust fell in a soft rush from the flue throat onto his shoulder.

That was it. It was the first proper answer from the grounds. The school war had become an actual battle.

The heat in his hand turned savage.

Owen caught the ladder before the sudden weakness in his knees could show on his face. Below that shaking iron, below the kitchen, below the service stone, he could almost feel the anchor responding like a live thing under a knife.

"It's begun," he said.

Prue's voice went raw for the first time. "I know it's begun. That's why I'm here."

A line of older students forced down into the passage carrying sacks of sand and two shields between them. Owen and Prue had to flatten apart to let them through. One of the shields clipped Owen's shoulder hard enough to spin him half back toward the ladder. A girl at the rear saw where he was facing and did a double take that was almost comic in its plainness.

"Are you going down?" she asked, because people under pressure said obvious things.

Prue answered for him without taking her eyes off his face. "Not if he's got any sense."

The girl nodded as if that settled it and kept moving.

When they were alone again, Prue said, much quieter, "You can still turn round."

He could. That was the hideous part. Neville was three corridors away. Martha was somewhere above the kitchen traffic, probably swearing at anyone who tried to take the satchel off her. He could go back into the human emergency with both hands and whatever courage he possessed. He could spend himself there. He could be one more boy in a school full of frightened boys pretending not to be frightened.

And below them the deeper seam was opening on its appointed logic, indifferent to courage, indifferent to Potter, indifferent to every name the castle was currently using to save itself.

He heard himself say, very calmly because panic would only have sounded like a lie, "No. I'm not turning back."

Prue's face did not change at once. Then it did, subtly and much worse than tears would have been. She became more exact.

"Right," she said. "I wanted you to say it straight, that's all."

He should have said something then. He should have said he was sorry, or told her to come with him, or asked her to tell Neville. He should have said anything. Instead he put his foot back on the rung.

Prue did not grab him. That would have been mercy. She moved one step aside so he could pass cleanly if he meant it.

"Owen."

He looked down.

"When you hear them," she said, "don't translate it into architecture."

Then she held the passage and the kitchen light behind her, and he climbed down into the wall.


# Chapter 16

Smoke had got down even here.

It came under the kitchen doors, along the service flues, through the crooked throat of the passage Tallow had shown him in winter, until the East Undercroft smelled less of wet mineral and old lime and more of a hearth gone wrong. Owen reached the last squeeze of stone with his satchel banging his hip and his lungs already full of soot. Every curse cast above travelled through the castle first as a noise and then as a pressure. The noise came thin and distorted, a scatter of muffled shouts and breaking timber. The pressure arrived in his teeth, in the sore hinge of his jaw, in the way the wall against his shoulder seemed to jump a fraction and settle again.

He turned sideways through the final slit and almost stepped into Tallow.

The house-elf had stopped at the threshold where the old work began to hurt in earnest. He was holding a dented pudding basin wrapped in a tea towel, as if he had simply taken a wrong turn while carrying something out of the scullery. His singed ears lay flat. Damp curls of soot stuck to his cheeks. There was still flour dried in the crease of one wrist.

"You're late," Tallow said.

"I know. Sorry."

"No need to tell Tallow what he knows." The elf looked past him into the passage Owen had come down, as if he expected the battle itself to push through after him. "The potatoes went to mush and then caught. One pan cracked. Nobody cared. That's how bad it is upstairs."

The basin in his hands held what Owen had asked for hours ago and had not expected to receive now that the school was at war: chalk snapped down to stubs, a skin of water grey with stone dust, two folded rags, a narrow kitchen knife with the handle dark from years of washing, and a stoppered jar of coarse salt. Tallow had laid them out neatly anyway. Of course he had.

"You shouldn't have come this far," Owen said.

Tallow made a face that might have been irritation and might have been pain. "And you shouldn't drip blood all over a flag joint older than your Headmaster. We're both being unwise tonight."

Another impact travelled down through the vault. Dust sifted from the curve of the ceiling and stippled the elf's tea towel. Tallow flinched, then smoothed the cloth flat with the side of his hand, fussy even now.

"Is it worse?" Owen asked.

"The house is too bright." Tallow's eyes did not leave Owen's face. "The moving staircases are sulking, half the second-floor lamps burst together, and the north passage by the Charms corridor has started humming like a kettle. There are children running where there ought not to be children. There are grown wizards running where there ought not to be grown wizards. The ovens keep drawing smoke down instead of up." He pushed the basin into Owen's hands. "If you can mend the lower thing, mend it. But mind yourself while you do it. The old stones are hungry tonight."

That was as close to pleading as Tallow had ever sounded.

Owen took the basin. The metal knocked lightly against his cracked nails. "All right."

Tallow hesitated. The old-work pressure had already made his breathing shallow. Even so, he reached out and pinched soot from Owen's sleeve as if Owen were about to embarrass him in front of guests.

"You've got ash on the cuff," he said. "It smears the pages."

Then he backed away up the passage, one hand against the wall, his expression set into the formal blankness he used when pain had outrun manners. Owen watched him go until the dark swallowed the twitch of the tea towel over his shoulder. After that there was only the undercroft and the battle above it.

The East Undercroft was not a chamber made for comfort, secrecy, or worship. It had none of the theatrics school legends would have given it. It was a built cavity under the castle, circular and low-vaulted, with founder-era ribs in the ceiling and damp shining at the joints. The floor had been cut into rings and channels so old that even the damage looked ancient. At the centre the anchor stone rose from the ground in a blunt canted mass of dark mineral, shoulder-high on Owen, too large to be an object and too specific to be natural. When he had first found it, months ago, it had seemed dead in the way machinery looks dead when nobody remembers the switch. The channels had been there. The star-table had been there. The pressure in the body had been there too: hot stone, ringing ears, the faint nausea of being somewhere reality had been persuaded into a stricter shape than the human body liked. But the anchor itself had stayed stubbornly blank.

Tonight it was lit from within by the castle's distress.

It was not bright, which would have made it easier. The grooves across its face held a dim metal wash, as if quicksilver had been rubbed into them and mostly wiped away. The floor-rings around it came and went with every heavy impact overhead. A hairline crack Owen had only inferred before now lay plain across one angle of the stone, no wider than a thread at first glance and then, when the next concussion struck somewhere up in the school, opening to the width of a fingernail before drawing tight again.

There you are, he thought, with the hard ugly satisfaction of finally seeing a thing he had been right about for too long.

He set the basin down on the outer ring and unshouldered his satchel. The Grinlow Leaves came out wrapped in his spare shirt. His ash-linen notebook came after, swollen with inserted pages and singed at two corners. He laid both on the driest stretch of floor he could find.
