When he tried for wandlight on instinct, the spell broke in his grip into a red spit of sparks and a smell like singed copper. He swore softly, shook the sting out of his fingers, and dragged one of Tallow's rags through the grey water instead.

The work did not want wizard theatre. It wanted surfaces.

He knelt on damp stone and scrubbed at the ring nearest the anchor until grit came up under the rag and the old cut lines showed cleaner. He wiped the face of the stone where the seam pulsed. He cleaned a flat patch for the manuscript. Dust kept falling. Once, a distant detonation above made a fresh veil of white sift over the place he had just cleared. He scrubbed it again. By the time the ring was usable, soot had printed the heel of his hand black and his knees ached from the pressure of the floor.

He opened the Leaves with his burned palm.

The pages that had stayed blind in the greenhouse and only half-yielded in the Departure Chamber now came up dense at once. Ink darkened out of the fibres in layered tables, old notation crossing itself, marginal hooks answering the geometry in the stone at his knees. The active node had given the manuscript teeth. Whole columns that had been pallid three days ago were suddenly legible enough to hurt. He found the sequence he had prepared in his notebook and then found more under it, conditional branches that had not existed until the castle met whatever threshold the battle had satisfied.

It was siege, he thought, or something close enough for founder work.

A thud passed through the ribs of the ceiling so hard that the water in the pudding basin jumped. A second followed before the first had finished travelling through the stone. The seam flashed wider and did not entirely close this time. Owen felt the old work gather under his hands, not welcoming, not exactly, but available. Hogwarts was close enough to breaking that the lower machinery had stopped pretending it did not need him.

He let out one breath, then another, and rolled his sleeves to the elbow.

He had his window because children and murderers were hitting the castle hard enough to wake the foundations.

* * *

The first difficulty was blood.

The Leaves had been clear enough about that much from the start. The work did not always require blood, it did not require it for every amendment, and it did not require it in the theatrical sense boys in dormitories whispered about when they wanted to feel wicked without understanding anything. The work wanted claim. It wanted a body to answer for the sentence being laid into the world. Founder stone could carry more of the burden than bare wood or open air, but not all of it. For anything beyond the botched little changes he had made in the greenhouse and corridors, the cost had to stick somewhere living first.

Owen dried the kitchen knife on his sleeve and set the edge against the thickened scar across his right palm.

He had expected the old burn to split reluctantly. Instead it opened almost at once, as if the scar had been waiting for a better excuse than adolescence and homework. Dark blood welled into the white seam and ran down to his wrist. He hissed, stupidly more from surprise than pain, and held his hand over the basin while the first drops hit the chalk-streaked water and unclouded it into threads of pink.

That, at least, was legible.

He stirred chalk and blood together with the knife tip until it made a thin slurry, not paint and not quite paste. The outer ring wanted width more than intricacy. He dipped two fingers into the mixture and began laying it into the cut channel circling the anchor. The line had to be unbroken. Twice the floor shuddered hard enough to make him skid outside the groove, and twice he wiped the mistake away with the heel of his hand and went over it again. By the time he completed the ring his fingertips were white and red together, the chalk drying his skin to paper.

He moved inward.

Nothing about the amendment resembled spellcasting once it was actually happening. There was no sensation of power gathering cleanly at the wand arm. There was no obedient wait in the air for the right syllables. It felt more like aligning a cracked weight-bearing wall while the rest of the building was being shelled. He had to persuade the categories to meet. That was the only language his mind ever found for it, however little it resembled school magic.

He could not try to mend the ward. That was too large and too vain and would have killed him inside a minute.

He could not try to save the castle. The castle did not require saving in the sentimental sense, and sentiment was poison in this work.

He went smaller until the terms fitted.

He aimed at this seam, this local failure of counted enclosure, this refusal at one edge of one node to go on meaning hidden when pressured to brightness from outside and violence from within.

He wrote the narrowed claim down in the notation he actually possessed: Meridian hooks where he trusted them, translated English in his notebook margin where he did not, old school Latin only when the Leaves and the anchor table both supported the same inference. He spoke as he wrote, quietly at first and then louder when the chamber kept trying to drown him out.

"As bounded, remain bounded. As sheltered in stone, remain counted under shelter. Let the broken edge answer to the held edge."

The words were not the amendment. They were the handhold that let his mind keep the shape from blowing apart.

He drew a second line from the outer ring to the seam's lower fork, then a third toward the star-cut nearest the anchor's western face. The moment the third line connected, the stone answered with pressure rather than light.

Something pushed up through his hands and into his forearms, a low hard surge that made the joints in his wrists feel threaded too tight. The chamber hummed. A crack raced in one of the damp wall-joints and sealed again. Somewhere far above, several spells failed at once. He knew that not because he could see them but because the backlash came through the castle like a missed step through rotten stairs. Conventional magic did not like old work. Conventional magic never had.

He nearly lost the line there. The wording in his head blurred. For a few ugly seconds the symbols on the page looked as arbitrary as scratches in frost. Then another impact overhead widened the seam again and the geometry became obvious in the brutal way only panic ever made things obvious to him. He bent lower, blood dripping off his hand onto the stone, and corrected the branch he had misread.

"Not the whole join," he muttered. "Just the edge. Just this edge. Don't be stupid."

He was speaking to himself. He was speaking to the castle. The distinction had stopped mattering.

The next ten minutes, or forty, or three, had no sensible shape. Time in the undercroft broke down into labour units: redraw, check, phrase, feel for answer, repair the place where the answer had come through too violently, start again before the whole thing sloughed off. Smoke kept descending in erratic breaths from the passage behind him. Dust gathered in the wet channels and had to be blown clear. Once his nose started bleeding and would not stop, hot threads down over his upper lip and into his mouth. He wiped it away on his shoulder without looking up. The blood helped and that made him hate it.

The battle above never resolved into scenes. It only kept producing evidence. A run of impacts on the same side of the castle sent a long groan through the ceiling ribs and shook loose chips of mortar that pattered into the chalk like hail. A sound reached him down one service flue so cleanly that for a second it might have been in the room itself: someone screaming a wordless full-throated scream and then cutting off in the middle. The next concussion came half a breath later, heavy enough to ring the anchor like struck iron.

Owen did not look upward. There was no upward to look at.

He kept working until one section of the seam stopped flickering under pressure and held the same width through three impacts in a row.

That was the first real success.

He knew it bodily before he knew it in words. The nausea eased by a degree. The ringing in his ears changed pitch. The anchor's western face, which had been spitting dim metallic pulses at random, settled into a steadier wash. The repaired edge did not heal exactly, because healing was a human metaphor and the stone had no use for it. But one side of the failure stopped behaving like an open mouth.

And because that side held, something else in the table declared itself.

It rose first on the manuscript page as a set of marginal bars he had taken for damage. Then the same pattern came up in the stone to his left, under a film of dust he had not bothered to wipe because it had seemed irrelevant. Owen stared, breathing through his mouth, while the branch clarified.

It was not Meridian in origin. The syntax sat closer to the local work woven through Hogwarts by people who had cared about rooms and bodies and about who was permitted to do what under a school roof. A founder clause nested inside the broader anchor relation, dormant until the castle entered a condition near enough to open siege that its own protections began offering older remedies.

He wiped the dust off with his sleeve.

The clause read: hostile wandwork within the held bounds may be suspended by the house under direct claim.

That did not mean disarmed. It did not mean prohibited. It meant suspended.

The branch beneath it resolved further as he watched, legal in the old practical way of architectural magic. Hostile was not mood. It was intent joined to act. Wandwork was not possession. It was the active relation between wielder, instrument, and aimed harm. Suspended did not mean shattered. It meant the relation stopped signifying while the clause held. A wand raised to curse would have all the magical authority of a spoon.

Owen sat back on his heels so abruptly that the chamber tilted.

He knew at once that the reading was sound. The anchor told him so in the way the right answer always felt hateful: too clean once seen. Hogwarts had an old siege-condition buried in its protections, and the battle itself had brought it above threshold. With the branch open, with the authority he already had half-built under his hands, he could reach through the castle and cancel hostile wand relation inside the bounds.

Every duel, every Killing Curse, every jinx half-formed in a corridor, every shield meant against a second spell, every wand turning in a frightened hand toward someone else's body would fail together. The whole war above him would collapse into shouting and dropped wood in less time than it took Neville to count a retreat.

Another impact struck somewhere close enough to shed grit over the page. The new clause flared silver-grey in the stone.

It was not hypothetical. It was there. It wanted saying.

* * *

He had one live working and two possible sentences.

That was the shape of it once he forced himself to stop staring.

The seam he had caught on the western face was still only a caught edge. If he took both hands away from it, if he let the phrasing blur for long enough to retune the relation entirely, the repair would not wait politely in place. It would sag back open. He had never held this much old work before. His forearms already trembled with it. The cracked beds of his nails throbbed in time with the anchor hum. The taste in his mouth had gone from blood to chalk to something stranger, a flat mineral bitterness as if the chamber air were ground from old mortar and swallowed directly.

He turned the choice over anyway, because not to do so would have been a lie so childish even he could not manage it.

There were ways to fake ignorance. He had used them all year. Narrow the question. Answer the smaller thing. Pretend timing was the only issue. Pretend a task had no moral surface because it had technical depth. None of that worked here. The branch sat in the stone beside him, exact and bright. Upstairs, people were already dying. The clause would help them. He knew it. He knew it with the same certainty with which he knew the outer ward crack had widened in April. A few more minutes of the battle at its present pitch and the seam under his left hand would start failing faster than he could correct.

He could not have both.

That was the cruel mechanical fact of it, and because it was mechanical he trusted it. He could either spend this authority locally, on the school war everybody else understood, or he could keep the sentence narrow and force one edge of the larger breach to remain answerable. There might be a world in which a stronger practitioner could have split attention and done both. There was not a world in which Owen Phipps, fifteen, half-bleeding into a chalk ring under a collapsing school, could do it.

The clause for hostile wandwork opened under his fingers like a second handle.

He touched it.

The response was immediate and monstrous in its intimacy. What the anchor offered was relation, not faces or names or anything human enough to comfort him. There were hundreds of active lines in the castle above, each one a wand made meaningful by intent to harm. They were not visible exactly, but they were present in the way a net is present when you run a hand along the knots. The school was full of strained wood, aimed cores, curses already choosing routes. If he took hold of the branch and finished the sentence, the relation would collapse. The wands would remain in people's hands. The magic in them would remain magic. But the hostile use would stop counting as a thing the house had to permit.

He saw, in the ugly cold shorthand the First Art always preferred, how quickly it would happen: spells guttering into sparks, duels failing all at once, somebody's murderous certainty turning in the grip to confusion and then to fear. Even Voldemort's war had to pass through means. The clause did not care who called himself destiny. It only cared what sort of act Hogwarts was willing to host.

A distant boom travelled through the floor so heavily that the new branch brightened to white for an instant.

He thought of Neville because the castle above had reorganised itself in his head by voices over the year, and Neville's was the plain one that always kept coming back when action needed doing. He thought of Martha's limp and the way she had stood angled on bad weight without complaint because complaint wasted time. He thought, against his will, of Prue in the corridor outside the kitchens less than an hour ago, curls damp against her temple, speaking to him with the precision she used when she was trying not to sound frightened. The thought did not soften him. It only made the terms worse.

If he turned the authority sideways now, they might live.

If he let the seam go, the larger breach remained what it was: Earth flashing brighter to the thing outside, the old masking structure one hard battle closer to tearing where no corridor heroics could reach it.

A scream carried down the flue again. This one did not cut off cleanly. It thinned into the kind of ragged shouting that meant several people at once and no room left for dignity.

Owen put his bleeding hand flat over the hostile-wand branch and began, under his breath, to shape the opening claim.

The anchor lurched toward him.

It did not do so bodily. It did so categorically. The live seam under his other hand loosened at once, the repaired edge slackening like stitching pulled from wet cloth. He felt the outer relation shift, the held margin giving way because he had stopped asking it to remain held and had offered the stone a different priority. It would take so little. That was the most terrible part. It would take one more line into the branch, one full sentence of direct claim. The battle above would break open into useless wood and empty hands while the older wound widened quietly below.

He stopped.

For a second he stayed bent over both possibilities, not moving, the opening words of the clause sour in his mouth.

Then he took his hand off the new branch.

The slack in the repaired seam turned instantly savage, as if the stone were punishing indecision. Owen swore, shoved two fingers back into the chalk-and-blood slurry, and rammed the line deeper into the western fork to keep the edge from unravelling altogether. The correction cost him a fresh burst of pain up both arms. His nose bled harder. One of his thumbnail beds split wide enough to wet the stone.

He knew what he had just done. More precisely, he knew what he had just not done.

Above him the battle went on at full pitch. The clause beside his elbow remained legible, ready, entirely valid. He had not rejected it because it would fail. He had not rejected it because he had run out of time before understanding it. He had not rejected it because nobody downstairs had spelled out the consequence clearly enough. He had understood. He had begun. He had chosen the other sentence.

The recognition passed through him cleanly and left no room for self-pity.

Another blow landed somewhere in the school and sent a wave of dust across the floor. It thickened the edge of the hostile-wand branch until the symbols looked almost frosted. Owen did not wipe them clear this time. He bent back over the seam, shoulders hunched, and narrowed his attention again to this edge, this bound, this held relation under stress.

He spoke the repair phrasing louder now because the chamber had become difficult to hear inside. Each word scraped his throat raw with soot. He fed the next line from the outer ring into the western face. He pressed his reopened palm against the crack until blood slicked into the cut channel and the stone accepted it. The anchor answered with a hard descending hum that ran through the floor and up the walls. Somewhere above, conventional magic buckled in reply. He felt it in the undercroft as a long shiver of failed compatibility moving through stair, corridor, classroom, hall.

Good, he thought with a viciousness that surprised him. Answer, then.

It was not bravery. It was not sacrifice. It was work, and he treated it as work because that was the only way to keep going.

He built the next sentence smaller than the last, precise enough that it could survive his exhaustion. The repaired edge tightened. The hairline crack across the anchor's face reduced from a fingernail width to something thread-fine and stubborn. It still existed. Of course it did. He was patching, not restoring. But the western side now held through impact after impact without opening all the way to brightness.

By the time he trusted that much, both sleeves were stiff with chalk and blood. The kitchen knife had fallen somewhere into the outer dark. The basin water was almost black. His hands no longer felt attached at the wrists in an entirely ordinary way. When he flexed his wand fingers, the air around them fuzzed faintly, as if the old work had left an afterimage in his magical signature and not yet decided whether to depart.

The hostile-wand branch stayed lit on the anchor's left face.

Once, as he shifted his weight, his sleeve brushed it and came away silver-grey with stone dust. He looked at the mark, then looked back to the seam.

Above him people kept killing and being killed by means he could have interrupted.

He pressed his bleeding palm back to the older wound and kept speaking.


# Chapter 17

The chamber had no proper weather of its own, but battle made one above it. Dust kept sifting out of the seams in the roof in thin grey veils. The stone under Owen's knees answered distant impacts a half second late, as if the castle were taking blows somewhere else and deciding whether to pass them on. Every time the anchor caught a fresh flare from the fighting overhead, the carved ribs around it lit fox-red and the open pages of the Grinlow Leaves showed more script than they had any right to hold. He had been living for hours inside that ugly bargain: let the school break hard enough, and the deeper work became legible.

He knelt in the founder-cut ring with his right hand laid flat on the anchor stone and his left braced against the floorboards of notation he had scratched over the flags in chalk, soot, and blood. Blood had dried under his fingernails in dark crescents and then opened again. His nose had stopped on its own and started twice more. The front of his shirt was stiff where he had wiped his face without thinking. Every breath brought mineral damp, old lake-cold, and the singed-metal smell that came when the ward edge rubbed against his amendment and failed to tear.

It was not mended. It was only no longer widening as fast.

He kept the phrasing narrow because narrow held. He did not ask the world to heal, or protect, or save. Those were theatrical words and the First Art disliked them for the same reason good tools disliked vague hands. He asked for continuance at the seam. For dimness. For a lower answer where the breach had begun to shine. Each clause had to be braced against the next before the previous one slipped. He could feel the whole lattice in his teeth. When his attention blurred, the stone under his palm went hot enough to make the old burn wake and pulse like a second mouth.

Something in the passage scraped.

At first he sorted it with the other noises. Overhead collapse. A fallen suit of armour. One of the service grates knocking loose in the draft. Then it came again: the particular drag and halt of a person turning sideways in the narrow run between the cracked lintel and the old boiler flue. He knew that sound because he had once gone first with a lamp and Prue behind him swearing softly into the back of his jumper every time the stone caught her spectacles.

He looked up too quickly. The chamber tilted, then settled.

Prue came through the low opening with one hand on the wall and the other still clenched around her wand. Dust had turned the dark curls at her temples almost grey. One lens of her spectacles was milked over with ash except for the thumbprint arc where she had tried to clear it. She had kept, absurdly, a short library pencil hooked behind her right ear, as if there might still be a table somewhere she meant to work at. Her cardigan was gone. Her shirt-sleeves were rolled unevenly, one to the elbow and one halfway, and her left cuff was black with soot where she had wiped blood or smoke from her mouth. She shut the stone hatch behind her by feel, not because stealth mattered down here now, but because she had learned the route properly and habit survived panic.

For a second she only stared at the chamber. The chalk grid. The Leaves. Owen inside the ring with his hand fixed to the anchor like something nailed there.

"You found it in the dark," he said, because his mind offered route before motive.

"I found it because you brought me here in January and then spent the rest of the year acting as if memory was your private invention." Her voice came rougher than usual, smoke-scraped, but still careful on the edges. She took one step forward, saw the live line cut across the floor, and stopped. "Don't be stupid. I can see it's active."

Another impact sounded somewhere far above. Dust struck the pages. The anchor flared under Owen's palm and a cold pain went up his arm to the base of his neck. He swallowed against it.

"You shouldn't be here," he said. "The route isn't stable when the castle's taking this much load."

"I know. I came anyway." She was breathing too fast and trying to hide it by speaking on the out-breath. "I couldn't find you in the hall and Tallow wouldn't answer a direct question, which, frankly, was answer enough."

At Tallow's name a small practical corner of Owen's mind pictured the elf in the kitchen with a ladle still in his hand, pretending not to look at the service door while smoke came down the chimney. It made him feel, absurdly and briefly, ashamed of the clean circle of work around him. He set the shame aside because the seam moved when he did.

"How bad is it?" he asked, and heard how narrow the question was. Not who. Not where. Only load, pressure, scale.

Prue heard it too. Something in her face altered, not surprise exactly. Confirmation.

"Bad," she said. "The front stairs are gone in one section. The courtyard keeps changing hands. There are children down there, Owen. There are people trying to move the injured on dining benches because nobody can keep the corridors clear long enough for anything clever. Neville's still standing. I saw him ten minutes ago, unless time is doing something odd on me. The rest..." She shook her head once, impatient with the uselessness of summary. "It's still a battle. That's the point. It's not over."

He nodded. The ward seam answered that news before he did. A pulse ran through the chamber wall, outward and back again, as if the school had taken a breath through cracked ribs. The notation nearest his knee rearranged its own soot shadow and showed him the dormant local branch once more: a buried adjunct nested under the house-protection tables, old as the foundations, waiting for authority and enough catastrophic stress to wake.

Prue followed his glance.

Her eyes dropped to the line he had been refusing not to see. Not refusing, he corrected inwardly. Accounting for. The clause sat half above and half within the stone, its house-scope markers plain now that the battle had made them answer: not the small bounded amendments he had managed in corridors or punishment rooms, but castle-wide condition, hostile instrument, denial of ignition. Even exhausted, even half-blind with chalk grit, he could feel how much authority it would take and how quickly it would bite.

Prue stepped sideways for a clearer view, then went very still. When she spoke, her voice had lost the smoke-scraped raggedness and gone thin with exactness.

"That isn't just a shield."

"No."

"It suspends wandwork."

"Hostile wandwork," he said automatically. "Locally. Within the house. There's a duration problem and the boundary language is old."

She looked at him. "Owen."

"That matters."

"It matters to the wording. It doesn't matter to the moral sentence."

A laugh tried to happen in his throat and came out as a cough. Fresh blood salted the back of his mouth. He turned his head and spat red into an old drainage groove cut beside the ring. It ran pink for a moment and vanished under the anchor.

"The seam is open," he said when he could talk again. "Not fully, not like the lens showed under the lake, but enough. This is what the battle is doing. If I spend the hold on the house clause now and lose the edge, the whole point of coming down here goes with it."

Prue took the pencil from behind her ear and, without seeming to know she was doing it, rolled it between thumb and forefinger like a worry bead. "You said if the battle peaked, the node would answer. You didn't say it would answer with a way to stop the battle."

"I didn't know it would."

"But now you do."

The chamber shuddered. Not the ordinary concussion of curses traded overhead, but something heavier and nearer: a stone strike that made dust pour in sheets from the left wall and sent a hairline crack through one of Owen's outer chalk braces. He swore and reached with his free hand to redraw the broken segment before the branch-language bled into the repair. The floor bit cold through his trousers. By the time he closed the gap his fingers were shaking hard enough to blur the last mark.

Prue flinched at the sound but held her ground. "Can I touch anything?"

"No."

"Can I hold the left margin open?"

"No."

"You didn't even look."

"I don't need to. The surface is live and keyed through the scar. If you put your hand in the ring it'll answer you as noise."

"Then tell me what you need."

There was something almost unbearable in that question because it was practical, because it came from the part of the year when practical had still meant shared work and not judgment. He kept his eyes on the anchor and said, "Less interference."

Prue closed her eyes once. When she opened them again she had gone quieter, which with her was worse than shouting.

"Upstairs," she said, "people are dying in intervals while you sort terminology."

He nearly said that dying in intervals was still dying. He nearly said that if the breach announced itself beyond the planetary mask those intervals would become meaningless. He nearly said several accurate things, and accuracy stood there between them with its hands folded like a school prefect.

Instead he said, "I know what it's doing."

"No, you know what the outer seam is doing." She crouched carefully outside the ring, keeping her skirt and sleeves clear of the marks. Her knees cracked on the stone and she winced, annoyed at the body for being audible. "I'm talking about the clause in front of you. The one that would stop hostile wandwork in the castle. Not perfectly, perhaps, not forever, I heard you, but long enough. Long enough for the battle to break the other way. Long enough for children to stop being set on fire in corridors while you decide which apocalypse counts."

The last word had too much shape in it. She knew that and pulled back half an inch from her own rhetoric. "Sorry. That's not... It is, actually. It's what I mean."

Owen pressed harder into the anchor until the heat climbed his arm. Pain made the notation cleaner. The repaired seam narrowed by a fraction. He could not tell whether the fraction came from his work or from some turn upstairs. He hated that uncertainty. He hated, more quietly, that her language landed because it was imprecise in the human direction and therefore truer than his.

"If I switch branches now," he said, "I may not get this hold back. The clause isn't free. Nothing here is free. The whole reason it woke is that the school's under catastrophic load and the stone is answering too much at once. If I divert that answer into the local condition, then resume the seam, there is no reason to assume the seam will still take. It might. I don't know. I really don't know. But if it doesn't, we've spent the only window on the wrong question."

Prue listened without interrupting, which was how he knew she had already decided what he was saying.

"The wrong question," she repeated.

He tried again, smaller. "The smaller one."

She gave a short, unbelieving sound. "They're not smaller to the people upstairs."

The chamber settled into a brief lull. No impact. No shout through stone. Just water ticking somewhere in the wall and Owen's own breath sawing in and out between his teeth. In that quiet the dormant clause glowed faintly inside the anchor, patient and available. It did not press him. The First Art never pressured in the human sense. It merely made options legible and then waited to see which damage you preferred.

Prue saw it too. Her gaze kept returning to that lit branch the way a tongue returns to a split tooth.

"You could do it and then come back," she said, but even as she spoke she was reading the brace structure around the seam and knew what the answer would be. "No. You'd have to unhook the hold. You'd lose the continuity."

"Yes."

"How long to finish this part?"

He almost said minutes. That would have been a lie offered in good style. He looked at the moving edge, at the place where the world kept failing to keep its old promise.

"I don't know," he said. "Less than from the start. More than anyone upstairs would call acceptable."

She sat back on her heels. Her spectacles slid down with sweat and dust. She pushed them up with the heel of one hand and left a fresh ash streak across her cheek. When she spoke again there was less urgency in the volume and more in the repetition, the way someone checked a latch already tested three times.

"So you can stop it."

He kept tracing the seam with his thumb on the stone. "Prue-"

"So you can stop it."

"I can invoke a local condition with unknown edge failure and bounded duration."

"That means yes."

"Yes," he said, because narrowing further would only make him sound sly, and perhaps he was. "Yes."

Above them something screamed. Not a person exactly; stone, timber, magical structure. The sound went through the chamber in a low animal bellow and set the water in the drainage cuts trembling. Prue's head jerked toward the ceiling as if she could see through it by force. Owen felt the battle answer in the anchor: hostile load cresting, the whole castle bright as a beacon to things no one upstairs even knew enough to fear.

"Then do it," she said.

He did not.

There was no gesture to mark the refusal. No heroic stillness, no dark revelation arriving in him whole. He kept his hand where it was. He followed the seam through another small correction. He redrew a failing bracket. He breathed through the hot nail driven up the middle of his arm and let the house clause remain dormant and available and unused.

Prue made a noise then that he had not heard from her before in all the months of libraries and cold rooms and quiet work. Not a sob. Not anger in any tidy form. It was the sound a person made when thought and revulsion met without enough time to arrange themselves.

"You mean it," she said.

He did not look at her.

"You mean to let them finish it without you."

"I mean to keep the breach from brightening past recall."

"That isn't an answer to what I said."

"It's the answer I've got."

She laughed once, breathlessly. "Course it is."

Another impact. Nearer. One of the old lamp-hooks broke loose from the chamber wall and shattered on the floor outside the ring. Prue flinched back, then immediately looked ashamed of the flinch, as if being startled in a battle were a personal indulgence. She picked up one splintered bit of iron, turned it in her fingers, and set it carefully aside so it would not roll into the marks. Even now she would not make his work sloppier than it already was. That small obedience hurt more than if she had kicked the whole circle to pieces.

"You brought me in here," she said after a moment. "That night with the overlays and the anchor tables. You remember."

"Yes."

"You showed me the service route and the keyed braces and the way the stone answered the old notation because you needed another mind, and because for one hour you forgot to be alone on purpose." Her voice shook once and steadied. "Don't now pretend I'm too ignorant to understand what this is."

"I haven't."

"Then say it plainly." She leaned forward, not into the ring but toward him, demanding the line with her whole body. "Not the seam. Not the wider field. Say the other part plainly. Say what you're choosing."

His mouth dried. The chamber tasted of wet stone and old pennies. A fresh thread of blood had worked loose from somewhere high in his nose and was making its slow way down the back of his throat.

He could have lied. Not well, perhaps, not to Prue, but enough to blur the edge. He could have spoken about probabilities, about branch instability, about catastrophic visibility, about the unnamed thing leaning toward any bright magical wound in the planetary mask. All of it would have been, in pieces, true.

Instead, because the clause glowed there between them and because she had earned the shape of it, he said, "I'm choosing not to stop the battle."

Prue stared at him.

The words, once spoken, did not improve. They sat in the chamber like spoilage. Outside the ring, water kept ticking in the wall.

"Because you think this matters more," she said.

"Because it does."

She shut her eyes. For a moment he thought she might stand and leave, or swear at him, or say his name in the tone Martha had used when friendship broke and stayed broken. But Prue was not Martha and her hurt turned colder.

"When we were mapping the four anchors," she said, eyes still closed, "I kept telling myself there was a difference between abstract cruelty and actual one. That perhaps if the choice became real you would... I don't know. Resize. Remember bodies. Be forced into scale."

He said nothing.

Her eyes opened. "You haven't failed to save them because you can't. That part matters, and I need you to hear me say it. You can. You're not lacking the thing. You're withholding it."

The seam twitched under his hand. He had let his attention soften too far toward language. He bent back over the anchor, corrected the drift, and hated himself a little for the relief of having a technical error to answer.

"I heard you," he said.

"No, you filed me. There's a difference."

There was, and because there was, he had no defense that did not prove her point.

The battle above changed.

He knew it before the next sound reached them. The anchor lost a whole band of pressure at once, as if a hand the size of the castle had been clamped around his throat for hours and now eased two fingers away. The hostile load did not vanish, but it ceased behaving like escalation and started behaving like aftermath: scattered bursts, collapsing vectors, magic no longer converging on victory but breaking into pockets and routs.

A second later the stone carried down a muffled concussion so deep it felt not heard but remembered. Dust billowed from the roof. Somewhere very far off, through floor and wall and earth, rose a noise that might have been shouting.

Prue went white.

Owen kept his hand on the anchor.

The dormant house clause dimmed. Not disappeared. Only dimmed, the moment passing out from under it as the battle tipped beyond the exact kind of undecided catastrophe that had woken it. He felt the availability narrowing and with it, stupidly, a wash of relief so filthy he wanted to retch. The choice had not been taken from him. It had simply been carried past its sharpest edge by other people's work.

"It's turning," he said before he could stop himself.

Prue stared at him with such naked disgust that the words fell dead in the air.

"Yes," she said. "Up there. Without you."

Something like cheering reached them then, thin through the stone, torn by distance and masonry until it sounded almost like crying. Another voice shouted. Another. Then a scatter of noises too blurred to sort: grief, triumph, orders, disbelief. Human sound after a decision.

Owen's amendment still needed him. The seam had not sealed because Hogwarts won a battle. The planetary ward did not care about school heroics. He knew this. He kept working because the work remained. Yet the chamber no longer felt separate from upstairs. Prue had broken that insulation by arriving. Every careful stroke of attention now stood beside the image she had forced into plain speech: people dying in intervals while he sorted questions.

His vision fuzzed at the edges. He blinked grit out and steadied the last brace. The stone under his palm cooled by degrees, not kindness but compliance. The seam answered lower. The raw shining at its edge dulled from knife-metal to banked ember. He followed it through the taper until the amendment could stand without constant pressure and then, only then, lifted his hand.

The absence hit him hard. His arm went numb to the shoulder. He folded forward at the waist and caught himself on blood-slick fingers before his face struck the stone. For a few seconds the chamber moved in long slow swings. He tasted chalk.

Prue did not cross into the ring. She knew better. She also, he thought, knew that if she touched him now he might mistake it for pardon.

When he could sit back, the anchor was only stone again. Old, damp, ribbed with founder cuts and the residue of too much answer. The house clause had sunk below visibility. The Leaves lay open to nonsense tables and blank margins. Above them the castle continued making victory and grief in ordinary human proportions.

Prue stood.

One of her knees had stiffened from crouching. She put a hand on it, massaged the joint once with brisk annoyance, and straightened fully. The pencil was still in her fingers. She looked at it as if wondering why, then snapped it in half without seeming to mean to and dropped the pieces beside the broken lamp-hook.

"I'll go back up," she said.

Owen wiped his mouth with the back of his wrist. "Prue-"

She cut across him, not loudly. "Don't explain. If you explain it smaller you'll only make it worse."

He stopped.

She adjusted her spectacles with soot-black fingertips. The ash streak on her cheek had dried into something almost childlike and thereby unbearable. When she spoke again, her voice was level from effort.

"I needed to see whether you were mistaken or merely wrong. That's all settled now."

That landed cleaner than anything else in the room had, and because she was upset enough to hate a clean sentence on herself, she shook her head and ruined it. "No. That sounds smug. It isn't smug. I just mean I know what happened. I know what you didn't do."

He wanted, very briefly, to say that he had done something. That the seam was banked, the brightness lowered, the breach not lost tonight. But even in his own skull the defense arrived in the wrong units.

So he said, "All right."

She gave him a look so tired it nearly counted as pity and then took the lamp from the wall niche with a hand that shook only on the first attempt. At the hatch she paused, not turning back yet.

"Martha will ask where you were," she said.

The name moved through him like grit in a wound. "You don't have to tell her."

Prue looked over her shoulder. "I know I don't have to."

He could not tell from her face whether that meant she would or would not. Perhaps she did not know yet either.

Another muffled shout came through the stone, closer to laughter this time. Dawn was not in the chamber, but some paler quality had entered the cracks around the hatch, a less absolute dark. Smoke threaded down after it.

Prue lifted the latch.

"Come up when you can stand," she said. "Or don't. But if you stay below, don't pretend it's because nobody gave you a human option."

Then she went into the passage, bending automatically at the low turn, lamp cupped against the draught. The light moved along the narrow stone for a moment, caught on the repaired wire at the hinge of her spectacles, and vanished.

Owen stayed where he was.

Above him, somewhere in the enormous body of the school, people were finding each other alive or not alive. They were calling names down corridors. They were setting benches straight, covering faces, laughing too hard because they had not died, vomiting from shock behind tapestries, running messages toward the Great Hall, becoming heroes in the version of the night the rest of the world would someday understand. The castle held all of that in its upper rooms.

Below, the anchor stone cooled under his ruined hand. The seam did not glow. The wider dark, for now, had been taught to keep its distance.

He sat in the chalk wreckage until the last tremors left the floor and the sounds above resolved from battle into aftermath, and what remained in the chamber with him was not uncertainty, not anymore, but the exact shape of the thing Prue had witnessed: help unused, made useless by decision.


# Chapter 18

By the time Owen came back up from the undercroft, dawn had got into the castle the way smoke did, through cracks that had not been there yesterday and under doors that no longer shut square. The kitchen stair was filmed with soot. Somebody had slopped broth or potion or blood over the fourth step from the top and it had dried tacky in the grit. He had one hand on the wall because the stone still moved fractionally under him, not in truth, not enough to throw him, but enough that his body had not yet accepted the battle was over above his head.

His right palm felt boiled inside the old burn. His nails had split at the white crescents. Blood had dried from his nose down the front of his jumper in a rusty fan, and each time he swallowed he could taste the chalk dust he had breathed below. The satchel strap bit into his shoulder. Inside it the Grinlow Leaves lay wrapped in a tea towel gone stiff with damp, and his ash-linen notebook pressed against his ribs like a second, smaller body.

The passage opened near the kitchens, but the smell from the Great Hall drew through the corridor and made a shape in the air before he reached it: smoke, old stone, singed wool, broth, blood, and the sweet wrong stink of spells burnt into masonry. He followed it because there was nowhere else to go that would count as coming back.

The Hall had stopped pretending to be a dining room.

The house tables had been shoved crooked against the walls to clear floor space. One trestle had lost a leg and leaned into Ravenclaw's bench at an angle that looked comic until Owen saw the dead laid in the gap beside it. Blankets, cloaks, table linen, a ripped curtain from somewhere above; anything that could cover a face had been pressed into use and none of it matched. Under the enchanted ceiling dawn showed itself as a dirty pearl, smoke dragging grey ribbons across the high charred blue. The windows on the courtyard side had blown inward. Glass crunched under shoes. The flagstones were gritty with shattered stone and the fine plaster dust that got into the back of the throat and stayed there.

People moved in the half-order that came after panic rather than before it. A pair of seventh-years carried a first-year between them, her feet trailing because none of them had strength left to lift properly. Somebody at the staff table was making lists in a voice too hoarse to rise above the room. Cauldrons of hot water smoked along the wall. A Hufflepuff boy from two years below Owen sat with both hands wrapped round an empty mug, blinking at nothing while a cut in his scalp dripped slowly behind one ear. Near the front, Colin Creevey had been covered with a blanket that stopped short of his shoes. The laces on one trainer had melted together into a black bead.

No one looked up when Owen entered. The room was full of people who had earned being seen.

He stood there too long. Then Martha's voice reached him from halfway down the Hall, brisk with fatigue.

"If you're upright, stop blocking the bloody doorway."

She was kneeling beside a makeshift pallet with her bad leg shoved straight out in front of her so she did not have to trust it bent. Someone had wrapped her left calf from ankle to knee in uneven white strips already gone grey at the edges. Her dark plait had come mostly loose and she had caught the tail of it under the collar of her jumper to keep it off the blood on her shoulder. She was tying off a bandage around a girl's upper arm with the quick domestic efficiency of somebody securing a parcel that had split open in transit.

Owen crossed the Hall because she had told him to do a thing and that was simpler than deciding what to say.

"Sorry," he said.

"Yeah." She jerked her chin toward a bucket by her knee. "That needs filling. Not from the lav by the stairs, that one's got plaster in it. Courtyard pump."

He picked up the bucket. The handle was sticky. "Are you all right?"

Martha looked up then, only once. Her face was ash-grey with tiredness under the grime, and there was a crescent bite mark on her lower lip where she had been worrying it raw.

"No," she said. "But the leg still works when I swear at it, so off you go."

He took the bucket to the courtyard pump because she had given him a route and a tool and he could still obey those. Outside, dawn made the broken cloisters look flatter than night had. Part of the outer wall had come down in a heap of pale blocks and splintered beams. The lawn beyond was blackened in streaks. A suit of armour lay in pieces near the archway, one gauntlet still twitching now and then as leftover enchantment ran out of itself. There were bodies out here too, though fewer, and a line of the living sitting against the wall with blankets round their shoulders, all facing the same direction as if waiting for a lesson to begin.

The pump handle was cold enough to hurt. He worked it until rusty water gave way to clear. When he bent to lift the bucket his vision pinched black at the edges. He waited, breathing through his mouth. Somewhere in the grounds a cheer started, broke, and turned into something that might have been crying from a distance. Victory, Owen thought, and the word felt like hearing about a meal from the bottom of a well.

When he brought the water back, Martha had shifted from the pallet to the floor and was rolling torn sheets into narrower strips. She did not thank him. She took the bucket and set it beside her.

"There," she said. "Useful."

He stayed standing because sitting down near her felt like presumption. "I didn't know if you were—"

"Alive?" She wet a strip of linen and wrung it hard. "Yeah. Same as you."

"Martha."

"Don't." Her hands kept moving. "Not while I'm doing this."

He looked at the girl on the pallet. Fourth year, maybe. Freckles across the bridge of her nose under the soot. Martha had tucked the blanket round her feet with absurd care, making sure the heels were covered. That detail hurt more than anger would have.

"I was below," he said, and heard at once how useless it was.

Martha gave one flat laugh with no humour in it. "I know you were somewhere. Everybody was somewhere."

"It mattered."

"Maybe." She yanked the knot tight with her teeth and spat a thread. "Maybe it mattered a lot. I haven't got the spare parts in me right now to care at your scale."

He could have told her about the crack brightening under battle magic, about the clause in the anchor he had not used, about the way the stone had nearly gone liquid under his hands. None of it would have answered the room they were in. None of it would have moved the blanket higher over Colin's shoes or put sleep back under Martha's skin.

"You look awful," she said after a moment, still not looking at him. "There's tea near the staff table if you're going to fall over. Don't do it on my bandages."

The kindness in it was so small and so stubborn that it landed like a refusal.

"Right," he said. "Yes."

She set another rolled strip in a neat pile. "And Owen."

He waited.

This time she did look at him. Her eyes were bloodshot, ordinary brown made strange by the night's length.

"Don't come asking me for the right shape of it," she said. "I haven't got one for you. I've got this and that girl and my leg and whoever they bring in next. That's what I've got."

"I wasn't going to."

"Good."

Somebody behind him called for clean water. Martha had already turned away, one hand braced on the floorboards as she hauled herself toward the next body. The conversation had not ended cleanly because nothing in the Hall did. It just stopped needing him.

He moved aside. Two girls from Gryffindor came through carrying a transfigured door with a dead boy on it. One of them had tucked a sprig of greenhouse rosemary behind her ear and kept absently touching it with the back of her wrist, as if checking it was still there. Neither spared Owen a glance.

Prue found him near the wreck of the Hufflepuff table, where he had stood too long staring at a scorch mark shaped like a thrown map.

She had tied her hair back with a strip torn from blue graph paper. The knot had softened in the damp and one corner of the paper kept lifting against her temple. Her spectacles were smeared white at the edges, and the left lens had a crack through the bottom corner that made the flagstones buckle if he looked directly at it. She was holding a stack of folded blankets against her chest with the rigid care of someone afraid that if she loosened her arms at all, they would start shaking.

"You've come up, then," she said.

"Apparently."

She looked him over from the dried blood at his mouth to the satchel on his shoulder. "You should sit down."

"I'm fine."

"You're not. I didn't say collapse dramatically. I said sit."

He did not sit. The Hall was too public for it. "Are you hurt?"

"A bit." She shifted the blankets higher. "Nothing worth inventorying."

The crack in her lens flashed as she turned her head. Beyond her, at the far end of the Hall, a knot of people closed and opened round somebody important enough to change the air of the room merely by being alive. Owen did not need to see who it was. The castle had already tilted toward its legible heroes and its legible dead.

Prue followed his glance, then looked back at him. "I haven't said anything."

He answered the smallest question inside that sentence because it was the one he could bear. "You don't need to."

"No." She pressed her thumb into the blanket stack until the top one creased. "That isn't what I said."

He shut his mouth.

"I haven't said anything," she repeated, quieter now, "because if I do it now it'll go stupid at once. It'll turn into one sentence. Two, if we're lucky. You had a way to stop it and you didn't. People will hear that and fill in the rest with whatever makes them least sick."

"It's true enough."

"No." Her voice sharpened. "It isn't enough."

He could feel the anchor still in his bones, the patched line below the castle giving a faint sick tug every few breaths. He wanted, absurdly, to show her the exact condition as if the stone itself could speak for him. But she had seen enough. That was the point.

"It wouldn't have held the way they would've meant," he said. "The local clause would've collapsed hostile wandwork in the castle, yes, but only for the fight in front of us, and the breach condition would've kept widening because all the larger load was still there and I was already losing the edge on the seam and if I'd tried to split attention at that stage—"

"I know," she said, cutting across him before he could build a machine out of explanation. "I know that part. I was there."

He stopped.

For a moment they listened to the room together. The scrape of benches. A kettle lid rattling. Somebody saying a name twice because the first time had not brought an answer.

Prue let out a breath through her nose. "You keep doing this thing where you tell the truth in units too small to be argued with."

"Do I?"

"Yes." She adjusted her spectacles with the side of one wrist because her hands were full. "You say the clause wouldn't have solved the larger problem. Fine. It wouldn't have. You say the breach is still there. Fine. I believe you. You also left us upstairs with the smaller problem while it was still killing people one body at a time. That part doesn't shrink because the other part is planetary."

The word sounded ridiculous in the ruined Hall. Planetary. As if the world could be made of anything grander than broken benches and damp blankets and Martha dragging herself on a bad leg from one body to the next.

"I know," he said.

"Do you?"

"Yes."

Prue studied him. He knew what she saw: the blood, the ash, the spent look of somebody who had not been idle. Proof had never been the issue with her. Only scale.

"What happens now?" she asked.

He could have lied, or made it smaller, or offered a technical answer so narrow it sounded almost manageable. He was too tired.

"The patch holds," he said. "For now. The crack isn't gone. It's closed enough that nothing notices immediately, I think. Not from outside. But the node is damaged and it'll need tending. Re-inscribing. Watching. If the castle takes another battering like tonight before I settle it properly..."

"It opens again."

"Yes."

"How long have we got?"

"I don't know." He hated how honest that sounded, how much like begging. "Longer than last night. Short of safe. That's the best I can do."

She nodded once. Not agreement. Classification.

"All right," she said.

He waited for more and hated himself for it.

What came was softer, and worse for that. "I can't go down there with you again."

His fingers tightened on the satchel strap. "I didn't ask."

"No. You don't ask for the expensive bits. You just keep moving as if other people will sort themselves around the work." Her mouth twitched, not with humour. "I'm not helping you disappear into it. If you go back, call it what it is."

He said nothing.

Prue looked past him toward the smashed windows where the dawn had brightened from pearl to a thin exhausted silver. "They're going to remember this as an ending," she said. "Maybe they have to. I can't even say they're wrong. But don't stand here wanting it from me."

"I'm not."

She gave him a tired, incredulous look that would have been almost ordinary in the library and was not ordinary here at all. "Right."

Someone called her name from the far end of the Hall. She flinched before she answered, as if the sound had reached a bruise.

"I'm coming," she called back, and then to Owen, "Wash your face. You look frightening."

That, too, was not absolution. It was housekeeping from a person who still preferred precision to spectacle. She shifted the blankets in her arms and went, pausing only once to hitch the torn graph-paper ribbon tighter at the back of her head.

Owen stayed where he was until the room began to reorganize around breakfast for the living and names for the dead. The cauldrons along the wall changed from hot water to tea. A tray of bread appeared from the kitchens and was descended on without ceremony. People found one another or failed to. House loyalties and battle loyalties and family names began to pull the Hall into comprehensible shapes again. None of them had room for him. He could have taken a mug, sat on a bench, waited for someone to tell him what happened in the battle he had not seen finish. The thought made his skin crawl.

He turned back toward the kitchen corridor before anyone asked him where he had been.

***

The kitchens looked less ruined than the Hall because labour had already begun to erase the ruin.

Bread had been sliced directly on the scrubbed tables, leaving long pale furrows in the wood where the knife had bitten too deep in a hurry. A copper pan hung off one hook by its handle because the rim had buckled from heat. The big black range was going hard enough to sweat the stones. Steam and soot pasted themselves to the ceiling in greasy layers. Three house-elves slept sitting up against the flour bins with their chins on their chests, hands still powdered white to the wrists. Every flat surface held something halfway back to usefulness: cracked mugs sorted by size, damp towels, onions, bowls of broth skinning over while somebody shouted for more salt.

Tallow stood at the scullery end with a dishcloth over one shoulder and a row of chipped serving platters spread before him like a set of difficult medical cases. He was fitting broken edges together to see which ones could still be trusted with weight. One of his singed ears had been wrapped in a narrow strip of linen and pinned up with a bent fork tine. He did not look up until Owen was close enough to drip soot on the clean boards.

"Shoes off the flagged bit if you're bleeding," he said. "That side's for bread."

Owen stepped where indicated without argument. His legs were starting to shake in earnest now that he was under a lower ceiling.

"Sorry."

"Mm." Tallow set two platter halves together, decided against them, and laid one in the discard basket. "Sorry is what children say when the milk's gone over. This is a longer job than that."

The satchel seemed suddenly obvious on Owen's shoulder. Tallow's eyes flicked to it and away.

"House is still standing," the elf said. "Not gracefully. But then neither are half the students, and here we all are."

"You felt it."

"Tallow felt every saucepan in the west kitchen rattle itself stupid and one pudding collapse clean in the middle, yes. The stone settled with a complaint from one end to the other." He reached for a fresh platter. "Young sir has got the look of a wall that has taken weight and not decided whether it resents it yet."

Owen lowered the satchel to the table. "I need to put these away."

That made Tallow's mouth flatten. "Away where?"

"Not the shelf-chase. Too exposed now. The north stacks are half-broken and everyone will be rummaging for weeks."

"So."

"At the node." He touched the tea towel bundle through the satchel flap. "It answers there. More than anywhere else."

Tallow stared at the bag for a long second, then picked up the dishcloth and wiped his hands with greater care than the dirt required.

"Of course it does," he said. "Nasty little thing has found its way home."

He hopped down from the bench and crooked two fingers. "Come along, then. Before somebody decides they need this table for pastry and we all lose what little reason remains."

The route back to the undercroft felt shorter in daylight, though there was no daylight in it. Owen followed Tallow through the hot wet dark behind the ovens, down the passage where the stone always smelled faintly of onions and cold iron, through the warped service door that opened only if lifted a quarter inch before the latch turned. The castle changed underfoot as they descended. Kitchen vibration gave way to the dull mineral hush of the older levels. Sound lost its edges. Owen's own breathing came back to him off the walls like somebody else's.

By the time they reached the anchor chamber, his headache had narrowed to a hard point behind one eye.

Nothing down here cared that the war had been won upstairs. The chamber was still damp, still half-carved and half-grown out of the bedrock, with water beading in the same seams and running down the same grooves it had yesterday and all the yesterdays before that. But the night's work had marked it. Chalk and ash were ground into the floor in overlapping arcs around the anchor stone. Dark handprints smeared one flank where he had caught himself from falling. Blood had dried in the fine inscription channels and turned almost black. The repaired seam through the stone's face showed as a pale tight line like a healed cut that would split again if smiled too hard.

Owen set the satchel down with both hands. The motion sent a flare through his palm sharp enough that he had to close his eyes for a moment.

Tallow, without comment, pushed a three-legged stool toward him with one foot. Owen sat because refusing would have been performance.

"You made a filth of it," Tallow said, surveying the floor.

"Yes."

"Well. A battle isn't a tea tray." He moved nearer the anchor and winced, not theatrically, simply because the old work hurt him here. "Is it holding?"

"Yes."

"That wasn't a courtesy question."

"Yes," Owen said again, more exactly. "It's holding under present load. The outer brightness has dropped. The seam isn't travelling."

"Mm."

Tallow looked at him sideways. "And if the castle has another night like that one by supper."

"Then we're in trouble."

"There we are."

Owen pulled the tea towel bundle from the satchel and laid it on the stool across his knees. It was damp clear through. The cloth had taken an outline from the pages inside, a flattened uneven rectangle with corners that no longer met. His notebook he set aside on the floor, safely out of the drip line from the ceiling. When he unwrapped the manuscript, the chamber seemed to draw a fraction tighter around them.

The Grinlow Leaves had never looked like one proper book. They were a packet of unequal sheets, some thick as card, some thin enough to show the shadow of writing through from the other side, all of them darkened at the edges from age and lamp smoke and his handling. The scripts on the upper pages shifted when he glanced across them, not changing exactly, but refusing to stay in the category of ordinary ink. Near the anchor they were more restless. Fine mineral brightness began to show in the fibres, as if the stone beneath the chamber floor had started reading back.

Tallow muttered something under his breath about ugly paperwork and stepped away from the direct line of it.

Owen had thought on the stairs that he would choose where to hide it. That he would examine the seams, test the shelves cut into the founder stone, decide on a placement and a covering layer the way a sensible person hid a dangerous object. The undercroft removed that illusion from him at once.

When he lifted the top leaf, the anchor answered. Not with light. With recognition.

The pale repaired line on the stone darkened. A hairline split opened beside it, no wider than the edge of a fingernail. Cold damp air came out of it, carrying the lead-and-stone smell of the old shelf-chase. The uppermost page bowed downward toward the seam. Owen held on instinctively, and the sheet resisted him with the terrible softness of soaked paper. Under his thumb the edge roughened. Grey mineral thread showed through the tear before he let go.

"Leave it," Tallow snapped, sharper than he had spoken all morning. "Do not pull against the grain when the house is taking weight."

Owen released the page.

One by one the Leaves bent and settled. They did not fall. They were not dragged. They simply lost the argument that they were separate objects. The lower edges touched the seam and blurred into it, script and fibre sinking together until the pages lay half-visible under a skin of new stone no thicker than frost on glass. Some of the older tables remained readable in fragments. Others went at once, their lines travelling sideways into the anchor where no human eye could follow. The tea towel wrapper slumped empty in Owen's lap.

He sat very still.

There was no question of carrying the manuscript back upstairs now. He had not rehidden it in a niche or box. The undercroft had taken it into the node itself and sealed the question under mineral glaze. From now on, if the Grinlow Leaves could be consulted at all, they could be consulted only here, on the stone's terms, with the seam in front of him and the damage under his hands.

Tallow breathed out slowly through his nose. "There. That's told us."

"I thought there might be a choice."

"There was. Last year, perhaps. Before young sir put his whole sticky life into it."

Owen gave a laugh that scraped his throat. It hurt too much to count as humour.

Through the thin stone skin, one page remained more legible than the others: not the opening, not the broad tables of departure, but a cramped later hand full of maintenance intervals, threshold symbols, and corrections written into the margins at different dates. Not lore. Upkeep. Somebody else's ugly year preserved beside his.

"Of course," he said.

"Yes," Tallow said. "Of course. Houses are work."

The elf moved to a shelf cut into the wall and returned with a bucket, a stiff brush worn to a fan at the end, a packet of chalk, and a squat jar of something that smelled of lime and wet ash. He set them down one by one with the solemnity of laying out cutlery.

"Now," he said, "before the blood sets deeper in the scoring."

Owen looked from the tools to the anchor and back. "Now."

"Did young sir think mending was the end of it."

"No. I just—"

"Just hoped the castle would clean up after you because castles are large and boys are tired." Tallow's expression did not soften, but neither was it cruel. It was workman's irritation at a fantasy of finished labour. "Listen properly. The seam will need watching at first light and last light. The channels will need keeping clear. If the stone sweats black, you wake me. If the line travels, you wake me. If a duel, a feast, a Ministry inspection, or some fool with a commemorative plaque starts hammering on this bit of floor from above, you notice. That is the arrangement now."

Owen put his good hand round the brush handle. The wood was swollen from age and damp. Somebody, long ago, had carved a shallow thumb-rest into it.

"All right," he said.

Tallow tilted his bandaged ear as if listening to whether the chamber believed him. "You'll miss breakfast."

Owen looked up toward the layers of stone and hall and smoke above them, where the living would be drinking tea and counting losses and beginning, already, to turn horror into story.

"Yes," he said.

"You'll miss the speeches as well, when they come."

"Fine."

"Mm." Tallow handed him the jar of lime wash. "Tallow thought so."

The first stroke of the brush through the inscription channel loosened blackened blood and chalk into a paste the colour of dishwater after roasting tins. Owen had to work the bristles into the groove carefully, following the old marks without widening them. It was not noble. It was not mysterious. It was scrubbing filth out of a damaged line before the residue dried hard enough to distort the next reading.

After a minute the chamber settled around the rhythm of it. Dip. Scrub. Wipe the stone with the rag. Check the seam. Add fresh chalk where the line had thinned. Tallow moved beside him, sorting tools, muttering over a crack in the side shelf, stopping now and then with a small pained shake of his head when the old work pressed too close.

Owen's knees began to ache on the damp floor. His face tightened with each pass of the brush when the movement pulled at the burn in his palm. He kept going. Under the thin skin of stone the trapped Leaves waited, no longer his in any ordinary sense. His notebook lay open by the bucket, ready for intervals, seep, brightness, load. Above, faint through yards of rock, the castle started ringing its bells for the dead.

He marked the first time in the notebook. Then he bent back to the seam and kept working.
